# 庄子

In the first chapter of the first scroll of *Zhuangzi* (*The Scripture of Southern Florescence*), "Free and Easy Wandering":

In the Northern Darkness there is a fish, and its name is Kun. The size of the Kun—no one knows how many thousand *li* it spans. It transforms into a bird, and its name is Peng. The back of the Peng—no one knows how many thousand *li* it spans. When it rouses itself and takes flight, its wings are like clouds draped across the sky. This bird, when the seas surge, will journey to the Southern Darkness. The Southern Darkness is the Celestial Pond.

*Qi Xie* is a record of marvels. Its words say: "When the Peng journeys to the Southern Darkness, the waters churn for three thousand *li*. It mounts the whirlwind and ascends ninety thousand *li*, borne on the winds of a six-month exhalation." The wild galloping of vapors, the drifting of dust motes—these are living things blown about by each other’s breath.

Is the deep blue of the sky its true color? Or is it because it stretches so far that nothing marks its end? When the Peng looks down, it sees only the same.

Moreover, if water does not accumulate to sufficient depth, it lacks the strength to bear a great vessel. Pour a cup of water into a hollow in the floor, and a mustard seed becomes a boat; set a cup there, and it sticks fast—too shallow the water, too great the vessel. If wind does not accumulate to sufficient depth, it lacks the strength to bear great wings. Thus, at ninety thousand *li*, the wind lies beneath it, and only then can it ride the gale. With the azure sky at its back and nothing to obstruct its path, only then does it set its course for the south.

The cicada and the fledgling dove laugh at it, saying: "We burst forth and fly, darting to the elm or sandalwood, and when we cannot reach, we simply alight upon the ground—what need have we to ascend ninety thousand *li* and journey south?"

One who travels to the near wilds brings three meals and returns with belly still full. One who travels a hundred *li* must pound grain for the night’s provisions.

One who travels a thousand *li* must gather provisions for three months. What do these two creatures know? Lesser knowledge does not reach greater knowledge; short years do not reach long years. How do we know this is so? The morning mushroom knows not dawn nor dusk; the cicada knows not spring nor autumn—these are short years. South of Chu lies the *Mingling* tree, for which five hundred years make a spring, five hundred years an autumn. In high antiquity there was the great *Chun* tree, for which eight thousand years make a spring, eight thousand years an autumn—these are long years. Yet Pengzu alone is famed for his longevity; for common folk to compare themselves to him—is this not lamentable?

This is what Tang asked Ji about. In the dark northern sea of the barren lands, there is a heavenly pool. There is a fish there, several thousand *li* in breadth, whose length none knows—its name is Kun. There is a bird there, its name Peng, its back like Mount Tai, its wings like clouds hanging from the sky. It wheels upon the whirlwind, rising ninety thousand *li*, piercing the clouds, bearing the azure sky on its back, then sets its course south, bound for the southern darkness.

The marsh pipit laughs at it, saying: "Where does it think it’s going? I leap and soar, rising no more than a few fathoms before descending, wheeling among the mugwort and wormwood—this is the perfection of flight! Where does it think it’s going?" This is the distinction between small and great.

Thus, those whose knowledge suffices for one office, whose conduct is matched in one village, whose virtue pleases one lord, or whose merit is proven in one state—such men regard themselves as the marsh pipit does. Yet Song Rongzi would still laugh at them. When the whole world praises him, he is not spurred on; when the whole world blames him, he is not disheartened. He has settled the division between inner and outer, discerned the boundary between honor and disgrace—this is all. In his dealings with the world, he is not eager. Even so, he has yet to plant his roots.

Liezi rides the wind and travels, lightly and well, returning after fifteen days. In his pursuit of fortune, he is not eager. Though he avoids walking, he still depends on something. But if one were to ride the constancy of Heaven and Earth, harness the transformations of the six qi, and wander through the boundless—what would he depend on? Thus it is said: the Perfect Man has no self, the Spirit Man has no merit, the Sage has no name.

Yao offered the world to Xu You, saying: "When the sun and moon have risen, yet the torch still burns—is it not difficult for its light to be seen? When the rain has fallen, yet one still waters the fields—is this not labor in vain? You stand, and the world is ordered; yet I still occupy the throne—I see myself as lacking. Take the world."

Xu You replied: "You govern the world, and the world is already well-governed. Yet I would still replace you—would I do so for a name? A name is but the guest of reality; would I be a guest?"

The wren nests in the deep forest, yet needs no more than a single branch; the mole drinks from the river, yet takes no more than fills its belly. Go home, my lord—I have no use for the world!

Though the cook neglects his kitchen, the invocator at the altar would not leap over the wine vessels and sacrificial stands to take his place.

Jian Wu asked Lian Shu: "I heard words from Jie Yu that were vast but without measure, sweeping onward without return. I was startled and terrified by them—they were as boundless as the Milky Way, wildly divergent, far from human sensibilities."

Lian Shu said: "What did he say?"

Jian Wu replied: "On distant Mount Gushe, a spirit-man dwells. His skin is like ice and snow, his grace like a maiden’s. He eats no five grains, but drinks the wind and sips the dew. He rides the clouds and qi, drives dragons, and wanders beyond the four seas. His spirit is so concentrated that he keeps things free from blight and ensures the harvest ripens. Yet I deem this madness and do not believe it."

Lian Shu said: "Indeed. The blind cannot partake in the sight of patterns, nor the deaf in the sound of bells and drums. Is it only the body that can be deaf and blind? The understanding, too, may suffer the same."

Such words of his are but the prattling of a child. That man, that power of his—it would sweep across the ten thousand things, uniting them as one, while the world yearns for order. Who would wear themselves out taking the world as their task!

That man—nothing harms him. Though the great floods rise to the heavens, he does not drown; though great drought melts metal and stone, scorches earth and mountains, he does not burn. From his dust and chaff, one could mold a Yao or Shun—who would trouble themselves with mere things!

The men of Song carried ceremonial caps to Yue, yet the people of Yue cut their hair short and tattoo their bodies, having no use for them. Yao governed the people of the world and brought order to the lands within the seas. Yet when he went to see the Four Masters on distant Mount Gushe, north of the Fen River, he was so profoundly moved that he forgot his rule over the world.

Hui Zi said to Zhuangzi: "The King of Wei gave me seeds of a great gourd. I planted them, and they grew to bear fruit of five *shi*. When I used it to hold water, its strength was not enough to support itself. When I split it to make a ladle, its size was so vast it could hold nothing. It was not that it was not resoundingly large—I deemed it useless and smashed it."

Zhuangzi said: "You are indeed clumsy in putting great things to use. There was a man of Song skilled in making an ointment that prevented chapped hands. For generations, his family had made their living by washing silk floss. A traveler heard of this and offered to buy the formula for a hundred gold pieces. The family gathered and deliberated: 'For generations, we have washed silk floss and earned no more than a few gold pieces. Now, in a single morning, we can sell this skill for a hundred gold pieces—let us give it to him.'

The traveler obtained it and used it to persuade the King of Wu. When Yue raised a rebellion, the King of Wu sent him to lead the troops. In the winter, they fought a naval battle with Yue, and he utterly defeated the Yue forces, winning a fiefdom as his reward. The ability to prevent chapped hands was the same, yet one man gained a fiefdom, while another could not escape washing silk floss—such is the difference in how it was used.

Now you have a gourd of five *shi*—why not consider making it into a great float and drifting upon the rivers and lakes, instead of fretting that it is too large to hold anything? Truly, your mind is still as tangled as a clump of weeds!"

Hui Shi said to Zhuangzi: "I have a great tree, which people call the *chu*. Its massive trunk is gnarled and does not meet the carpenter’s line, its slender branches are twisted and do not conform to compass or square. Planted by the roadside, no craftsman even glances at it. Now, your words are grand but of no use—just as the multitude discards them."

Zhuangzi replied: "Have you alone never seen the wildcat or weasel? Crouching low to lie in wait, it pounces on its prey; leaping east and west, it spares neither high nor low. Yet it falls into the trap or snare and dies. Now consider the yak: its size is like a cloud hanging in the sky. This creature is great indeed, yet it cannot catch a mouse. You have a great tree and fret that it is useless—why not plant it in the land of Nothing-Whatever, the vast and boundless wilds? Wander idly by its side, roam freely beneath its shade. Never felled by axe, unharmed by any thing, without use—where is the hardship in that?"

South Guo Ziqi leaned against his armrest, gazed upward, and sighed, his response so absent it seemed he had lost his counterpart. Yan Cheng Ziyou stood attending before him and asked: "What is this? Can the body truly be made like withered wood, the heart like dead ashes? Yet the one who now leans against the armrest—is he not the same who once leaned before?"

Ziqi replied: "Yan, is it not well that you ask? Today I have lost my self—do you understand? You have heard the human *lai*, but not the earthly *lai*; you have heard the earthly *lai*, but not the heavenly *lai*!"

Ziyou said: "I venture to ask the method."

Ziqi said: "The great clod exhales its *qi*, and this is called wind. It does nothing of itself, but when it arises, the myriad hollows roar in fury. Have you alone not heard its long, sighing sound? The towering forests and jagged cliffs, the great trees with hollows a hundred spans around—some like noses, some like mouths, some like ears, some like beams, some like rings, some like mortars, some like pools, some like pits; they hiss, they shriek, they bellow, they gasp, they cry, they wail, they moan, they gnash. The first sings *yu*, and the rest sing in chorus. A gentle breeze brings a small harmony; a whirlwind brings a great harmony. When the wind subsides, all hollows fall silent. Have you alone not seen them tremble and quiver?"

Ziyou said: "The earthly *lai* is simply the sound of the myriad hollows; the human *lai* is but the sound of bamboo flutes. I venture to ask about the heavenly *lai*."

Ziqi said: "When the wind blows, the myriad sounds differ, yet each ceases of itself. Who is it that stirs them to fury?"

The great knowledge is broad and unhurried, the petty knowledge is narrow and fussy; the grand words blaze forth, the small words chatter on. In sleep, their spirits intertwine; in waking, their forms unfold. They engage in contention, daily battling with their hearts. Some are entangled, some are trapped, some are secretive. Small fears leave them trembling, great fears leave them dazed. Their outbursts are like the release of a crossbow’s trigger—this is what it means to judge right and wrong; their persistence is like a sworn oath—this is what it means to cling to victory. Their decline is like autumn and winter, speaking of their daily decay; their immersion in such ways cannot be reversed. Their stifling is like a sealed casket, speaking of their utter stagnation. The heart near death cannot be revived. Joy and anger, sorrow and delight, anxiety and sighs, fickleness and dread, caprice and indulgence, frivolity and affectation—pleasure arises from emptiness, steam gives rise to mushrooms. Day and night they alternate before us, yet none know where they sprout. Enough, enough! Morning and evening we attain this—is this not the very source from which life arises? Without that, there is no self; without the self, there is nothing to grasp. This is close indeed, yet one knows not what moves it. As if there were a true master, yet no trace of it can be found. It can act and be trusted, yet its form is unseen—it has essence but no form. The hundred bones, nine orifices, and six viscera all exist in completeness, but with whom should I feel kinship? Do you delight in all of them? Or do they have their favorites?

Are all these but servants and handmaids? Are servants and handmaids insufficient to govern one another? Do they take turns being lord and subject? Or is there a true lord that abides? Whether one seeks and attains its truth or fails to do so, it neither adds to nor detracts from its reality. Once receiving this completed form, one does not perish until its end. Engaging with things, they grind and wear against one another—one’s conduct races to its end like a galloping steed, yet none can halt it. Is this not lamentable? A lifetime spent toiling without seeing success, wearily exhausted yet unaware of one’s destination—how can this not be sorrowful? People call it "not dying," but what benefit is there? The body transforms, and the heart follows suit—can this not be called great sorrow? Is human life inherently so deluded? Am I alone in my confusion, or are there others who are not?

Those who follow their settled minds as teachers—who is without a teacher? Why must one know the succession of ages to have a mind that grasps itself? Even fools possess this! When the mind is formed and there is right and wrong, it is like arriving in Yue today yet having reached it yesterday. Thus, one takes what does not exist to be existent. Taking what does not exist to be existent, even the divine Yu would be at a loss—what can I alone do?

Words are not the wind’s breath; speakers have their say. Yet what they say remains unsettled. Is there truly speech? Or has there never been speech? If one deems it distinct from the chirping of fledglings, is there still distinction? Or is there none? Where does the Dao hide that it has truth and falsehood? Where does speech hide that it has right and wrong? Where does the Dao go that it ceases to exist? Where does speech abide that it cannot be affirmed? The Dao is obscured by petty achievements; speech is obscured by florid ornamentation. Hence, there are the Confucians’ and Mohists’ disputes over right and wrong, each affirming what the other denies and denying what the other affirms. To affirm what they deny and deny what they affirm, nothing surpasses clarity.

Nothing exists that is not "that"; nothing exists that is not "this." From "that," one does not see; from self-knowledge, one knows. Thus it is said: "That emerges from this, and this likewise depends on that." This is the theory of simultaneous arising. Yet even as things arise, they die; as they die, they arise. Even as they are affirmed, they are denied; as they are denied, they are affirmed. Affirming this and denying that, denying this and affirming that—therefore, the sage does not proceed thus but illuminates all by the light of Heaven. This, too, is "that," and "that," too, is "this." "That" has its right and wrong, and "this" has its right and wrong. Is there truly a "that" and a "this"? Or is there truly neither "that" nor "this"? Where "that" and "this" find no counterpart, this is called the pivot of the Dao. Once the pivot is grasped at the center of the circle, it responds endlessly. "This" is endless, and "not-this" is likewise endless. Thus, it is said: nothing surpasses clarity.

To use a finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger is not as good as using a non-finger to illustrate that a finger is not a finger. To use a horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse is not as good as using a non-horse to illustrate that a horse is not a horse. Heaven and Earth are one finger; the myriad things are one horse.

What can be affirmed is affirmed; what cannot be affirmed cannot be affirmed. The Dao is realized through action; things are so called by convention. Why are they so? They are so because they are so. Why are they not so? They are not so because they are not so. Things inherently have what they affirm and what they accept. Nothing is not so; nothing is unacceptable. Therefore, whether one takes a stalk or a pillar, a leper or Xi Shi, the grotesque, the strange, the deceptive, and the bizarre—the Dao unites them all as one. In their division, they are completed; in their completion, they are destroyed. All things are neither completed nor destroyed, but return to unity.

Only the enlightened know this unity and, for this reason, do not employ it but lodge it in the ordinary. The ordinary is function; function is passage; passage is attainment. Attaining this, one is nearly there. Following this, though unaware of how it is so, is called the Dao. To exhaust one’s spirit and intelligence in unifying things yet remain unaware of their sameness is called "Morning Three." What is "Morning Three"? It is said: "The monkey keeper distributed chestnuts, saying, 'Three in the morning and four at dusk.' The monkeys were all enraged. He then said, 'Then four in the morning and three at dusk.' The monkeys were all pleased." Names and realities were diminished, yet joy and anger were employed—this, too, follows from this. Therefore, the sage harmonizes right and wrong and rests in the evenness of Heaven. This is called walking two paths.

The knowledge of the ancients reached its utmost. Where did it reach?

There are those who hold that things have never yet begun to exist—this is the utmost, the exhaustion of all, beyond which nothing can be added! Next are those who hold that things exist, yet boundaries have never yet arisen. Next are those who hold that boundaries exist, yet right and wrong have never yet appeared. When right and wrong become manifest, the Dao suffers loss. The Dao suffers loss, and attachment takes form. Is there truly gain and loss? Or is there truly neither gain nor loss? If there is gain and loss, then Master Zhao’s zither-playing exists; if there is neither gain nor loss, then Master Zhao’s zither-playing does not exist. Master Zhao’s zither-playing, Kuang’s wielding of the staff, and Master Hui’s leaning against the catalpa tree—these three men’s knowledge nearly reached perfection, each in their own excellence, and so their deeds are recorded at life’s end. Yet because they were fond of something, they differed from others; because they were fond of it, they sought to make it clear. But what they sought to clarify was not what could be clarified, and so they ended in the obscurity of "hard and white." And Master Zhao’s son ended with the threads of his father’s art, yet accomplished nothing his whole life. If this can be called accomplishment, then even I have accomplished something. If this cannot be called accomplishment, then neither things nor I have accomplished anything. Thus, the dazzling confusion of slippery doubt is what the sage despises. Instead of employing such things, one lodges them in the ordinary—this is called "illuminating through non-illumination."

Now, let me say something here: is it of the same kind as this, or not of the same kind? Whether of the same kind or not, all things are of the same kind, and so there is no difference from that. Nevertheless, let me try to speak of it. There is a beginning; there is a time before that beginning; there is a time before the time before that beginning. There is being; there is non-being; there is a time before non-being; there is a time before the time before non-being. Suddenly there is being and non-being, yet we do not know which of the two—being or non-being—truly is. Now I have already spoken, yet I do not know whether what I have spoken truly speaks of something or truly speaks of nothing.

In all the world, nothing is greater than the tip of an autumn hair, yet Mount Tai is small; no one lives longer than a child who dies young, yet Pengzu died prematurely. Heaven and Earth were born together with me, and the myriad things and I are one. Since we are already one, can there still be speech? Since I have already called it one, can there be no speech? One and speech make two; two and one make three. From this point onward, even the most skillful reckoner cannot reach the end—how much less can ordinary people! Therefore, from non-being to being, we reach three—how much more if we proceed from being to being! Do not proceed; abide in this!

The Dao has never had boundaries; words have never had constancy. Yet because of this, distinctions arise. Let me speak of these distinctions: there is left, there is right; there are ranks, there are duties; there are divisions, there are debates; there is competition, there is contention—these are called the eight virtues. Beyond the six directions, the sage acknowledges but does not discuss; within the six directions, the sage discusses but does not debate. The *Spring and Autumn Annals* records the intentions of the former kings, and the sage debates but does not argue. Thus, where there is division, there is also non-division; where there is debate, there is also non-debate.

One asks: "Why is this?" It is because the sage embraces it, while the multitude debate it to display to one another. Thus it is said: debate exists because there are things unseen. The great Dao is not named; great debate does not speak; great benevolence is not benevolent; great integrity is not modest; great courage does not harm. When the Dao is made manifest, it is no longer the Dao; when words are debated, they do not reach; when benevolence is constant, it does not succeed; when integrity is pure, it is not trusted; when courage is harmful, it does not succeed. These five are round yet nearly square! Therefore, to know where knowledge stops is perfection. Who knows the debate of no words, the Dao of no speaking? If one can know this, it is called the Heavenly Storehouse. Pour into it and it is never full; draw from it and it never runs dry, yet one does not know its source—this is called the Preserved Light.

Thus, in ancient times, Yao asked Shun: "I wish to attack Zong, Kuai, and Xu’ao. When I face south, I feel uneasy. What is the reason for this?"

Shun replied: "Those three rulers remain among the brambles and weeds. Why should you feel uneasy? In ancient times, ten suns appeared together, illuminating all things—how much more so the virtue that surpasses the sun!"

Nie Que asked Wang Ni: "Do you know what all things agree upon as right?"
Wang Ni said: "How would I know?"
"Do you know what you do not know?"
"How would I know?"
"Then do things have no knowledge?"
"How would I know?"

Though this be so, let me try to speak of it. How do I know that what I call knowing is not not-knowing? How do I know that what I call not-knowing is not knowing? Now, let me ask you this: When people sleep in dampness, their waists ache and they become half-paralyzed—is that true of loaches? When they dwell in trees, they tremble with fear—is that true of monkeys? Of these three, which knows the proper place to dwell? People eat the flesh of grass-fed and grain-fed animals; deer eat grass; centipedes delight in snakes; owls and crows relish mice. Of these four, which knows the proper taste? Monkeys pair with monkeys, deer mate with deer, loaches consort with fish. Mao Qiang and Li Ji were considered beautiful by men; but when fish saw them, they dove deep; when birds saw them, they flew high; when deer saw them, they bolted away. Of these four, which knows the true beauty of the world? From my perspective, the beginnings of benevolence and righteousness, the paths of right and wrong—all are tangled and confused. How could I distinguish them?"

Nie Que said: "If you do not know benefit and harm, does the Perfect Man also not know benefit and harm?"
Wang Ni replied: "The Perfect Man is divine! Though great marshes burn, he is not hot; though rivers freeze, he is not cold; though thunder splits mountains and whirlwinds shake the sea, he is not startled. One like this rides the clouds and qi, mounts the sun and moon, and roams beyond the four seas. Death and life do not alter him—how much less the beginnings of benefit and harm!"

Ququezi asked Changwuzi: "I have heard from the Master that the sage does not engage in affairs, does not pursue profit, does not avoid harm, does not seek, does not follow the Dao; speaks as though silent, is silent as though speaking, and roams beyond the dust and grime. The Master considered this reckless talk, but I see it as the practice of the wondrous Dao. What do you think?"
Changwuzi said: "Even the Yellow Emperor would be dazzled by these words—how could Confucius suffice! Moreover, you are too hasty in your calculations, seeing an egg and demanding a rooster at dawn, seeing a slingshot and craving roast owl. Let me speak to you recklessly, and you may listen recklessly.

Why cling to sun and moon, embracing the cosmos? Let your lips meet, set aside confusion, and honor one another as servants. The multitude toil endlessly; the sage appears foolish and dull, blending the myriad ages into a single purity. All things are thus, mutually enfolding one another. How do I know that delight in life is not delusion? How do I know that aversion to death is not like a lost youth who does not know his way home?

Li Ji was the daughter of the border warden of Ai. When the state of Jin first obtained her, she wept until her collar was soaked; but once she reached the king’s palace, shared his bed, and ate fine meats, she regretted her tears. How do I know that the dead do not regret their former longing for life? Those who dream of drinking wake to weep; those who dream of weeping wake to hunt. While dreaming, they do not know it is a dream; within the dream, they may even interpret it. Only after waking do they know it was a dream. And only after the great awakening will they know that this was all a great dream. Yet fools believe themselves awake, busily distinguishing—lord and shepherd, how rigid! You and I are both dreams; when I say you are dreaming, I too am dreaming. Such words are called ‘uncanny reversal.’ Even after ten thousand ages, if a great sage appears who understands their meaning, it will be as though you met him morning and evening.

Suppose you and I debate: if you defeat me, and I do not defeat you, are you truly right and I truly wrong? If I defeat you, and you do not defeat me, am I truly right and you truly wrong? Is one of us right and the other wrong? Are both of us right or both wrong? You and I cannot know. Then people are inherently shrouded in darkness—whom shall we ask to judge? Shall we ask one who agrees with you? Since he already agrees with you, how can he judge? Shall we ask one who agrees with me? Since he already agrees with me, how can he judge? Shall we ask one who differs from both of us? Since he differs from both, how can he judge? Shall we ask one who agrees with both of us? Since he agrees with both, how can he judge? Then you, I, and others cannot know—must we wait for another?"

"What does it mean to harmonize with the heavenly equality?"
"It means: ‘Is it so? Is it not so? If it is truly so, then its difference from ‘not so’ requires no debate; if it is truly so, then its difference from ‘not so’ requires no debate.’ The mutual dependence of changing sounds is like their independence. Harmonize with the heavenly equality, follow the flowing transformations, and thus live out your years. Forget years, forget righteousness, be stirred by the boundless, and thus dwell in the boundless."

The Shadowy Doubt asked the Shadow: "Earlier you were walking, now you are still; earlier you were sitting, now you are standing. Why are you so without constancy?"
The Shadow said: "Do I depend on something to be thus? Does what I depend on also depend on something to be thus? Do I depend on the scales of a snake or the wings of a cicada? How do I know why it is so? How do I know why it is not so?"

Once, Zhuang Zhou dreamed he was a butterfly, a fluttering butterfly, content with himself and doing as he pleased. He did not know he was Zhou. Suddenly he awoke, and there he was, solidly and unmistakably Zhou. But he did not know whether he was Zhou who had dreamed he was a butterfly, or a butterfly dreaming he was Zhou. Between Zhou and the butterfly, there must be some distinction. This is called the transformation of things.

**Chapter 3: The Secret of Caring for Life**

My life has a limit, but knowledge has none. To pursue the limitless with the limited is perilous. And to pursue knowledge thus is even more perilous! In doing good, avoid seeking fame; in doing evil, avoid incurring punishment. Follow the central course as your guiding principle—this way you may preserve your body, fulfill your life, nurture your kin, and live out your years.

Cook Ding was carving an ox for Lord Wenhui. Where his hand touched, his shoulder leaned, his foot pressed, his knee braced—with a *hua* and a *xiang*, the blade sang, slicing *huo* through the flesh, never missing the rhythm, harmonizing with the "Mulberry Grove" dance, attaining the "Jingshou" melody.

Lord Wenhui exclaimed, "Ah! Excellent! How has your skill reached such heights?"

Cook Ding laid down his knife and replied, "What your servant loves is the Dao, which goes beyond mere skill. When I first began carving oxen, all I saw was the whole ox. After three years, I no longer saw the whole ox. Now, I meet it with my spirit rather than my eyes; my senses halt, but my spirit moves. Following the natural grain, I cleave the great seams, guide the blade through the great cavities, all according to its inherent structure. My knife has never touched the ligaments or tendons, much less the great bones! A good cook changes his knife every year—it is worn from cutting. An ordinary cook changes his knife every month—it is broken from hacking. But my knife has been in use for nineteen years, carving thousands of oxen, and its edge is still as if freshly whetted. For between the joints there are gaps, and the blade has no thickness. When what has no thickness enters where there is a gap, how vast the space for the blade to move! That is why after nineteen years, my knife’s edge is still as if freshly whetted. Nevertheless, whenever I come to a complex joint, I see the difficulty, I grow cautious, my gaze fixes, my movements slow. The blade moves ever so slightly, and *pffft*—the flesh falls apart like earth crumbling to the ground. Then I stand, knife in hand, look all around, savor the moment with satisfaction, wipe the blade clean, and put it away."

Lord Wenhui said, "Excellent! From Cook Ding’s words, I have learned how to care for life."

Gongwen Xuan saw the Right Master and was startled, saying, "What sort of man is this?"

"Whence comes this distinction? Is it from Heaven, or from man?"
"It is from Heaven, not from man. Heaven’s life creates this uniqueness, while man’s form has companions. By this we know it is from Heaven, not from man."

A marsh pheasant takes ten steps for one peck, a hundred steps for one drink, yet it does not seek to be kept in a cage. Though its spirit may be regal, it would not be well.

When Lao Dan died, Qin Shi went to mourn him. After three cries, he left.
His disciples said, "Was he not your teacher’s friend?"
"Yes."
"Then is it proper to mourn him in this way?"
"Yes. At first, I thought he was a man, but now I see he was not. When I entered to mourn just now, the elders wailed for him as if for their own children, the young wailed for him as if for their own mothers. In their gathering, there must have been those who spoke without seeking to speak, who wailed without seeking to wail. This is fleeing from Heaven and doubling back on feeling, forgetting what one has received—what the ancients called ‘the punishment of fleeing from Heaven.’ When he came, it was the Master’s time; when he departed, the Master followed. Abiding in timeliness and dwelling in compliance, sorrow and joy cannot enter. The ancients called this ‘the Great Release of the Lord.’"

A finger may be exhausted as firewood, but the fire is transmitted—who knows when it will end?

**Book Two, Chapter Four: "Among the World of Men"**
Yan Hui saw Confucius and asked to go forth.

Yan Hui said, "Where are you going?"
He replied, "I intend to go to Wei."
Confucius asked, "What will you do there?"
Yan Hui said, "I have heard that the Lord of Wei is in his prime, yet his conduct is willful. He treats his state lightly and does not recognize his faults. He treats the lives of his people as if they were nothing—so many die that the state measures them like marsh reeds, and the people have nowhere to turn. I once heard you say, ‘Leave a well-governed state; go to a disordered one. The physician’s gate is crowded with the sick.’ Reflecting on what I have heard, I hope that perhaps his state may yet be cured."

Confucius said, "Alas! You will likely go only to be punished! The Dao does not tolerate mixture—once mixed, it becomes excessive, and excess brings disorder. Disorder brings worry, and worry cannot be remedied. The perfected of old first established it within themselves before establishing it in others. When what is established within is settled, how could one have leisure to meddle in the conduct of violent men?

"Do you also know where virtue is swept away and from where knowledge arises? Virtue is swept away by fame, and knowledge arises from contention. Fame is the means by which men wound one another; knowledge is the tool of contention. Both are inauspicious instruments—not the way to act fully.

"Moreover, though one’s virtue be deep and trust unshaken, if one has not yet penetrated the qi of others, and though one’s fame be uncontentious, if one has not yet reached their hearts—yet to force upon them the language of benevolence and righteousness, the carpenter’s line of moral judgment, before violent men, is to make others resent what they see as one’s own beauty. This is called ‘bringing calamity upon others.’ And those who bring calamity upon others will surely have calamity returned upon them. You will likely become a calamity to others!

"If you merely delight in the worthy and despise the unworthy, what use is it to seek to be different? If you do not even issue commands, the lords and princes will surely exploit your presence to vie for advantage. Your eyes will be dazzled, your complexion smoothed, your mouth will scheme, your demeanor will take shape, and your heart will be molded by it. This is like quenching fire with fire, or flooding water with water—it is called ‘increasing the excess.’ If you follow this course without end, you will surely perish before violent men, for your earnest words will be met with distrust.

"Moreover, in ancient times, Jie killed Guan Longfeng, and Zhou killed Prince Bigan—both were men who cultivated themselves and humbled themselves to soothe the people, yet they opposed their superiors. Their lords used their cultivation to cast them out. They were lovers of fame.

"Again, in ancient times, Yao attacked Congzhi and Xuao, and Yu attacked Youhu. Their states became wastelands of the dead, and they themselves were executed. Their warfare did not cease, and their pursuit of substance knew no end. These were all men who sought fame and substance—have you alone not heard of this? Fame and substance are things even the sages cannot overcome—how much less can you!

"Nevertheless, if you must have some way, come and tell me your plan."

Yan Hui said, "If I am upright yet empty, diligent yet single-minded, will that suffice?"
Confucius said, "Alas! How could that suffice?"

"Now, those who take yang as their fullness, their brilliance dazzling and colors shifting, are never opposed by ordinary men. They follow the inclinations of others to seek harmony with their hearts. Yet this is called a virtue that forms day by day—how much less can great virtue be achieved! They cling rigidly without transformation, outwardly compliant yet inwardly unexamined—how could this ever suffice!"

"Then let me be inwardly straight yet outwardly yielding, achieving while aligning with those above. To be inwardly straight is to follow Heaven as one’s companion. Those who follow Heaven know that the Son of Heaven and they themselves are all children of Heaven. Do they seek others’ approval when they speak, or fear others’ disapproval? Such a one is called a childlike man—this is what it means to follow Heaven as one’s companion. To be outwardly yielding is to follow men as one’s companions. Kneeling, bowing, and curving the body are the rites of a minister. If all men do this, how dare I not do the same? To do what others do is to be free from their blame—this is what it means to follow men as one’s companions. To achieve while aligning with those above is to follow the ancients as one’s companions. Though their words may instruct, their essence is borrowed—what the ancients possessed, I do not. Such a one, though straight, is not reproached—this is what it means to follow the ancients as one’s companion. Would this suffice?"

Confucius said, "Alas! How could this suffice!"

Excessive governance, with laws that do not deceive, may be firm yet blameless. Yet this is but the least of it—how could it reach transformation? It is still to follow one’s own mind."

Yan Hui said, "I have no further way forward. I venture to ask for your method."
Confucius said, "Fast, and I will tell you. To act while possessing—is this easy? Those who find it easy are not fit for the vastness of Heaven."

Yan Hui said, "My family is poor, and for months I have neither drunk wine nor eaten meat. Is this fasting?"
Confucius said, "This is the fasting for sacrifice, not the fasting of the mind."
Yan Hui said, "I venture to ask about the fasting of the mind."
Confucius said, "Unify your will. Do not listen with the ears but with the mind; do not listen with the mind but with qi. Listening stops at the ears, the mind stops at signs. Qi is emptiness that awaits things. Only the Dao gathers in emptiness. Emptiness is the fasting of the mind."

Yan Hui said, "Before I received your instruction, I was truly myself. Having received it, I have never been myself—can this be called emptiness?"
The Master said, "It is complete! Now I tell you: If you can enter its enclosure without being moved by its name, respond when it comes, cease when it does not—without gate, without harm, dwelling in one abode and lodging in the unattainable—you are nearly there. To leave no trace is easy; to walk without touching the ground is hard. To be employed by men is easy to falsify; to be employed by Heaven is hard to falsify. I have heard of flying with wings, but never of flying without wings; I have heard of knowing with knowledge, but never of knowing without knowledge. Look into that empty chamber: the empty room generates brightness, and auspiciousness comes to rest. If it does not rest, this is called sitting but galloping. If you follow the ears and eyes to communicate within while shutting out the mind’s knowledge, even ghosts and spirits will come to dwell—how much more men! This is the transformation of the ten thousand things, the pivot of Yu and Shun, the ultimate course of Fuxi and Jiqu. How much more those who are scattered!"

Lord Zigao of Ye was about to be sent as an envoy to Qi. He asked Confucius, saying, "The king’s charge to me is most weighty. Qi treats envoys with great respect but little urgency. Even a common man cannot be moved—how much less a feudal lord! I am deeply anxious. You once told me: ‘In all matters, whether small or great, few do not follow the Way to achieve success. If the matter does not succeed, there will surely be human calamity; if it succeeds, there will surely be the calamity of imbalance between yin and yang. Only those of virtue can achieve success or failure without calamity.’ I eat coarse food and do not seek refinement, and my cook has no desire to purify himself. Yet now, having received my charge in the morning, by evening I drink ice—am I not inwardly burning? Before I have even reached the truth of the matter, I already suffer the calamity of yin and yang. If the matter does not succeed, there will surely be human calamity—this is a double burden. As a minister, I am not equal to the task. I venture to ask for your instruction!"

Confucius said, "Under Heaven there are two great precepts: one is fate, the other is duty. A child’s love for parents is fate—it cannot be untied from the heart. A minister’s service to his lord is duty—there is no place in Heaven and Earth where it does not apply. These are called the great precepts. Thus, in serving one’s parents, one finds peace without choosing the place—this is the perfection of filial piety. In serving one’s lord, one finds peace without choosing the task—this is the height of loyalty. In serving one’s own mind, sorrow and joy do not alter what lies before it; knowing the inevitable and finding peace in it as fate—this is the perfection of virtue. As a minister and child, one is fundamentally constrained by what cannot be avoided. Proceeding with the truth of affairs and forgetting oneself, how could one not delight in life and dread death? Your conduct is sufficient! Let me repeat what I have heard: In close dealings, trust must be mutual; in distant dealings, words must convey loyalty. Words must sometimes be transmitted. To transmit words that bring double joy or double anger is the most difficult task under Heaven. Double joy must overflow with excessive praise; double anger must overflow with excessive blame. All excess is delusion; delusion makes trust unreliable, and unreliable transmission brings misfortune. Thus the adage says: ‘Transmit the constant truth, do not transmit excess words—then you are nearly whole.’

Moreover, those who contend with skill and strength begin with openness but often end in secrecy; when it grows great, it produces many strange tricks. Those who drink with ritual begin in order but often end in chaos; when it grows great, it produces many strange pleasures. All affairs are like this: they begin in good faith but often end in pettiness. Their beginnings are simple, but their endings must be vast. Words are like wind and waves; actions are like gain and loss. Wind and waves are easily stirred; gain and loss are easily endangered. Thus anger arises without cause, and clever words lead to biased speech. When beasts die, they do not choose their cries; their breath is labored, and their hearts grow fierce. When scrutiny becomes extreme, there must be unworthy responses, though one does not know why. If one does not know why, who knows how it will end! Thus the adage says: ‘Do not urge completion when changing orders. Excess leads to transgression.’ Urging completion when changing orders endangers affairs. Beauty achieved over time, evil done before it can be corrected—can one not be cautious!

To ride things with the mind, entrusting oneself to the unavoidable to nurture the center—this is perfection. What need is there for action in return? It is best to deliver the charge—this is the difficult part."

Yan He, about to serve as tutor to the crown prince of Duke Ling of Wei, asked Qu Boyu, saying: "There is a man here whose virtue is heaven-sent in its destructiveness. If I deal with him without method, it will endanger our state; if I deal with him by method, it will endanger myself. His knowledge is just sufficient to perceive others' faults, yet he does not know why they are at fault. In such a case, what am I to do?"

Qu Boyu replied: "Excellent question! Be on guard, be cautious—first rectify yourself! In form, draw near; in heart, harmonize. Yet even these two have their perils. In drawing near, do not enter too deeply; in harmonizing, do not reveal too much. If in form you draw near and enter too deeply, you will stumble, be destroyed, be overthrown. If in heart you harmonize and reveal too much, you will become a voice, a reputation, a portent, a calamity.

If he acts like an infant, then act like an infant with him; if he acts without boundaries, then act without boundaries with him; if he acts without limits, then act without limits with him. In penetrating this, you will enter into flawlessness.

Do you not know of the mantis? It brandished its arms to block the chariot wheel, unaware that it was unequal to the task—such was the beauty of its talent! Be on guard, be cautious! To accumulate merit and beauty and then offend him—this is perilously close!

Do you not know of those who raise tigers? They dare not feed them living creatures, for fear of provoking their killing rage; they dare not feed them whole creatures, for fear of provoking their rending rage. They time their hunger and satiety, understanding the heart of their anger. Tigers are of a different kind from humans, yet they fawn upon those who feed them—this is because of compliance. Thus, those they kill are those who act in opposition.

Now, those who love horses use baskets to collect their droppings and clamshells to collect their urine. Yet if a mosquito or horsefly lands upon them and they are slapped at the wrong moment, the horse may break its bit, smash its head, or shatter its chest. The intention was to care, yet the love led to loss. Can one not be cautious?"

A master carpenter named Shi traveled to Qi and arrived at Quyuan, where he saw a sacred oak tree of the earth shrine. Its vastness could shade thousands of oxen; measuring its girth required a hundred spans. Its height towered to the clouds before branches appeared, and a dozen or more of its side limbs could serve as boats. Spectators gathered like a marketplace, yet the master carpenter paid no heed and walked on without pause. His disciple, having feasted his eyes upon it, caught up to Master Shi and said, "Since I took up the axe and followed you, I have never seen timber so magnificent. Why did you not even glance at it, but walk on without stopping?"

The master replied, "Enough! Say no more! It is but a worthless tree. If used for a boat, it would sink; for a coffin or outer casket, it would rot swiftly; for vessels, it would quickly break; for doors or gates, it would ooze sap; for pillars, it would be eaten by worms. It is a tree of no use. Precisely because of this, it has attained such longevity."

Upon returning home, the sacred oak appeared to him in a dream, saying, "With what would you compare me? Would you liken me to the so-called 'useful' trees? Those hawthorns, pears, oranges, and pomelos—all the fruit-bearing kinds—once their fruits ripen, they are stripped and humiliated. Their great branches are broken, their small branches plundered. This is how their abilities bring suffering to their lives. Thus, they do not fulfill their natural span but perish midway, struck down by the common world. All things are like this. As for me, I have sought to be of no use for a long time; nearly did I die before I attained it—now it is my great use. Had I been useful, could I have grown so vast? Moreover, you and I are both things—how can one thing judge another? And you, a nearly dead worthless man, what do you know of a worthless tree?"

Master Shi awoke and pondered his dream. His disciple asked, "If it seeks to be of no use, why then does it serve as a shrine?"

"Hush!" he replied. "Speak no more!"

That tree merely lodges there—taking the scorn of those who do not know it as its refuge. Had it not become a shrine, it would long since have been felled! Moreover, what it preserves differs from the common, yet to explain it by righteousness—how far removed is that!"

Master Nanbo Ziqi wandered to the hills of Shang, where he saw an extraordinary tree. A thousand chariots could shelter beneath its shade, its branches thick enough to conceal the sun. Ziqi said, "What tree is this? It must possess rare timber!" Looking up at its slender branches, he saw they were gnarled and useless for beams or rafters; looking down at its great roots, he saw they were split and rotten, unfit for coffins or outer caskets. Licking its leaves, his mouth blistered and burned; inhaling its scent, he fell into a frenzy, waking only after three days.

Ziqi said, "This is truly a worthless tree—yet it has grown so vast! Alas, the spirit-like person—such is their worthlessness!"

In Song there was a region called Jing, where catalpa, cypress, and mulberry thrived. Those with trunks the span of two hands were felled by those seeking pegs for monkey perches; those three or four spans around were felled by those seeking timber for lofty names; those seven or eight spans around were felled by the households of nobles and wealthy merchants seeking planks for their halls. Thus they did not fulfill their natural span but perished midway under the axe—this is the calamity of being useful.

Therefore, an ox with a white forehead, a pig with an upturned snout, or a person with hemorrhoids cannot cross the river—all known by shamans and diviners as inauspicious. Yet this is precisely what the spirit-like person deems most auspicious.

There was one named Zhili Shu, whose chin nestled into his navel, shoulders rose above his crown, the meeting of his spine pointed heavenward, his five organs sat atop, and his thighs flanked his ribs. Mending needles and washing clothes, he earned enough to feed himself; winnowing grain and sifting chaff, he could feed ten. When the ruler conscripted warriors, Zhili Shu rolled up his sleeves and wandered among them; when great labor was imposed, he, being chronically infirm, was exempt from service; when grain was given to the ailing, he received three bushels and ten bundles of firewood. Though his form was broken, it still sufficed to nourish his body and fulfill his natural span—how much more so for one whose virtue is thus "broken"!

Confucius traveled to Chu, and Jie Yu, the madman of Chu, wandered near his gate, singing:
*"Phoenix! Phoenix!
How the virtue has declined!
The age to come cannot be awaited,
The past cannot be pursued.
When the world follows the Dao,
The sage achieves;
When the world loses the Dao,
The sage survives.
In this present time,
One barely escapes punishment.
Blessing is lighter than a feather,
Yet none know how to bear it;
Calamity is heavier than the earth,
Yet none know how to avoid it.
Enough! Enough!
Approach others with virtue!
Perilous! Perilous!
Marking the ground to follow!
Thorns! Thorns!
Do not harm my path!
My path winds crookedly—
Do not harm my feet!"*

Mountain trees bring ruin upon themselves; fat fuels its own burning. Cinnamon is edible, so it is felled; lacquer is useful, so it is slashed. All know the use of the useful, but none know the use of the useless.

**Chapter Five: Signs of Full Virtue (Part Two, Below)**

In Lu there was a man named Wang Tai who had lost his feet; those who followed him were as numerous as those who followed Confucius. Chang Ji asked Confucius, "Wang Tai is a man without feet, yet his followers divide Lu equally with you. He stands without teaching, sits without discoursing. Those who go to him empty return full."

"Is there truly a teaching without words, a shaping of the heart without form? What manner of man is this?"

Confucius replied, "This Master is a sage. I merely follow behind him. If even I would take him as my teacher, how much more should those beneath me! Why limit this to the state of Lu? I would lead all under Heaven to follow him."

Chang Ji said, "He is a man without feet, yet he surpasses his teacher—how far he surpasses the common man! If this is so, how does he employ his mind?"

Confucius said, "Though life and death are great matters, they do not alter him. Though heaven and earth should collapse, he would not be lost with them. He examines what is without reliance and does not shift with things; he commands the transformations of things yet holds fast to his root."

Chang Ji asked, "What does this mean?"

Confucius replied, "Viewed from what makes them different, liver and gall are as distant as Chu and Yue. Viewed from what makes them the same, all things are one. One who is thus does not know what the ears and eyes deem fitting but roams the mind in the harmony of virtue. He sees what is one in things and not what is lost; he regards the loss of his feet as no more than discarded dirt."

Chang Ji said, "He cultivates himself through his understanding, attains his heart through his heart. Why do things gather to him above all others?"

Confucius replied, "Men do not mirror themselves in flowing water but in still water. Only stillness can still the many. Rooted in earth, only the pine and cypress stand upright, green through winter and summer. Rooted in Heaven, only Yao and Shun stand upright, foremost among the myriad things. Fortunate is he who can rectify life, thereby rectifying the lives of others. The proof of preserving the beginning lies in fearlessness—one brave man can charge into nine armies. Those who seek fame and bind themselves to it are yet like this, how much more one who governs Heaven and Earth, stores the myriad things, treats the six limbs as mere lodging, takes the ears and eyes as mere images, unifies all he knows, and whose heart never dies! He will choose his day to ascend on high, and men will follow him. How would he deign to make things his concern?"

Apintu Jia, a man without feet, studied under the same master as Zichan of Zheng, Bohun Wuren. Zichan said to Apintu Jia, "When I leave, you stay; when you leave first, I stay." The next day, they again sat together in the same hall on the same mat. Zichan said to Apintu, "When I leave first, you stay; when you leave first, I stay. Now I am about to leave—can you stay, or not? Moreover, when you see the one in power, you defy him. Do you consider yourself equal to the one in power?"

Apintu Jia replied, "In the master’s gate, is there truly one in power such as this? Are you pleased to be called one who defers to power and puts himself last? I have heard it said: ‘When the mirror is bright, dust does not settle; if dust settles, it is not bright. To dwell long with the worthy is to be without fault.’ Now what you value most is the master, yet you still speak like this—is this not a fault?"

Zichan said, "You are already like this, yet you still contend with Yao in goodness. By your own virtue, are you not sufficient to reflect upon yourself?"

Apintu Jia said, "Those who justify their faults and claim they should not have lost their feet are many; those who do not justify their faults and yet should not have kept their feet are few. To know there is nothing to be done and yet accept it as fate—only one with virtue can do this. In Yi’s drawn bow, the center is the target; yet those who do not hit it is a matter of fate. People with whole feet laugh at me for not having whole feet, and I would bristle with anger. But when I come to the master’s place, I return calm and composed. Do I not know that the master washes me with goodness? Or is it that I awaken myself? I have wandered with you for nineteen years, yet you have never known that I lack feet. Now you wander with me within the realm of form and spirit, yet you seek me outside of form—is this not a fault?"

Zichan, startled, changed his expression and said, "You need not say more!"

In Lu there was a man without feet, Shushan Wuzhi, who came to see Confucius on his heels. Confucius said, "You were not careful, and already suffered such misfortune. Though you come now, what can be done?"

Wuzhi said, "I simply did not know my duty and lightly used my body, which is why I lost my feet. Now I come because I still possess what is more honorable than feet, and so I strive to preserve it whole. Heaven covers all without exception; Earth bears all without exception. I took you, Master, to be like Heaven—how could I have known you would be like this?"

Confucius said, "I am indeed shallow! Why do you not come in? Let me share with you what I have heard." Wuzhi left.

Confucius said to his disciples, "Take heed! Wuzhi, a man without feet, still strives to learn to make up for the faults of his past conduct—how much more should a man of whole virtue do so!"

Wuzhi said to Lao Dan, "Is Confucius not yet a perfected man? Why does he persistently treat learning as a guest? He seeks to be known for strange and marvelous fame, unaware that to the perfected man, this is but his own shackles."

Lao Dan said, "Why not simply lead him to see life and death as a single thread, the permissible and impermissible as one continuum, and thereby free him from his shackles? Would that be possible?"

Wuzhi said, "It is Heaven’s punishment—how can it be freed?"

Duke Ai of Lu asked Confucius, saying, "In Wei there is an ugly man named Aitai Tuo. Men who dwell with him grow so attached they cannot bear to leave. Women who see him beg their parents, saying, 'Rather than be another man’s wife, I would be this gentleman’s concubine'—a dozen or more, and still they come. He never takes the lead in anything, only ever harmonizing with others. He holds no lordly rank to deliver men from death, nor hoards wealth to fill men’s bellies. Moreover, his ugliness startles all beneath heaven, yet he harmonizes without leading, and his wisdom never extends beyond his four bounds. Yet men and women alike gather before him—he must possess something beyond other men. I summoned him to observe him, and indeed, his ugliness startled all beneath heaven. When he dwelt with me, before a month had passed, I took note of his character; before a year had elapsed, I trusted him. The state had no chief minister, so I offered him the governance. He responded with reluctance, as if declining; I felt ashamed, yet in the end I entrusted him with the state. In no time at all, he left me and departed. I grieved as though I had suffered a loss, as though no one remained to share the joy of ruling this state. What manner of man is he?"

Confucius said, "Once, when I was sent on a mission to Chu, I happened to see some piglets nursing from their dead mother. After a little while, they glanced about in alarm and all abandoned her to flee. They did not see themselves in her, nor did they find her like them. What they loved in their mother was not her form, but what animated her form. When men die in battle, their burials are not furnished with ritual trappings; when a man’s feet are severed, no one cherishes his shoes—neither has its root."

The attendants of the Son of Heaven do not pare their nails or pierce their ears; those who take a wife remain outside the palace and are no longer employed. Even one whose form is whole may thus suffice for such service—how much more so the man of complete virtue!

Now, this Ai Taituo spoke not yet won trust, yet was trusted; had no merit, yet was beloved. He made men offer him a state, only fearing he would not accept. He must be one whose talents are whole and whose virtue does not manifest in form."

Duke Ai said, "What is meant by *talents whole*?"

Confucius replied, "Life and death, survival and ruin, success and failure, poverty and wealth, worthiness and unworthiness, slander and praise, hunger and thirst, cold and heat—these are the workings of fate, the course of destiny. They alternate before us day and night, yet knowledge cannot fathom their beginning. Thus, they must not be allowed to disturb harmony or enter the treasury of the mind. Let them bring harmony and joy, flowing freely without losing their openness. Let day and night pass without gap, so that one’s springtime spirit pervades all things. This is what it means to have *talents whole*."

"What is meant by *virtue not manifest in form*?"

"Stillness is the fullness of water at rest. It may serve as a model, for inwardly it preserves equilibrium without agitation. Virtue is the cultivation of perfect harmony. When virtue does not manifest in form, things cannot depart from it."

On another day, Duke Ai told Min Zi what he had heard: "At first, I thought that by facing south as ruler of the world, upholding the people’s guiding principles and grieving over their deaths, I had attained the utmost understanding. Now, hearing of the *perfect man*, I fear I lack his reality—have taken my person lightly and risked losing my state. Between Confucius and me, there is no lord and minister, only a friendship of virtue."

Yin Qi, Zhili, and Wu Chun persuaded Duke Ling of Wei, and the duke delighted in them.

When he looked at whole men, their necks seemed so slender.

The hunchbacked man with a huge goiter charmed Duke Huan of Qi, and the duke delighted in him; when he looked at whole men, their necks seemed so slender.

Thus virtue may excel while form is forgotten. When men forget what should be forgotten and forget even what should not be forgotten, this is called true forgetting.

Therefore, the sage wanders freely—yet knowledge becomes a snare, covenants turn to glue, virtue becomes a bond, and craft becomes commerce. The sage does not scheme; why employ knowledge? He does not carve; why employ glue? He suffers no loss; why employ virtue? He does not trade; why engage in commerce? These four are the nourishment of Heaven. What Heaven nourishes is the sustenance of Heaven. Having received sustenance from Heaven, why employ man?

One may possess the form of a man yet lack the passions of a man. Possessing the form of a man, one mingles with men; lacking the passions of a man, right and wrong do not adhere to one’s person. How tiny and insignificant—this way of being human! How vast and great—alone to complete one’s Heaven!

Hui Zi said to Zhuangzi: "Can man truly be without passion?"
Zhuangzi said: "Yes."
Hui Zi said: "If a man is without passion, how can he be called a man?"
Zhuangzi said: "The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form—how can he not be called a man?"
Hui Zi said: "If he is called a man, how can he be without passion?"
Zhuangzi said: "This is not what I mean by passion. What I call being without passion is that one does not allow likes and dislikes to enter and harm the self, but constantly follows the natural without adding to life."
Hui Zi said: "If one does not add to life, how can one preserve one’s body?"
Zhuangzi said: "The Dao gives him his appearance, Heaven gives him his form—do not allow likes and dislikes to enter and injure the body. Now you externalize your spirit, exhaust your essence, lean against a tree, rest on a withered catalpa, and doze. Heaven chose your form, yet you chatter about 'hard and white'!"

Book Three, Part One: Chapter Six – The Great and Venerable Teacher

To know what Heaven does and to know what man does—this is perfection! To know what Heaven does is to be born of Heaven; to know what man does is to use what one’s knowledge knows to nourish what one’s knowledge does not know, so that one completes one’s Heaven-appointed years without dying prematurely—this is the fullness of knowledge.

Yet there is a danger. Knowledge must await something before it is fitting, but what it awaits is never certain. How do I know that what I call Heaven is not man? How do I know that what I call man is not Heaven? Only when there is a true person is there true knowledge.

What is a true person? The true persons of old did not oppose the few, did not take pride in success, did not scheme. When they erred, they did not regret; when they hit the mark, they did not feel self-satisfied. When they climbed high, they did not tremble; when they entered water, they were not drenched; when they entered fire, they did not feel the heat. Their knowledge could ascend to the Dao in this way.

The true persons of old slept without dreaming, woke without worry; they ate without relishing flavor, and their breath was deep. Their breath came from their heels, while the breath of ordinary people came from their throats. Those who are bent and submissive choke on their words as if retching. The deeper their desires, the shallower their Heavenly mechanism.

The true persons of old did not delight in life, did not detest death. They did not rejoice at coming forth, nor resist at going in. Swiftly they came, swiftly they went—no more. They did not seek the beginning, nor pursue the end. They received and rejoiced in it, forgot and returned to it—this is what is called not using the mind to harm the Dao, not using man to assist Heaven. This is what is called a true person.

Such a one’s mind is forgotten, their countenance is still, their forehead is broad. They are chill like autumn, warm like spring; their joy and anger come in due season, fitting the things of the world, yet none knows their limit.

Thus, when the sage uses arms, they may destroy a state yet not lose the people’s hearts. Their beneficence extends to ten thousand generations, yet they do not do it out of love for others. To be all-pervading is not sageliness; to have partiality is not benevolence; to follow the seasons is not worthiness; to be unable to comprehend advantage and harm is not a gentleman; to act for fame is not a scholar; to lose oneself yet not be true is not to serve others.

Those like Hu Buxie, Wu Guang, Bo Yi, Shu Qi, Jizi, Xu Yu, and Shentu Di—these are servants of others’ service, followers of others’ following, yet do not follow their own following.

The true persons of old were upright yet not rigid, sufficient yet not complacent. They were angular yet not unyielding, open yet not ostentatious. They were cheerful as if pleased, solemn as if compelled. Their demeanor was expansive, their virtue restraining. They were stern as if worldly, lofty as if beyond control. They were connected as if fond of seclusion, absent-minded as if forgetful of their words.

They took punishment as their body, ritual as their wings, knowledge as their timing, and virtue as their guide. Taking punishment as their body, they were lenient in execution. Taking ritual as their wings, they moved through the world. Taking knowledge as their timing, they acted only when compelled by circumstances. Taking virtue as their guide, they spoke of reaching the hills with those who had feet, yet others truly thought them diligent in conduct.

Thus, their likes were one, their dislikes were one. Their oneness was one, their non-oneness was one. Their oneness followed Heaven as a companion; their non-oneness followed man as a companion. That Heaven and man do not overcome each other—this is what is called a true person.

Life and death are fate; they have the constancy of night and day—this is Heaven. What man cannot partake in is the nature of things. They take Heaven as their father and love it as their own body—how much more that which transcends it! Men take having a ruler as better than themselves and die for it—how much more that which is true!

When the spring dries up, fish find themselves together on dry land. They moisten each other with breath, wet each other with foam—better to forget each other in rivers and lakes. Rather than praising Yao and condemning Jie, it is better to forget both and be transformed by the Dao.

The great clod burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me with death. Thus, to make my life good is to make my death good.

Hiding a boat in a ravine or a mountain in a marsh—one may call it secure. Yet in the dead of night, a strong man may carry it away, and the unaware do not know. Hiding the great is fitting, yet there is still escape. But if one hides the world within the world, there is no escape—this is the great constancy of things.

Only those who receive the human form rejoice in it. Yet those who have the human form are among the myriad transformations that have no beginning—how boundless their joy! Thus, the sage wanders where things cannot escape and abides in all.

To be good at early death, good at old age, good at beginning, good at ending—people emulate this. How much more that on which the myriad things depend and which the one transformation awaits!

The Dao has essence and trustworthiness, yet is without action or form. It can be transmitted but not received, attained but not seen. It is rooted in itself, existed before Heaven and Earth, and has endured since antiquity. It gives spirit to ghosts and gods, gives birth to Heaven and Earth. It is before the Supreme Ultimate yet not high; it is below the Six Directions yet not deep. It was born before Heaven and Earth yet is not long-lasting; it is older than antiquity yet not aged.

Xiwei obtained it and lifted Heaven and Earth. Fuxi obtained it and embraced the Mother of Qi. The Northern Dipper obtained it and has never erred since antiquity. The sun and moon obtained it and have never ceased since antiquity. Kanpi obtained it and embraced Kunlun. Feng Yi obtained it and roamed the great rivers. Jianwu obtained it and dwelled on the great mountains. The Yellow Emperor obtained it and ascended to the cloudy heavens. Zhuanxu obtained it and dwelled in the Dark Palace. Yuqiang obtained it and stood at the North Pole.

The Queen Mother of the West obtained it and sat upon Shaoguang, her beginning unknown, her end unknown. Peng Zu obtained it and lived from the time of Youyu down to the Five Hegemons. Fu Yue obtained it and served as minister to King Wu, ruling all under Heaven, riding the Eastern Ridge and mounting the Tail of the Winnowing Basket to take his place among the ranks of stars.

Nanbo Zikui asked Nü Yu, “You are advanced in years, yet your complexion is like that of a child—how is this?” She replied, “I have heard the Dao.” Nanbo Zikui said, “Can the Dao be learned?” She said, “How could it be! You are not the right person. Now, Bolang Yi possesses the talent of a sage but not the Dao of a sage, while I possess the Dao of a sage but not the talent of a sage. I wished to teach him, hoping he might truly become a sage. If not, to impart the Dao of a sage to one with the talent of a sage would be easy. I still kept to it and taught him. For three days, I was able to put the world outside myself. Having put the world outside, I kept to it for seven days, and then was able to put things outside myself. Having put things outside, I kept to it for nine days, and then was able to put life outside myself. Having put life outside, I was then able to attain morning clarity. With morning clarity, I was then able to perceive the solitary. Perceiving the solitary, I was then able to transcend past and present. Transcending past and present, I was then able to enter the realm of neither death nor life. That which kills life does not die; that which gives life to life does not live. As a thing, it sends nothing off, welcomes nothing in; nothing is destroyed, nothing is not completed. Its name is *yingning*. *Yingning* is that which is completed through engagement.”

Nanbo Zikui said, “You alone have heard of this!” She said, “I heard it from the disciple of Fu Mo; the disciple of Fu Mo heard it from the grandson of Luo Song; the grandson of Luo Song heard it from Zhan Ming; Zhan Ming heard it from Nie Xu; Nie Xu heard it from Xu Yi; Xu Yi heard it from Yu Ou; Yu Ou heard it from Xuan Ming; Xuan Ming heard it from Can Liao; Can Liao heard it as doubt.”

Zisi, Ziyu, Zili, and Zilai spoke among themselves, saying, “Who can take nonbeing as the head, life as the spine, and death as the tailbone, and who knows that life, death, existence, and nonexistence are one body—we would befriend such a one.” The four looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in accord, and so became friends.

Soon after, Ziyu fell ill, and Zisi went to inquire after him. Ziyu said, “How wondrous is the Creator! It would make me like this—all crooked!”

His spine was bent like a bow, his back hunched into a hump, with five vital channels rising from it. His chin sank into his navel, his shoulders towered above his crown, and his neck’s growth pointed heavenward.

Though the yin and yang had fallen into disorder, his heart remained at ease, untroubled. He paced lightly to peer into a well and said, “Alas! The Creator would make me so twisted and bound!”

Zisi asked, “Do you resent it?”

“No, why should I? If by degrees my left arm were transformed into a rooster, I would use it to crow the hours of the night. If my right arm were turned into a crossbow, I would use it to hunt for roasted owl. If my tailbone became a wheel and my spirit a horse, I would ride it—why ever change mounts? Moreover, to gain is timely; to lose is to follow the flow. At peace with the time and in accord with the flow, sorrow and joy cannot enter. This is what the ancients called ‘suspended release,’ yet those who cannot release themselves are bound by things. Besides, things have long been unable to overcome Heaven—what should I resent?”

Soon after, Zilai fell ill, gasping as if near death. His wife and children gathered around, weeping. Zisi went to see him and cried, “Away! Begone! Do not disturb the transformation!” Leaning against the doorframe, he spoke to Zilai: “How wondrous is the Creator! What will it make of you next? Where will it send you? Will it make you a rat’s liver? A bug’s leg?”

Zilai said, “A child follows the commands of its parents, whether east, south, west, or north. The yin and yang are no less to a person than parents. If they press me toward death and I refuse, I would be stubborn—what fault is theirs? The Great Clod burdens me with form, labors me with life, eases me with old age, and rests me in death. Thus, to treat my life well is to treat my death well. Now, if a great smith were casting metal and the metal leapt up, saying, ‘I must become a *Mo Ye* sword!’ the smith would surely deem it ill-omened metal. If one who has taken human form were to cry, ‘A human! A human!’ the Creator would surely deem it an ill-omened person. Now, if we take Heaven and Earth as a great furnace and the Creator as the great smith, where could one go and not be acceptable?” With that, he fell into a deep sleep and suddenly awoke.

Zisang Hu, Mengzi Fan, and Ziqin Zhang became friends, saying to one another, “Who can join together without joining, act without acting? Who can ascend to heaven and wander in the mists, stirring the boundless, forgetting life and never reaching an end?” The three looked at one another and smiled, their hearts in accord, and so became friends.

After some time, Zisang Hu died, and before he was buried, Confucius heard of it and sent Zigong to assist with the rites. One wove ropes, another strummed the zither, and together they sang in harmony: “Alas, Zisang Hu! Alas, Zisang Hu! You have returned to your true nature, while we still remain as humans!”

Zigong hurried forward and said, “May I ask if it is proper to sing before the corpse?”

The two looked at each other and smiled. “What does he know of the meaning of ritual?”

Zigong returned and reported to Confucius, saying, “What sort of men are they? They cultivate no conduct, disregard their physical forms, sing before the corpse without changing countenance—there is no name for them. What sort of men are they?”

Confucius said, “They wander beyond the realm of ordinary ways, while I wander within it. The inner and outer do not meet, and for me to send you to offer condolences was shallow indeed! They are about to become companions of the Creator, wandering in the single qi of Heaven and Earth. To them, life is an excrescence, a hanging tumor; death is the bursting of an abscess. How could such as they know where life and death, before and after, reside? Borrowing from different things, they entrust themselves to the same body, forgetting their livers and gallbladders, neglecting their ears and eyes. They cycle through beginnings and ends, unaware of their origins or limits, wandering in a vast blur beyond the dust and grime, at ease in the work of non-action. How could they be muddled by worldly customs to perform for the eyes and ears of the crowd?”

Zigong said, “Then what Dao do you follow?”

Confucius said, “I am one whom Heaven has punished. Nevertheless, I share it with you.”

Zigong said, “May I ask what that means?”

Confucius said, “Fish are made for water, just as humans are made for the Dao. Those made for water thrive by digging ponds to sustain themselves; those made for the Dao find peace without effort. Thus it is said: ‘Fish forget one another in rivers and lakes; humans forget one another in the arts of the Dao.’”

Zigong said, “May I ask about the *qi*-person?”

Confucius said, “The *qi*-person is one who is odd among humans but equal to Heaven. Thus it is said: ‘Heaven’s petty person is humanity’s noble one; humanity’s child is Heaven’s petty person.’”

Yan Hui asked Confucius, “When Mengsun Cai’s mother died, he wept without tears, his heart was not grieved, and he did not mourn. Yet for these three, he was praised throughout Lu for his mastery of mourning—could it be that one can gain such a reputation without substance? I find it strange.”

Confucius said, “Mengsun has advanced in understanding. He has simplified what cannot be simplified, yet he has already achieved some simplification. Mengsun does not know why he lives or why he dies, does not know what to pursue first or last. If he is transformed into something else, he awaits the transformation he does not know! Moreover, as he is about to transform, how could he not transform? As he is about to cease transforming, how could he know he has already transformed? You and I are but dreamers who have not yet awakened! Though his form may be startled, his heart remains unharmed; though his dwelling changes, his essence does not die.”

Mengsun alone has awakened—when others weep, he weeps as well, for this is simply his nature.

Moreover, we merely accompany one another in this; how could you know what I mean by "I"? You dream of being a bird and soaring through the heavens, dream of being a fish and diving into the depths. Who can tell whether the speaker now is awake or still dreaming? One reaches joy before laughter comes, offers laughter before arranging it—resting in arrangement, one departs into transformation, entering the vastness of the singular sky.

Yi’erzi went to see Xu You. Xu You asked, “What did Yao provide you?” Yi’erzi replied, “Yao told me: ‘You must personally embody benevolence and righteousness, and clearly distinguish right from wrong.’” Xu You said, “Then why have you come here? Yao has already branded you with benevolence and righteousness, and mutilated you with right and wrong. How will you wander the path of carefree drifting, uninhibited roaming, and ceaseless transformation?” Yi’erzi said, “Even so, I wish to wander within the fence.” Xu You said, “Not so. The blind cannot appreciate the beauty of brows and eyes, the blind musician cannot perceive the patterns of blue and yellow embroidery.”

Yi’erzi said, “Wu Zhuang lost her beauty, Ju Liang lost his strength, the Yellow Emperor lost his wisdom—all within the span of smelting and forging.
