How do we know the Creator will not cease my branding and mend my mutilation, allowing me to ride completion and follow you, Master?” Xu You said, “Alas! It cannot be known. Let me tell you the general principle: My teacher! My teacher!”

"To bestow upon ten thousand yet claim no righteousness, to benefit all generations yet claim no benevolence, to endure since high antiquity yet claim no seniority, to encompass heaven and earth and carve all forms yet claim no skill—this is the realm in which I wander."

Yan Hui said, "I have made progress."
Confucius said, "What do you mean?"
Yan Hui replied, "I have forgotten benevolence and righteousness."
Confucius said, "Good, but not yet sufficient."

Another day, they met again, and Yan Hui said, "I have made further progress."
Confucius asked, "What do you mean?"
Yan Hui replied, "I have forgotten ritual and music."
Confucius said, "Good, but still not sufficient."

On yet another day, they met again, and Yan Hui said, "I have advanced!"
Confucius asked, "What do you mean?"
Yan Hui replied, "I sit in forgetfulness."
Confucius started and said, "What is sitting in forgetfulness?"
Yan Hui answered, "To cast off the body, discard wisdom and clarity, depart from form and abandon knowledge, and merge with the Great Thoroughfare—this is called sitting in forgetfulness."
Confucius said, "To be without preferences is to be in accord; to transform without fixity is to be free. You are indeed worthy! I shall follow after you."

Yan Yu and Zi Sang were friends. After ten days of continuous rain, Yan Yu said, "Zi Sang must be in dire straits!" He wrapped up some rice and went to bring him food. At Zi Sang’s door, he heard sounds like singing, like weeping, and the strumming of a zither: *"Father! Mother! Heaven! Man!"* Yet his voice could not sustain the strain, and he hurriedly recited verses instead.

Yan Yu entered and asked, "Why do you sing and recite like this?"

Zi Sang replied, "I pondered who brought me to this extremity, but I could not find the answer. Would my parents wish me to be poor? Heaven covers all without partiality; Earth bears all without favoritism. Would Heaven and Earth single me out for poverty? I sought the one responsible, but I could not find them! Yet here I am at this extremity—it must be fate!"

---

*Book Three, Part Two, Chapter Seven: "Fit for Emperors"*

Nie Que asked Wang Ni four questions, and four times Wang Ni replied that he did not know. Nie Que leapt up in great joy and went to tell Master Pu Yi.

Pu Yi said, "So now you understand? The Lord of Yu falls short of the Lord of Tai. The Lord of Yu concealed his benevolence to entice men; he gained men’s allegiance, yet he had not yet transcended the realm of the unenlightened. The Lord of Tai slept in tranquil ease, awoke in carefree openness; at times he took himself for a horse, at times for an ox. His knowledge was true and sincere, his nature unspoiled, and he had never yet entered the realm of the unenlightened."

Jian Wu visited the madman Jie Yu.

Jie Yu the Madman asked, "What did Zhongshi of Noon-tide tell you?"
Jian Wu replied, "He instructed me on how to rule as a sovereign: issue decrees from one’s own authority, establish standards of propriety and measures of righteousness—who among the people would dare not heed and be transformed?"

Jie Yu said, "This is mere *virtue*—in governing the world, it is like crossing the sea by digging a river or making a mosquito bear a mountain. Does the sage’s governance concern externals? He simply rectifies himself and then acts, ensuring his affairs are firmly grounded in his capacity. Birds fly high to evade the harm of snares and arrows; the field mouse burrows deep beneath the sacred mound to escape the peril of fumigation and excavation—yet how could these two creatures be called ignorant?"

Tian Gen wandered to Yin-yang and arrived at the banks of the Liao River, where he encountered an unnamed man and asked, "I beg to inquire how to govern the world."
The man said, "Away with you! You are a vulgar man—why do you ask such an untimely question? I am about to become human with the Creator of Things. When weary, I will ride the nebulous bird to soar beyond the six directions and wander in the land of Nothing Whatever, dwelling in the boundless wilds. Why would you trouble my mind with governing the world?"

Tian Gen asked again.
The unnamed man said, "Let your mind wander in simplicity, harmonize your *qi* in stillness, follow things as they are without imposing private bias—and the world will be governed."

Yang Ziju visited Lao Dan and said, "Suppose there were a man here, swift and strong, penetrating and clear-sighted, tireless in studying the Dao. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?"
Lao Dan said, "In the eyes of the sage, such a man is like a petty clerk bound by skills, exhausting his body and startling his mind. Moreover, the patterned hides of tigers and leopards attract hunters; the agility of apes and the cunning of foxes and dogs bring them to be leashed. Could such a one be compared to an enlightened king?"

Yang Ziju, startled, said, "May I ask how an enlightened king governs?"
Lao Dan said, "The governance of an enlightened king: his achievements cover the world yet are not claimed as his own; he transforms and bestows upon the ten thousand things, yet the people do not rely on him. He achieves without being named, allowing things to rejoice in themselves. He stands in the unfathomable and wanders in the realm of Nothing."

In Zheng there was a divine diviner named Ji Xian, who knew of people’s death and life, survival and demise, fortune and misfortune, long life and early death, predicting them by the year, month, ten-day period, or day as if by spirit. When the people of Zheng saw him, they all fled in haste. Liezi saw him and was enchanted in his heart. Returning home, he told Master Hu, saying, "At first I took your Dao as the ultimate, but now there is something even more ultimate!"

Master Hu said, "I have shown you only its outward form, not yet its true substance. How could you have truly attained the Dao? A flock of hens without a rooster—how could they produce eggs? You take the Dao and contend with the world, and so you are inevitably believed. That is why others can see through you. Try bringing him here, and let me show myself to him."

The next day, Liezi brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu. After leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, "Alas! Your master is dead! He will not live! It will not be counted in days! I saw something strange—like damp ashes."

Liezi entered and wept, his tears soaking his lapels, as he told Master Hu. Master Hu said, "Earlier I showed him the pattern of earth: sprouting yet neither stirring nor upright. This is nearly seeing my closed virtue-mechanism. Bring him again."

The next day, he again brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu. After leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, "Fortunate that your master met me! He is recovering! He is fully alive! I see his closed power-mechanism!"

Liezi entered and told Master Hu. Master Hu said, "Earlier I showed him the realm of heaven and earth: names and realities did not enter, yet the mechanism arose from the heels. This is nearly seeing my mechanism of excellence. Bring him again."

The next day, he brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu. After leaving, Ji Xian said to Liezi, "Your master is unsettled; I cannot divine for him. Try to settle him, and I will divine again."

Liezi entered and told Master Hu. Master Hu said, "Earlier I showed him the state of supreme harmony where nothing prevails. This is nearly seeing my balanced qi-mechanism. The whirlpool formed by the turning of the *ni* fish, the whirlpool formed by still water, the whirlpool formed by flowing water—there are nine names for whirlpools, and I showed him three. Bring him again."

The next day, he again brought Ji Xian to see Master Hu. Before he could stand firmly, Ji Xian lost himself and fled. Master Hu said, "Pursue him!" Liezi chased after him but could not catch up. Returning, he reported to Master Hu, "He has vanished! He is gone! I could not reach him."

Master Hu said, "Earlier I showed him what had never yet been my origin. I met him with emptiness and compliance, so that he knew not who or what I was. Thus he took me for drifting grass, for flowing waves—hence his flight."

After this, Liezi considered himself as one who had never yet begun to learn and returned home. For three years he did not go out. He cooked for his wife, fed the pigs as if feeding people, and took no part in affairs. Carving and polishing returned to simplicity; he stood alone like an unworked clod. Entangled yet sealed off, he ended thus. He became no one’s representative for fame, no repository of schemes, no bearer of tasks, no master of knowledge. His body exhausted the boundless, and he wandered without trace. What he received from heaven, he did not regard as gain—it was emptiness alone!

The heart-mind of the perfected person is like a mirror: it does not send forth nor welcome, responds yet does not store, and so can overcome things without harm.

The emperor of the Southern Sea was Shu, the emperor of the Northern Sea was Hu, and the emperor of the Central Realm was Hundun. Shu and Hu often met in Hundun’s land, and Hundun treated them very well. Shu and Hu plotted to repay Hundun’s virtue, saying, "All people have seven openings for seeing, hearing, eating, and breathing—only this one lacks them. Let us bore them for him." Each day they bored one opening, and on the seventh day, Hundun died.

Joined toes and extra fingers—do they arise from nature? Yet they exceed in virtue. Hanging tumors and dangling warts—do they arise from form? Yet they exceed in nature. Those who square themselves with benevolence and righteousness and apply them—do they array them among the five viscera? Yet this is not the uprightness of the Dao and virtue.

Thus, joined toes are but connected useless flesh; extra fingers are but branches of useless digits. Those who multiply methods and join branches to the passions of the five viscera indulge in perverse conduct through benevolence and righteousness, and multiply their cleverness and brightness. Thus, those joined in brightness disorder the five colors, indulge in ornate patterns, and dazzle with blue and yellow, embroidered and figured robes—is this not Li Zhu? Those excessive in hearing disorder the five notes, indulge in the six pitch-pipes, and resound with bells, stones, strings, and bamboo, with *huangzhong* and *dalü*—is this not Shi Kuang? Those who branch into benevolence pluck at virtue and block nature to gather fame, causing the world to drum and blow in service of unattainable laws—is this not Zeng and Shi? Those joined in disputation pile tiles and knot cords, weave phrases, and roam their minds among the hard and white, the same and different—are not their half-step praises empty words? Are these not Yang and Mo? Thus, all these are ways of excessive joining and branching—none are the utmost uprightness of the world.

That which is truly upright does not lose the genuine condition of its nature and life. Therefore, what is conjoined is not called joined, and what is branched is not called extended; what is long is not called excessive, and what is short is not called insufficient. Thus, though the teal’s legs are short, to lengthen them brings sorrow; though the crane’s legs are long, to cut them brings grief. Therefore, what is long by nature is not to be cut, and what is short by nature is not to be lengthened—there is nothing to remove sorrow.

Do benevolence and righteousness truly not accord with human nature? Why then are those benevolent people so full of worries? Moreover, joined toes, when split, weep; extra fingers, when bitten, wail. These two—one exceeds in number, the other falls short—yet their sorrow is the same. The benevolent of this age gaze in distress at the world’s calamities; the unbenevolent sever the passions of nature and glut themselves on wealth and nobility. Thus, do benevolence and righteousness truly not accord with human nature?

Since the time below the Three Dynasties, why has the world been so clamorous? Those who wait for hooks, cords, compasses, and squares to be upright shave away their nature; those who bind fast with glue and lacquer encroach upon their virtue. Bending and folding with rites and music, cooing and soothing with benevolence and righteousness to comfort the world’s heart—this is its constant state. The world has its constant state: what is constant is that the curved does not rely on hooks, the straight does not rely on cords, the round does not rely on compasses, the square does not rely on squares, attachment does not rely on glue and lacquer, and restraint does not rely on ropes. Thus, the world is enticed and all things live, yet do not know how they live; all obtain, yet do not know how they obtain. Therefore, from ancient times to now, there is no duality—it cannot be diminished.

Then why do benevolence and righteousness cling like glue and lacquer, ropes and cords, roaming between the Dao and virtue? They confuse the world! Minor delusions alter direction; great delusions alter nature. How do we know this? When the House of Yu summoned benevolence and righteousness to vex the world, none failed to rush to serve benevolence and righteousness. Was this not using benevolence and righteousness to alter their nature?

Let us attempt to discuss this: since the time below the Three Dynasties, none have not used things to alter their nature! Petty men sacrifice themselves for profit; scholars sacrifice themselves for fame; great officers sacrifice themselves for their houses; sages sacrifice themselves for the world. Thus, though these several types differ in their undertakings and their reputations bear different names, in harming their nature and sacrificing themselves, they are one.

Zang and Gu together herded sheep, and both lost them. Asked what Zang was doing, he held a bamboo slip and read books; asked what Gu was doing, he played at dice and gambled. Though their undertakings differed, in losing their sheep they were the same. Boyi died for fame beneath Shouyang; Robber Zhi died for profit upon Dongling. Though their deaths differed, in injuring their lives and harming their natures they were the same. Why must Boyi be right and Robber Zhi wrong? The world is entirely given to sacrifice.

Those who sacrifice themselves for benevolence and righteousness are commonly called gentlemen; those who sacrifice themselves for wealth and property are commonly called petty men. Though their sacrifices are one, there are gentlemen and petty men among them. But in injuring their lives and harming their natures, Robber Zhi is no different from Boyi—how then can one distinguish gentlemen and petty men among them?

Those who bind their nature to benevolence and righteousness, though as accomplished as Zeng and Shi, are not what I call good; those who bind their nature to the five flavors, though as accomplished as Yu Er, are not what I call good; those who bind their nature to the five notes, though as accomplished as Shi Kuang, are not what I call keen; those who bind their nature to the five colors, though as accomplished as Li Zhu, are not what I call bright.

What I call good is not what is meant by benevolence and righteousness—it is being good in one’s virtue alone. What I call good is not what is meant by benevolence and righteousness—it is following the genuine condition of one’s nature and life. What I call keen is not hearing others—it is hearing oneself. What I call bright is not seeing others—it is seeing oneself.

Those who do not see themselves but see others, who do not obtain for themselves but obtain for others, obtain others’ obtainments yet do not obtain their own obtainments, suit others’ suits yet do not suit their own suits. Those who suit others’ suits yet do not suit their own suits—though it be Zhi or Boyi—are equally perverse and deviant. I am ashamed before the Dao and virtue, and thus above I dare not practice the conduct of benevolence and righteousness, and below I dare not engage in perverse and deviant acts.

**Chapter Nine: "Horse’s Hooves"**

A horse’s hooves can tread frost and snow; its hair can ward off wind and cold.

Gnawing grass and drinking water, lifting its hooves to gallop—this is the nature of the horse. Though there be halls of righteousness and chambers of state, they are of no use to it. Yet when Bo Le came and said, “I am skilled in managing horses,” he burned them, scraped them, carved them, and branded them. He bound them with halters and fetters, penned them in blackened stalls—of these horses, twelve or thirteen in every ten died. He starved them, parched them, drove them at a gallop, urged them to speed, aligned them, and made them uniform. Before them lay the peril of bit and ornament, behind them the threat of whip and goad—of these horses, more than half perished!

The potter says, “I am skilled in managing clay. The round fit the compass, the square the carpenter’s square.” The craftsman says, “I am skilled in managing wood. The curved fit the hook, the straight the marking-line.” But is it the nature of clay and wood to desire to fit compass, square, hook, and line? Yet generation after generation they praise them, saying, “Bo Le is skilled in managing horses, the potter and craftsman are skilled in managing clay and wood.” This is also the error of those who govern the world.

I suppose those who are truly skilled in governing the world would not act thus. The people have their constant nature: they weave to clothe themselves, till to feed themselves—this is called shared virtue. They are one and without faction, and this is called “Heaven’s release.” Thus in the age of utmost virtue, their movements were unhurried, their gazes unwavering. In that time, mountains had no paths or tunnels, marshes no boats or bridges. The myriad things thrived in flocks, linked to their native places. Beasts formed herds, grasses and trees flourished. Thus birds and beasts could be led by tether and wandered with, the nests of magpies and crows could be scaled and peered into. In the age of utmost virtue, people dwelt together with birds and beasts, their kind mingled with the myriad things. How would they know of gentlemen and petty men? Alike in knowledge, their virtue did not depart; alike in desirelessness, this was called “plain simplicity.” In plain simplicity, the people’s nature was preserved.

Yet when the sage came, he limped and strained for benevolence, stamped and struggled for righteousness—and the world began to doubt. He spread extravagance as music, plucked and pranced as ritual—and the world began to divide. Thus if plain simplicity is not shattered, who would make sacrificial vessels? If white jade is not broken, who would carve scepters and tablets? If virtue is not abandoned, why take up benevolence and righteousness? If nature and feelings are not estranged, why employ ritual and music? If the five colors are not confused, who would fashion patterned embroidery? If the five notes are not disordered, who would tune them to the six pitches? Shattering simplicity to make vessels is the fault of the craftsman; destroying the Dao and virtue to establish benevolence is the error of the sage.

The horse, when at rest, eats grass and drinks water; when pleased, it nuzzles necks together; when angered, it turns its back and kicks. The horse’s knowledge extends only so far! Yet when you impose a crossbar and yoke, align it with moon-shaped blinkers, the horse learns to glare, gnash, resist the bit, and steal the reins. Thus the horse’s knowledge, able to reach the point of theft, is Bo Le’s crime.

In the time of He Xu, the people dwelt without knowing what to do, walked without knowing where to go. They chewed their food in joy, drummed their bellies in play. The people were capable of this! Yet when the sage came, bending and twisting ritual and music to correct the world’s form, dangling benevolence and righteousness to soothe the world’s heart, the people began to stretch and strain for knowledge, to contend and rush toward profit—it could not be stopped. This, too, is the sage’s error.

To guard against thieves who pry open chests, rifle through bags, and break into coffers, one must bind them with cords, fasten them with ropes, and secure them with bolts and locks—this is what the common world knows. Yet when the great thief comes, he shoulders the coffer, hoists the chest, and carries off the bag, fearing only that the cords, ropes, bolts, and locks are not strong enough. Thus what was once called wisdom is but the accumulation for the great thief.

Let us examine this: is there any so-called wisdom in the common world that does not accumulate for the great thief? Is there any so-called sage who does not guard for the great thief? How do we know this is so? In ancient times, the state of Qi had villages so close that the crowing of cocks and barking of dogs could be heard from one to the next. The land where nets and traps were set, where plows and hoes struck the earth, spanned over two thousand *li*. Within its four borders, all that was established—ancestral temples and altars of soil and grain, the governance of cities, districts, hamlets, and lanes—was it not all modeled after the sages? Yet Tian Chengzi killed the ruler of Qi in a single day and stole the state. What he stole was not merely the state—he also took the sages’ wisdom and laws. Thus Tian Chengzi bore the name of thief and bandit yet lived in the security of Yao and Shun; small states dared not condemn him, and great states dared not punish him. For twelve generations, he held Qi. Was this not stealing the state while also seizing the sages’ wisdom and laws to guard his thieving self?

Let us examine further: is there any so-called supreme wisdom in the common world that does not accumulate for the great thief? Is there any so-called supreme sage who does not guard for the great thief? How do we know this is so? In ancient times, Long Feng was disemboweled, Bi Gan was carved open, Chang Hong’s corpse was exposed, and Wu Zixu was torn apart. Thus even the worthiness of these four could not spare them from execution. The followers of Robber Zhi asked him, “Does theft also have a Dao?” Zhi replied, “Where is there anything that does not have a Dao? To guess the treasures hidden in a room is sagacity; to enter first is courage; to withdraw last is righteousness; to know what can and cannot be done is wisdom; to divide spoils equally is benevolence. Without these five, no one in the world has ever become a great thief.” From this we see: the good man cannot stand without the Dao of the sage, and Robber Zhi cannot act without the Dao of the sage. Since the good are few and the ungood many in the world, the sage benefits the world little but harms it much. Thus it is said: when the lips are gone, the teeth grow cold; when the wine of Lu was weak, Handan was besieged; when sages arise, great thieves also rise. Strike down the sages, release the thieves and bandits, and the world will begin to be ordered.

When rivers run dry, valleys empty; when hills are leveled, deep pools fill. When sages die, great thieves will not arise, and the world will be at peace without incident. As long as sages do not die, great thieves will not cease. To exalt sages in governing the world is to heap profit upon Robber Zhi. Fashion measuring cups to gauge volume, and he will steal the cups along with what they measure; fashion scales to weigh, and he will steal the scales along with what they weigh; fashion tallies and seals to authenticate, and he will steal the tallies and seals along with what they verify; fashion benevolence and righteousness to correct, and he will steal benevolence and righteousness along with what they rectify. How do we know this is so? He who steals a hook is executed; he who steals a state becomes a feudal lord. At the gates of feudal lords, benevolence and righteousness abide. Is this not the theft of benevolence, righteousness, sagacity, and wisdom? Thus those who chase after great thieves, who exalt feudal lords, who covet the profits of benevolence, righteousness, measuring cups, scales, tallies, and seals—even with the rewards of high rank they cannot be encouraged, nor with the threat of the executioner’s axe can they be restrained. This heaping of profit upon Robber Zhi, making him unstoppable, is the fault of the sages.

Thus it is said: “Fish must not leave the deep; the state’s sharpest tools must not be shown to others.” The sage is the state’s sharpest tool, not what should be displayed to the world. Therefore, cut off sagacity and cast aside wisdom, and great thieves will cease; smash jade and shatter pearls, and petty thieves will not arise; burn tallies and break seals, and the people will return to simplicity; smash measuring cups and snap scales, and the people will not contend; annihilate the sages’ laws of the world, and the people will at last be fit for discussion. Uproot the six pitch-pipes, melt the *yu* and *se*, deafen the ears of Music Master Kuang, and the world will begin to preserve its hearing; obliterate patterns and scatter the five colors, blind the eyes of Li Zhu, and the world will begin to preserve its sight; destroy hooks and discard compasses and squares, cripple the fingers of Master Chui, and the world will begin to possess its skill. Thus it is said: “Great skill appears clumsy.” Eradicate the conduct of Zeng and Shi, silence the mouths of Yang and Mo, cast aside benevolence and righteousness, and the world’s virtue will begin to achieve mysterious unity.

Those who preserve their sight will not be dazzled by the world; those who preserve their hearing will not be burdened by the world; those who preserve their wisdom will not be deluded by the world; those who preserve their virtue will not be perverse in the world. Zeng, Yang, Mo, Music Master Kuang, Master Chui, and Li Zhu all externally established their virtue to dazzle and confuse the world—useless under the law.

Do you alone not know of the age of utmost virtue? In the times of Rong Cheng, Da Ting, Bo Huang, Zhong Yang, Li Lu, Li Xu, Xuan Yuan, He Xu, Zun Lu, Zhu Rong, Fu Xi, and Shen Nong, the people knotted cords for use. They savored their food, admired their clothing, delighted in their customs, and dwelt content in their homes. Neighboring states were within sight, the sounds of cocks and dogs carried between them, yet the people grew old and died without ever traveling. In such an age, there was utmost governance. Now, however, the people crane their necks and stand on tiptoe, saying, “In such-and-such a place there is a worthy,” and they carry provisions to rush there, abandoning their parents at home and neglecting their lords’ affairs abroad. Their footprints crisscross the borders of feudal lords, and their cart tracks weave a thousand *li*.

This is the fault of those above who delight in knowledge. If those above truly favor knowledge while lacking the Dao, then the world will fall into great chaos. How do we know this is so? When the cunning of bows, crossbows, snares, and traps multiplies, birds are thrown into chaos above; when the craft of hooks, baits, nets, weirs, and fish traps proliferates, fish are thrown into chaos in the water; when the devices of snares, fences, nets, and traps abound, beasts are thrown into chaos in the marshes. When the shifts of cunning deceit, gradual poison, tangled sophistry, the "hard and white" debate, and the distinctions of "dirt and sameness" multiply, customs are thrown into chaos by disputation.

Thus, the world time and again falls into great disorder, and the blame lies in the love of knowledge. The world knows only to seek what it does not know yet fails to seek what it already knows; it knows only to condemn what it does not deem good yet fails to condemn what it already deems good. Hence the great chaos. Above, it confounds the brightness of sun and moon; below, it scatters the essence of mountains and rivers; in between, it disrupts the operations of the four seasons. Even the timid insects and delicate creatures—none retain their nature. How severe is the chaos wrought by the love of knowledge upon the world! This has been the case since the Three Dynasties and after: abandoning the subtle mechanisms of the myriad things, they delight instead in the toiling flattery of the obsequious. Forsaking the tranquil and effortless, they take pleasure in the clamorous clamor of disputation—clamor that has already thrown the world into chaos!

**Chapter 11: "In Yu" (Letting Be and Granting Freedom)**
I have heard of letting the world be and granting it freedom; I have never heard of governing the world. To let it be is to fear that the world will corrupt its nature; to grant it freedom is to fear that the world will alter its virtue. If the world neither corrupts its nature nor alters its virtue, why would one seek to possess it?

In the past, when Yao governed the world, he made the world joyful, each delighting in their nature—yet this was not tranquility. When Jie governed the world, he made the world weary, each suffering in their nature—yet this was not contentment. Neither the absence of tranquility nor the absence of contentment is virtue. There has never been a case where what is not virtue could endure.

Does one become greatly joyful? It leans toward yang. Does one become greatly angry? It leans toward yin. When yin and yang are both excessive, the four seasons fail to arrive, the harmony of cold and heat is lost, and it harms the human form. It causes people’s joy and anger to lose their proper place, their dwellings to lack constancy, their thoughts to find no ease, and their ways to become entangled mid-course. Thereupon, the world begins to grow haughty, perverse, and defiant, and only then do the deeds of Robber Zhi, Zeng, and Shi arise.

Thus, if one were to reward all the good in the world, it would not suffice; if one were to punish all the evil in the world, it would not be enough. Therefore, the vastness of the world is insufficient for rewards and punishments. Yet since the Three Dynasties and after, they have clamored endlessly over rewards and punishments—when would they have leisure to settle into the true essence of their natures and lives?

Moreover, do they delight in beauty? This is an indulgence in form. Do they delight in keen hearing? This is an indulgence in sound. Do they delight in benevolence? This disrupts virtue. Do they delight in righteousness? This perverts principle. Do they delight in ritual? This is mutual artifice. Do they delight in music? This is mutual indulgence. Do they delight in sagacity? This is mutual fault-finding. Do they delight in knowledge? This is mutual nitpicking.

If the world seeks to preserve the true essence of its nature and life, these eight may exist or vanish—it matters not. But if the world does not preserve the true essence of its nature and life, these eight begin to carve and hack at it, throwing the world into chaos. And yet the world exalts and cherishes them—how deluded the world has become! Is it merely a matter of recognizing their errors and abandoning them? They warn with words, kneel to present them, and drum and sing to accompany them with dance. What am I to make of this?

Thus, when the noble person is compelled to oversee the world, there is nothing better than non-action. Only through non-action can the true essence of nature and life be preserved. Therefore, one who values their own person over governing the world may be entrusted with the world; one who cherishes their own person over governing the world may be charged with the world.

Thus, if the noble person can refrain from unsettling their five viscera and from flaunting their keen hearing and sight; if they dwell like a corpse yet manifest like a dragon, thunder like thunder, move like a spirit with heaven following, and act with ease and non-action—then the myriad things will be steamed and piled up of their own accord. What leisure would I have to govern the world?

Cui Qu asked Lao Dan, “If one does not govern the world, how can the hearts of the people be settled?” Lao Dan replied, “Be careful not to agitate the human heart. The heart presses downward yet strives upward; above and below, it is imprisoned and slain. Soft and yielding, it overcomes the hard and strong; sharp and incisive, it carves and polishes. Its heat is scorching fire; its cold, congealed ice. In the space between a glance and a breath, it twice traverses beyond the four seas. At rest, it is deep and still; in motion, it soars to heaven. Proud and unbridled, nothing can be bound—is this not the human heart alone?

In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor first used benevolence and righteousness to agitate the human heart. Yao and Shun then wore away the flesh of their thighs and the hair of their shins to nourish the forms of the world; they exhausted their five viscera for benevolence and righteousness, strained their blood and qi to model laws and standards. Yet they still could not prevail. Thus, Yao banished Huan Dou to Mount Chong, exiled the Three Miao to Sanwei, and banished Gong Gong to Youdu—this was their failure to overcome the world. When this spread to the Three Kings, the world was greatly alarmed. Below, there were Jie and Robber Zhi, Zeng and Shi, while the Confucians and Mohists all arose. Thereupon, joy and anger suspected one another, the foolish and the wise deceived one another, the good and the bad condemned one another, the truthful and the deceitful mocked one another—and virtue declined. Great power diverged, and the nature and life of the people scattered and dissolved. The world loved knowledge, and the common people were utterly exhausted.

Thereupon, axes and saws were fashioned, the plumb line was used to kill, and hammers and chisels were employed to decide. The world was thrown into great disorder, and the blame lay in agitating the human heart. Thus, the worthy hid away beneath the cliffs of great mountains, while the lords of ten thousand chariots trembled with anxiety in their ancestral halls.

In the present age, those condemned to death lie heaped upon one another, those in cangues and stocks jostle one another, and those subjected to corporal punishment are within sight of one another—yet the Confucians and Mohists still stretch on tiptoe and wave their arms amidst the fetters and shackles. Alas, how extreme it has become!

How extreme is their shamelessness in feeling no shame! I know not whether sagely wisdom is not but the crossbeam and pegs of a cangue, whether benevolence and righteousness are not but the shackles and chisel-holes of fetters—how then can one know that Zeng and Shi are not the first arrows of Jie and Robber Zhi! Thus it is said: Cut off sageliness, cast aside wisdom, and the world will be greatly ordered.

After the Yellow Emperor had reigned as Son of Heaven for nineteen years, his decrees circulated throughout the world. Hearing that Guangchengzi dwelt on Mount Kongtong, he went to see him, saying: “I have heard that you, Master, have attained the utmost Dao. I venture to ask: what is the essence of the utmost Dao? I wish to draw forth the essence of Heaven and Earth to aid the five grains and nourish the common people. Moreover, I wish to govern yin and yang to bring all living things to fruition. How may this be done?”

Guangchengzi replied: “What you wish to ask concerns the substance of things; what you wish to govern concerns the remnants of things. Since you began to rule the world, clouds have rained without gathering, plants and trees have withered before yellowing, and the light of the sun has grown ever more dim. With the heart of a flattering sycophant—how could one such as you be fit to speak of the utmost Dao?”

The Yellow Emperor withdrew, relinquished the world, built a solitary chamber, spread a mat of white couch-grass, and dwelt in seclusion for three months. Then he went again to seek Guangchengzi. Guangchengzi lay with his head to the south; the Yellow Emperor approached from the lower side of the wind, crawling on his knees. He bowed, touching his head to the ground, and asked: “I have heard that you, Master, have attained the utmost Dao. I venture to ask: how may one cultivate the body so as to attain longevity?”

Guangchengzi sprang up and said: “Excellent, your question! Come, and I shall tell you of the utmost Dao.”

The essence of the utmost Dao is profound and obscure;
Its ultimate reaches are dim and silent.
See nothing, hear nothing, embrace the spirit in stillness—
Then the body will right itself.
Be still, be pure; do not weary your form,
Do not agitate your essence, and you may attain long life.
Let the eyes see nothing, the ears hear nothing,
The heart know nothing—then your spirit will guard you,
And your form will endure.
Guard your inner, be cautious of your outer;
Excessive knowledge brings ruin.
For you, I have ascended to the great brightness,
To the source of utmost yang;
For you, I have entered the gate of profound obscurity,
To the source of utmost yin.
Heaven and earth have their offices,
Yin and yang have their treasuries.
Guard your body with care, and things will naturally flourish.
I preserve this and dwell in harmony.
Thus have I cultivated my body for one thousand two hundred years,
Yet my form has never decayed.”

The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, and said:
“Guangchengzi is truly heaven!”

Guangchengzi said: “Come! I shall tell you:
Those things are boundless, yet men deem them finite;
Those things are unfathomable, yet men deem them limited.
One who attains my Dao may become a sovereign above or a king below;
One who loses the Dao may see light above yet become earth below.
Now all the myriad beings are born from earth and return to earth.
Therefore, I shall leave you and enter the gate of the boundless,
To wander the wilds of the limitless.
I share the radiance of the sun and moon,
I endure with heaven and earth.
Approach me, and all is clear!
Withdraw from me, and all is dark!
Men may all perish, yet I alone endure!”

Cloud General was traveling east when he passed the branch of the whirlwind tree and happened upon Hong Meng. Hong Meng was just then slapping his sides, leaping and frolicking in play. Cloud General saw him, halted in surprise, and stood in astonishment, saying: “Old sir, who are you? What are you doing here?”

Hong Meng, still slapping his sides and leaping without pause, replied: “Wandering!”

Cloud General said: “I wish to ask you something.”

Hong Meng looked up at Cloud General and said: “Ah!”

Cloud General said: “Heaven is not harmonious, earth’s qi is pent up, the six qi are unbalanced, the four seasons are out of order. Now I wish to blend the essence of the six qi to nurture the multitude of beings. How may this be done?”

Hong Meng, still slapping his sides and leaping, shook his head and said: “I do not know! I do not know!”

Cloud General could not press his question. Three years later, he was wandering again when he passed through the wilds of Song and once more happened upon Hong Meng. Cloud General rejoiced greatly, hastened forward, and said: “Has heaven forgotten me? Has heaven forgotten me?” He bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, and wished to hear Hong Meng’s teaching.

Hong Meng said: “Drifting without aim, knowing not what is sought;
Rampant without direction, knowing not where to go;
Restless and distracted, observing what is not false.”

“What do I know!” said Cloud General. “I, too, thought myself reckless, yet the people follow wherever I go. I cannot be free of them—now they are the ones who have gone astray! I wish to hear a single word.”

Hong Meng said: “To disorder heaven’s warp, to go against the nature of things—even the dark and boundless heavens cannot be brought to completion. To scatter the herds of beasts so that birds cry all night; calamity reaches the grasses and trees, disaster befalls even the still insects. Alas! This is the fault of those who govern men.”

Cloud General said: “Then what am I to do?”

Hong Meng said: “Alas! How poisonous! Off you go, lightly, lightly, back to where you belong.”

Cloud General said: “I have encountered great difficulty from heaven. I wish to hear a single word.”

Hong Meng said: “Alas! Nurture the heart-mind. You need only abide in non-action, and things will transform of themselves. Let your form dissolve, your hearing and sight fade, and forget both human relations and things. Merge utterly with the primal vastness. Unbind the heart-mind, release the spirit, and be empty, without soul. The ten thousand things, drifting like clouds, each return to their root, each return to their root yet know it not. In utter chaos and undifferentiation, you will never depart from it all your life. If you claim to know it, you have already departed from it. Do not ask its name, do not seek its nature—things will then arise of themselves.”

Cloud General said: “Heaven has bestowed virtue upon me and shown me silence. Through cultivating myself, I have now attained it.” He bowed twice, touching his head to the ground, then rose and took his leave.

The common run of men all delight in others being like themselves and detest others differing from themselves. Those who wish others to be like themselves and do not wish them to differ do so with the intent of standing out from the crowd. Yet how can one who takes standing out from the crowd as their aim ever truly stand out? Resting content with the crowd, what they hear cannot surpass the skills of the many.

Those who would rule others’ states grasp at the advantages of the Three Kings yet fail to grasp their troubles. This is to gamble with others’ states. How many such gambles end without the ruin of those states? In preserving others’ states, they succeed not one in ten thousand times; in ruining them, one failure brings ten thousand losses. How lamentable that those who hold land do not understand!

Land is a great thing. Those who possess great things cannot be possessed by things; only by not being things can one master things. To understand that the master of things is not a thing—how could this apply merely to governing the world’s people? Moving in and out of the Six Directions, roaming the Nine Provinces, coming and going alone—this is called “possessing alone.” Those who possess this are called “most noble.”

The teaching of the great person is like the form to its shadow, the sound to its echo. Questioned, they respond, exhausting what lies in their heart-mind, acting for the world. They dwell where there is no echo, move where there is no direction. They lead you to the bustling hub of return, wandering without end; entering and exiting without sides, with the sun having no beginning. In discussing the body, they merge with the Great Sameness, the Great Sameness without self. Without self, how could there be possession? Those who see possession are the gentlemen of old; those who see non-possession are the heavens and earth.

That which is lowly yet cannot be shunned is things; that which is humble yet cannot be disregarded is the people; that which is hidden yet cannot be left undone is affairs; that which is coarse yet cannot be left unpresented is law; that which is distant yet cannot be left unoccupied is rightness; that which is close yet cannot be left unexpanded is benevolence; that which is measured yet cannot be accumulated is ritual; that which is centered yet cannot be left unexalted is virtue; that which is one yet cannot be left unchanged is the Dao; that which cannot be left undone is heaven.

Thus the sage observes heaven yet does not assist, completes virtue yet is not burdened, emerges from the Dao yet does not scheme, meets benevolence yet does not rely, is sparing in action yet does not accumulate, responds to ritual yet does not conceal, engages with affairs yet does not refuse, aligns with law yet is not disordered, depends on the people yet does not belittle, and attends to things yet does not abandon them. Things are never worth acting for, yet cannot be left unacted for. Those who do not understand heaven are not pure in virtue; those who do not penetrate the Dao have no ground to stand on; those who do not understand the Dao—how lamentable!

What is the Dao? There is the Dao of heaven and the Dao of man. That which is honored through non-action is the Dao; that which is burdened through action is the human Dao. The ruler follows the Dao of heaven; the minister follows the Dao of man. The distance between the Dao of heaven and the Dao of man is vast—it cannot go unexamined.

Though heaven and earth are great, their transformations are uniform; though the ten thousand things are many, their governance is one; though the masses of people are numerous, their lord is the ruler. The ruler’s virtue is perfected by heaven. Thus it is said: The ancient rulers of the world practiced non-action, and that was all—they embodied the virtue of heaven.

Viewing words through the Dao, the world’s rulers are rectified; viewing distinctions, the righteousness of ruler and minister is clarified; viewing abilities through the Dao, the world’s offices are well-ordered; viewing broadly through the Dao, the responses of the ten thousand things are complete. Thus that which penetrates all is virtue; that which moves through the ten thousand things is the Dao; that which governs people above is affairs; that which involves skill is technique. Technique is encompassed by affairs, affairs by rightness, rightness by virtue, virtue by the Dao, and the Dao by heaven.

Thus it is said: The ancients who nurtured the world had no desires, and the world was sufficient; the ten thousand things transformed, profound and still, and the hundred clans were settled. The *Record* says: “Penetrate the One, and the ten thousand affairs are complete; without intention, one attains it, and even ghosts and spirits submit.”

The Master said: “The Dao—it covers and bears the ten thousand things, vast and boundless! The noble person cannot but hollow out their heart-mind for it. Acting through action is called heaven; speaking through non-action is called virtue; loving people and benefiting things is called benevolence; uniting differences in sameness is called greatness; acting without extreme distinctions is called breadth; possessing the ten thousand differences is called wealth.

Thus holding to virtue is called the guiding principle; completing virtue is called establishment; following the Dao is called fulfillment; not allowing things to thwart one’s will is called wholeness. If you understand these ten, then your mind’s greatness will be profound, and you will flow with the ten thousand things like a surging flood. If so, you will hide gold in the mountains and pearls in the depths, not prizing goods or wealth, not drawing near to nobility or riches; not delighting in long life, not grieving over early death; not exalting success, not ashamed of poverty.”

One does not confine the world’s benefits to one’s private share, nor regard ruling all under Heaven as one’s own exalted position. Exaltation is clarity. The ten thousand things are a single treasury; death and life share the same form.

The Master said: “The Dao—how abysmal its dwelling, how limpid its purity! Without metal or stone, there would be no sound. Thus metal has voice, yet without striking, it does not ring. Who can determine the ten thousand things?

The person of royal virtue moves plainly yet feels shame at entanglement in affairs, establishes the root and origin yet understands their pervasiveness—such virtue is vast. When the heart issues forth, things gather to it. Thus form does not arise without the Dao, and life is not illuminated without virtue. To preserve form and exhaust life, to establish virtue and clarify illumination—is this not the person of royal virtue?

Vast and boundless! Suddenly emerging, suddenly stirring, and the ten thousand things follow—this is the person of royal virtue. Seeing into darkness, listening to silence: within the deepest obscurity, alone one perceives the dawn; within utter silence, alone one hears harmony. Thus the deeper the depth, the more it engenders things; the more divine the divinity, the more it refines essence. Therefore, in its encounter with the ten thousand things, it meets their needs with utmost nothingness, gallops with the seasons to grasp their lodging, embracing the small and great, the short and long, the near and far.”

The Yellow Emperor roamed north of the Red Waters, ascended the heights of Mount Kunlun and gazed south. Upon returning, he lost his Dark Pearl. He sent Knowledge to seek it, but it was not found; he sent Li Zhu to seek it, but it was not found; he sent Chi Gou to seek it, but it was not found. Then he sent Image-Net, and Image-Net found it. The Yellow Emperor said: “Strange indeed! Could only Image-Net obtain it?”

Yao’s teacher was Xu You; Xu You’s teacher was Nie Que; Nie Que’s teacher was Wang Ni; Wang Ni’s teacher was Pi Yi. Yao asked Xu You: “Could Nie Que be fit to match Heaven? I would rely on Wang Ni to assess him.” Xu You said: “Perilous indeed is the state of the world! Nie Que’s nature—keen of hearing, clear of sight, quick-witted and sharp, his nature surpasses others, yet he still takes man as his measure of Heaven. He scrutinizes prohibitions against transgression yet does not know whence transgression arises. Could he be matched with Heaven? He rides upon man yet lacks Heaven. He is about to take the body as root yet differentiate forms, about to exalt knowledge and race like fire, about to be driven by threads, about to be ensnared by things, about to look in all four directions and have things respond, about to respond to every fitting occasion, about to transform with things yet never begin with constancy. How could he be fit to match Heaven?

Nevertheless, he has kin, has ancestors; he may serve as father to the multitude, yet cannot serve as the father of fathers. Governance is the harbinger of chaos, the calamity of facing north, the bane of facing south.” Yao observed the Hua region.

The Hua border guard exclaimed, "Ah, sage! Permit me to invoke blessings upon you: may the sage enjoy longevity." Yao replied, "I decline." "May the sage grow wealthy." Yao replied, "I decline." "May the sage have many sons." Yao said, "I decline." The border guard asked, "Longevity, wealth, and many sons—these are what all men desire. Why do you alone not desire them?" Yao answered, "Many sons bring many fears; wealth brings many affairs; longevity brings many humiliations. These three are not the means to nurture virtue, so I decline."

The border guard said, "At first, I took you for a sage, but now you are merely a gentleman. Heaven generates the myriad people and must assign them their duties. If you have many sons and assign them duties, what fear is there? If you are wealthy and distribute it among others, what affairs remain? The sage dwells like a quail and eats like a fledgling, moving like a bird without leaving a trace. When the world follows the Dao, all things flourish together; when the world loses the Dao, he cultivates virtue and retreats to leisure. After a thousand years, weary of the world, he departs to become an immortal, riding the white clouds to the realm of the Emperor. The three calamities never reach him, and his person remains free from harm—what humiliation is there?"

The border guard departed, and Yao followed, saying, "May I ask a question?" The man replied, "Withdraw! This is not your concern."

When Yao governed the world, Bo Cheng Zi Gao was enfeoffed as a lord. Yao ceded the throne to Shun, and Shun to Yu, but Bo Cheng Zi Gao resigned his lordship to till the soil. Yu went to see him and found him plowing in the fields. Yu hastened to stand downwind and posed his question, saying, "In the past, when Yao governed the world, you served as a lord. Yao ceded the throne to Shun, and Shun to me, yet you resigned your lordship to plow. May I ask the reason?"

Bo Cheng Zi Gao replied, "In the past, when Yao governed the world, he did not reward yet the people were encouraged; he did not punish yet the people stood in awe. Now you reward and punish, and the people grow ever less humane. Virtue declines from this, punishments arise from this, and the disorders of later generations begin from this! Why do you linger here? Do not interfere with my work!"

He plowed on, absorbed and unheeding.

In the Great Beginning there was Nonbeing—nonbeing that had no name. The One arose from this, existing yet still formless. When things received this to come into being, it was called their *virtue*; when forms took on distinction yet remained seamless and unbroken, it was called their *fate*; when the ceaseless flux gave rise to things, and things attained their patterns of growth, it was called their *form*; when form and spirit were preserved together, each with its proper measure, it was called their *nature*. Cultivating this nature, one returns to *virtue*; when *virtue* reaches its fullness, one merges with the Beginning. This merging is emptiness; emptiness is vastness. Then the beaks join in song; when the beaks join in song, they harmonize with Heaven and Earth. This harmony is profound and subtle, like stupidity or confusion—this is called *mysterious virtue*, merging with the Great Compliance.

Confucius asked Lao Dan, “There is a man who governs the Way as if he were contradicting himself—what is permissible he deems impermissible, what is so he deems not so. A debater once said, ‘Separate hardness and whiteness as if they were suspended in space.’ Can such a one be called a sage?”

Lao Dan replied, “This is the sort of petty clerk whose skills ensnare him, whose body is wearied and whose mind is startled. Such a one grasps at fixed thoughts like a monkey caught in a trap—his agility comes from the mountains and forests. Qiu, let me tell you what you cannot hear and what you cannot speak. Of those with heads and feet but no heart or ears, there are many; of those with form and those without form or shape who all endure, there are none. Their movement is rest; their death is life; their decay is renewal—yet these are not their true source. There is a governance that lies with man: forget things, forget Heaven, and it is called forgetting oneself. The one who forgets himself is said to enter into Heaven.”

Jiang Lümian went to see Ji Che and said, “The Lord of Lu told me, ‘I beg to receive your instruction.’ I could not refuse his command and have already told him what I know—whether it is right or not, I do not know. Let me try to present it to you. I said to the Lord of Lu, ‘You must practice reverence and frugality, uproot partiality and favoritism, and promote those who are publicly loyal—then what people will not be harmonious?’”

Ji Che burst into hearty laughter and said, “If these are your words, then for the virtue of emperors and kings, it is like a mantis raising its arms to block an oncoming chariot—how could it bear the task! Moreover, if it were so, then such a one would place himself in peril, and his observation tower would attract many who come to tread in his footsteps.”

Jiang Lümian started in alarm and said, “I am utterly lost in what you have said! Nevertheless, I beg you to speak of its guiding principles.”

Ji Che said, “When the great sage governs the world, he sways and stirs the hearts of the people, shaping them into teachings and transforming their customs. He eradicates their predatory inclinations and advances their solitary aspirations. He acts thus of his own accord, yet the people remain unaware of how it comes to be. Is this not far removed from the way Yao and Shun instructed the people, treating them as younger kin in a boundless expanse? They sought to align with virtue, and their hearts dwelt in stillness.”

Zigong traveled south to Chu and, on his return to Jin, passed through Hanyin. There he saw an old man preparing his garden beds. The man dug a tunnel to enter the well, drew water in a jar, and labored intensely with little result. Zigong said, “There is a device that can irrigate a hundred beds in a day, requiring little effort yet yielding great results. Would you not wish to use it?” The gardener looked up and asked, “How does it work?” Zigong replied, “Carve wood into a mechanism—heavy at the front, light at the back—drawing water as if pulling, swift as boiling water. It is called a *gao* (well-sweep).” The gardener flushed with anger, laughed, and said, “I have heard from my teacher that those who possess machines will have mechanical affairs, and those who engage in mechanical affairs will harbor a mechanical heart. A mechanical heart dwelling in the breast disrupts the purity of whiteness; when purity is incomplete, the spirit is unsettled. One whose spirit is unsettled cannot be borne by the Dao. I am not ignorant of such things—I am ashamed to do them.” Zigong, abashed, bowed his head in response.

After a moment, the gardener asked, “What are you doing here?” Zigong replied, “I am a disciple of Confucius.” The gardener said, “Are you not one of those who amass learning to rival the sages, strut about to overshadow the masses, and pluck a single string to sing mournful songs, all to peddle your reputation throughout the world? You are about to forget your spirit and qi, cast off your bodily form, and only then might you approach the Way! If you cannot even govern yourself, how can you presume to govern the world? Be on your way and do not hinder my work.” Zigong’s countenance fell, and he walked away ill at ease. Only after thirty *li* did he recover.

His disciples asked, “Who was that man just now? Why did you lose composure before him and remain unsettled for the rest of the day?” Zigong replied, “At first, I thought there was only one true person in the world—I did not know there was another like him. I have heard from the Master that in affairs, one seeks what is feasible; in achievements, one seeks completion. To expend little effort and achieve much is the Way of the sage. Yet this is not so. One who holds to the Dao attains wholeness in virtue; wholeness in virtue brings wholeness in form; wholeness in form brings wholeness in spirit. Wholeness in spirit is the Way of the sage. Such a one entrusts life to the people, moving alongside them without knowing where they go, vast and complete! Those who pursue profit, skill, and cunning must forget the heart of such a person. For one like him, nothing but his own will moves him, and nothing but his own heart compels him to act. Even if the world were offered to him, if it accorded with his principles, he would remain aloof; if the world rejected him, if it contradicted his principles, he would remain unmoved. The world’s praise or blame adds nothing to him—this is what it means to be a person of complete virtue! I am but a wanderer tossed by the winds and waves.”

Returning to Lu, Zigong reported this to Confucius. Confucius said, “That man was but a dabbler in the arts of the Hun clan. He grasps one aspect but ignores the rest; he cultivates his inner self but neglects the outer.”

Those who attain clarity and return to simplicity, who practice non-action and embrace the spirit, wandering amidst the world’s customs—would you truly be so astonished? As for the arts of the Hun Dun clan, how could you or I presume to comprehend them?

Zhun Mang was traveling east toward the Great Abyss when he encountered Yuan Feng on the shores of the Eastern Sea. Yuan Feng asked, “Where are you going?” Zhun Mang replied, “To the Great Abyss.” “What will you do there?” “The Great Abyss is such a thing—pour into it and it never fills, draw from it and it never empties. I will wander there.” Yuan Feng said, “Do you have no concern for the people of the cross-eyed world? I wish to hear of sagely governance.” Zhun Mang said, “Sagely? The sage appoints officials without error, promotes the capable without oversight, fully perceives the circumstances and acts accordingly. His words arise from himself, yet the world is transformed. With a wave of his hand, the people of the four directions all come—this is what sagely governance means.”

“I wish to hear of the virtuous person.” “The virtuous person dwells without thought or worry, harbors no judgments of right or wrong, beauty or ugliness. When all within the four seas share in benefit, this is called joy; when all are provided for, this is called peace. He is forlorn as an infant who has lost its mother, bewildered as a traveler who has lost his way. Wealth and resources are abundant, yet he does not know their source; food and drink suffice, yet he does not know their origin—this is the bearing of the virtuous person.”

“I wish to hear of the spirit person.” “The supreme spirit person rides the light, and with the vanishing of form, this is called illuminating the vast. He fulfills his mandate and exhausts his nature; heaven and earth rejoice, and the myriad affairs dissolve. The ten thousand things return to their true nature—this is called primordial obscurity.”

Men Wu Gui and Chi Zhang Manji observed the armies of King Wu. Chi Zhang Manji said, “He does not measure up to the Yu clan—hence this calamity!” Wu Gui said, “Was the world well-governed and then the Yu clan governed it? Or was it in chaos and then they brought order?” Chi Zhang Manji replied, “A well-governed world is the wish—why calculate the Yu clan’s role! The Yu clan’s remedies are like treating sores: shaving the head to apply medicine, seeking help only when ill. A filial son administers medicine to his compassionate father, his face scorched with worry—this the sage finds shameful.

In an age of utmost virtue, worthies are not exalted, abilities are not employed. Superiors are like branches, the people like wild deer. They are upright yet do not consider it righteousness, they love one another yet do not consider it benevolence, they are sincere yet do not consider it loyalty, they are trustworthy yet do not consider it faithfulness. They move and work together without thought of reward. Thus their actions leave no traces, their affairs no records.”

A filial son does not flatter his parents; a loyal minister does not fawn upon his lord—this is the height of ministerial and filial conduct. If he agrees with his parents’ words and approves their good deeds, the world calls him an unworthy son. If he agrees with his lord’s words and approves his good deeds, the world calls him an unworthy minister. Yet how can one be certain this is necessarily so? When the world calls something right and one agrees, or calls something good and one approves, they do not call him a flatterer! Does custom then revere parents and honor lords more strictly?

When one calls another ‘enlightened,’ they bristle with anger; when one calls another a flatterer, they flush with indignation. Yet they spend their lives calling others ‘enlightened,’ spend their lives being called so, gathering crowds with polished words and parables—from beginning to end, root and branch, they never reflect. They drape themselves in robes, adorn themselves with colors, alter their expressions to ingratiate themselves with the world, yet do not call themselves flatterers. Associating with such people, debating right and wrong, yet not considering themselves common—this is the height of folly.

To know one’s folly is great folly; to know one’s confusion is not to be free of confusion. The greatly confused never awaken; the greatly foolish remain foolish all their lives. If three walk together and one is confused, the destination may still be reached, for the confused are few. If two are confused, the effort is laborious and the goal unattained, for the confused prevail. Now, with the whole world confused, though I seek a response, how can it be obtained? Is this not lamentable?

Grand music does not enter the ears of the village; Yang Zhu and Mo Di are met with scornful laughter. Thus lofty words do not lodge in the hearts of the common; ultimate words are unspoken, for vulgar words prevail. With two clanging bells, the path is lost—how much more so with the whole world confused! Though I seek a response, how can it be obtained? To know it is possible and yet force it is yet another confusion! Better to let go and not press. If one does not press, who shares the worry?

A man from Li, awakened at midnight to the birth of his child, hastily lit a torch to examine it, anxious only that it might resemble himself. A hundred-year-old tree, carved into a sacrificial vessel, painted with green and yellow patterns—its offcuts lie discarded in the ditch. Compared to the sacrificial vessel, the offcuts in the ditch differ in beauty and ugliness, yet in losing their nature they are the same. Robber Zhi and the sages Zeng and Shi differ in conduct, yet in losing their nature they are equal.

Now, the ways of losing one’s nature are five: first, the five colors confuse the eyes, making them blind; second, the five sounds confuse the ears, making them deaf; third, the five odors choke the nose, congesting the forehead; fourth, the five flavors dull the mouth, making it numb and tasteless; fifth, preferences and aversions agitate the heart, scattering the nature. These five are all harms to life. Yet Yang Zhu and Mo Di leap and prance, believing they have attained something—this is not what I call attainment.

If what is confined can be called attainment, then the owl in its cage has also attained. Moreover, chasing after sounds and colors to clutter the inner self, donning caps of skin and crane feathers, tucking tablets into sashes and straightening long robes to constrain the outer—this is not the Way.”

The inner self, clogged with entanglements, and the outer, bound by cords and snares—yet one glories in the snares, believing oneself to have attained. If so, then prisoners with arms pinioned and fingers dislocated, or tigers and leopards trapped in sacks and cages, could also be said to have attained!

**Chapter 13: The Dao of Heaven**

The Dao of Heaven moves without accumulation, and so the myriad things are completed. The Dao of the Emperor moves without accumulation, and so the world submits. The Dao of the Sage moves with accumulation, and so all within the seas obeys.

One who is enlightened by Heaven and attuned to the Sage, whose six senses and four limbs are aligned with the virtue of emperors and kings—such a one, in self-cultivation, is serene, with nothing unsettled.

The Sage’s stillness is not because stillness is good, and thus still; rather, with nothing in the myriad things to disturb the heart, stillness follows. When water is still, it clearly reflects beard and brows, and its level serves as a standard—even master craftsmen take it as their model. If still water is so luminous, how much more so the spirit! How still, then, is the Sage’s heart! It is the mirror of Heaven and Earth, the looking-glass of the myriad things.

For emptiness, stillness, tranquility, serenity, and non-action are the leveling of Heaven and Earth and the perfection of the Dao and its virtue. Thus do emperors, kings, and sages rest in them. Resting in them is emptiness; emptiness is fullness; fullness is order. Emptiness is stillness; stillness is movement; movement is attainment. Stillness is non-action; if there is action, then those who take up affairs bear responsibility. In non-action, there is ease. Ease means that cares and troubles cannot abide, and life is long.

Emptiness, stillness, tranquility, serenity, and non-action are the nurturing of the myriad things. Illuminating this and facing south, Yao became a ruler; illuminating this and facing north, Shun became a minister. Practicing this in high position, one becomes an emperor, king, or Son of Heaven; practicing this in low position, one follows the Dao of the obscure sage and plain king. Retiring to leisurely wander by rivers and seas, recluses of mountains and forests submit to it; advancing to serve the age, one’s achievements are great, one’s name illustrious, and the world is unified.

Still, one is a sage; in motion, a king. In non-action, one is revered; in simplicity, none in the world can rival one’s beauty.

One who clearly understands the virtue of Heaven and Earth—this is called the great root and great origin, one who harmonizes with Heaven. It is the means by which the world is balanced and harmonized with others. Harmonizing with others is called human joy; harmonizing with Heaven is called Heavenly joy.

Zhuangzi said: “My master! My master! You pulverize the myriad things yet are not cruel, your blessings extend to ten thousand generations yet you are not benevolent, you are older than high antiquity yet not long-lived, you cover and bear Heaven and Earth, carve and adorn the multitude yet are not skillful.” This is called Heavenly joy.

Thus it is said: “One who knows Heavenly joy moves with the Heavenly course in life and transforms with things in death. In stillness, one is one with the Yin; in motion, one is one with the Yang.” Therefore, one who knows Heavenly joy has no resentment from Heaven, no blame from others, no encumbrance from things, and no reproach from spirits.

Thus it is said: “In movement, one is Heaven; in stillness, Earth. With a single heart settled, one rules the world. Spirits do not haunt, the soul is not wearied, and with a single heart settled, the myriad things submit.” This means applying emptiness and stillness to penetrate Heaven and Earth and communicate with the myriad things—this is called Heavenly joy. Heavenly joy is the Sage’s heart, by which the world is nurtured.

The virtue of emperors and kings takes Heaven and Earth as its model, the Dao and its virtue as its master, and non-action as its constant. In non-action, one employs others and has abundance; in action, one is employed by the world and is insufficient. Thus did the ancients value non-action.

The ruler practices non-action, and the subjects also practice non-action, sharing the same virtue. When subjects share the same virtue as the ruler, they do not serve as ministers. When subjects act, and the ruler also acts, the ruler shares the same path as the subjects. When the ruler shares the path, he does not govern. The ruler must practice non-action and employ the world; the subjects must act and be employed by the world. This is the unchanging Dao.

Thus the kings of old, though their knowledge embraced Heaven and Earth, did not scheme for themselves; though their eloquence adorned the myriad things, they did not speak for themselves; though their abilities exhausted all within the seas, they did not act for themselves. Heaven transforms the myriad things, Earth nurtures them without growing, and emperors and kings achieve success through non-action.

Thus it is said: “None is more divine than Heaven, none richer than Earth, none greater than emperors and kings.” Thus it is said: “The virtue of emperors and kings matches Heaven and Earth.” This is the Dao of riding Heaven and Earth, driving the myriad things, and employing the human multitude.

The root lies above, the branches below; the essential lies with the ruler, the details with the ministers. The deployment of the three armies and five weapons is a branch of virtue; rewards and punishments, advantages and harms, the application of punishments—these are branches of governance. Ritual, laws, measures, and regulations, the comparison and verification of forms and names—these are branches of order. The sounds of bells and drums, the postures of feathered banners—these are branches of music. Weeping and wailing, mourning garments, the gradations of severity—these are branches of mourning. These five branches require the operation of spirit and the guidance of the heart’s art before they can be followed.

Branches were known to the ancients, but they were not what came first. The ruler leads, and the minister follows; the father leads, and the son follows; the elder brother leads, and the younger follows; the elder leads, and the younger follows; the man leads, and the woman follows. This precedence of honor and rank is the course of Heaven and Earth, and thus the Sage takes it as a model.

Heaven is honored, Earth is lowly—this is the position of the divine and bright. Spring and summer come first, autumn and winter follow—this is the sequence of the four seasons. The myriad transformations arise, each with its form; flourishing and decline, growth and decay—these are the currents of change. Though Heaven and Earth are most divine, they have a sequence of honor and precedence—how much more so the human Dao!

In ancestral temples, kinship is honored; in courts, rank is honored; in villages and districts, age is honored; in affairs, worthiness is honored—this is the sequence of the great Dao. To speak of the Dao without its sequence is not the Dao; to speak of the Dao and not follow the Dao—how can one grasp the Dao!

Thus, those of ancient times who illuminated the great Dao first illuminated Heaven, and only then did they order it with virtue and power. Once virtue and power were illuminated, benevolence and righteousness followed. Once benevolence and righteousness were illuminated, the division of duties followed. Once the division of duties was illuminated, forms and names followed. Once forms and names were illuminated, the assignment of tasks followed. Once the assignment of tasks was illuminated, the examination of origins followed. Once the examination of origins was illuminated, right and wrong followed. Once right and wrong were illuminated, rewards and punishments followed. Once rewards and punishments were illuminated, the foolish and the wise found their proper places, the noble and the base assumed their positions, the benevolent and the worthless revealed their natures, and each was assigned according to ability, following their names.

By this, one serves superiors; by this, one nurtures inferiors; by this, one governs things; by this, one cultivates the self. Scheming and calculation are set aside, and one must return to Heaven. This is called Great Peace, the ultimate of governance.

Thus, the ancient texts say: *"There are forms, there are names."* Forms and names existed among the ancients, but they were not primary. Those who spoke of the great Dao in ancient times could discuss forms and names only after five transformations, and rewards and punishments only after nine transformations. To rush to speak of names without knowing their root, or to rush to speak of rewards and punishments without knowing their beginning—this is to speak against the Dao, to expound contrary to the Way. Such a person may be governed, but how can they govern others? To rush to speak of forms, names, rewards, and punishments is to possess the tools of governance but not the Dao of governance. Such a one may be employed by the world, but cannot truly employ the world. This is called a disputatious scholar, a man of partial understanding.

Rites, laws, measures, and detailed comparisons of forms and names existed among the ancients. These are what inferiors use to serve superiors, not what superiors use to nurture inferiors.

Formerly, Shun asked Yao: *"How does the Son of Heaven employ his mind?"* Yao said: *"I do not disdain the helpless, nor abandon the destitute. I grieve for the dead, cherish the young, and pity women—this is how I employ my mind."* Shun said: *"It is fine, but not yet great."* Yao asked: *"Then what should I do?"* Shun said: *"Embody Heavenly virtue and emerge in tranquility. Sun and moon shine, the four seasons proceed—like the constancy of day and night, like clouds moving and rain falling."* Yao said: *"How entangled and troubled I am! You are in harmony with Heaven; I am merely in harmony with men."*

Heaven and Earth were what the ancients revered most, what the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun all praised together. Thus, how did the ancients rule all under Heaven? They simply followed Heaven and Earth.

Confucius traveled west to deposit his books in the Zhou archives. Zilu advised: *"I have heard that the Zhou’s Keeper of Archives has an elder named Lao Dan, who has retired and lives at home. If you wish to deposit your books, perhaps you should visit him."* Confucius said: *"Very well."* He went to see Lao Dan, but Lao Dan refused. Thereupon, Confucius expounded the Twelve Classics to persuade him. Lao Dan interrupted: *"This is too verbose. I would hear the essentials."* Confucius said: *"The essentials lie in benevolence and righteousness."* Lao Dan asked: *"Are benevolence and righteousness part of human nature?"* Confucius said: *"Indeed. Without benevolence, a lord cannot succeed; without righteousness, he cannot live. Benevolence and righteousness are the true nature of man—what more is needed?"* Lao Dan said: *"Tell me, what are benevolence and righteousness?"* Confucius said: *"To delight in the center of things, to love universally without partiality—this is the essence of benevolence and righteousness."* Lao Dan said: *"Alas, these are but later words! Universal love—is this not impractical? Without partiality—this is still partiality. If you wish to ensure that the world does not lose its shepherd, then know that Heaven and Earth already have their constancy, sun and moon already have their brightness, stars and constellations already have their order, beasts already have their herds, and trees already stand firm. You should follow virtue and act, wander freely and proceed—this is perfection. Why must you clamor about benevolence and righteousness like one beating a drum to seek a lost child? Alas, you confuse human nature!"*

Shi Chengqi came to see Laozi and said: *"I have heard that you are a sage. I did not hesitate to travel a great distance to see you, enduring a hundred blisters without rest. Now I observe you, and you are no sage! You leave surplus vegetables in the rat’s nest while abandoning your younger sister—this is not benevolence! Before you, raw and cooked foods are not exhausted, yet you hoard without limit."* Laozi remained silent and did not respond.

The next day, Shi Chengqi came again and said: *"Yesterday, I reproached you, but today my heart is at peace. Why is this?"* Laozi said: *"Those who are skillful, wise, spirit-like, and sagely—I consider myself free of such labels. Formerly, you called me an ox, and I accepted the name; you called me a horse, and I accepted the name. If one truly possesses the reality, yet refuses the name given by others, one invites misfortune. I wear my constant garb, and in this garb, I find my constancy."*

Shi Chengqi stepped aside to avoid his shadow, then advanced and asked: *"How ought one cultivate the self?"* Laozi said: *"Your countenance is haughty, your gaze piercing, your forehead protruding, your mouth gaping, your demeanor fierce—like a tethered horse held in check. Movement is restraint; action is mechanism. You scrutinize with cunning and perceive only excess. All this betrays a lack of trustworthiness. At the borders, there are those called thieves."*

Laozi continued: *"The Dao—it does not end in the great, nor does it abandon the small, so all things are complete within it. Vast and boundless, nothing does it not embrace; deep and unfathomable, it cannot be measured. Forms, virtue, benevolence, and righteousness are but the outermost branches of spirit—who but the perfected person can stabilize them?
