The perfected person possesses the world, yet how great is this! It is not enough to burden them. The world brandishes power, yet they do not join in its sway. They discern the unborrowed and do not shift with profit, attaining the truth of things and preserving their essence. Thus, they place heaven and earth outside themselves, leave the myriad things behind, and are never constrained. They penetrate the Dao, unite with virtue, harmonize with benevolence and righteousness, and submit to ritual and music—such is the settled mind of the perfected person!"*

What the world values as the Dao is but writing. Writing is no more than speech, and speech has its value. What is valued in speech is meaning, yet meaning follows what cannot be transmitted. What meaning follows cannot be conveyed in words, yet the world prizes words and transmits texts. Though the world values them, they are still not worthy of value—for what they value is not true value. What can be seen are forms and colors; what can be heard are names and sounds. Alas! The world takes forms, colors, names, and sounds as sufficient to grasp the essence of things. Yet if forms, colors, names, and sounds are truly insufficient to grasp that essence, then the knowing do not speak, and the speaking do not know—how could the world recognize this?

Duke Huan was reading in the hall when Wheelwright Bian, carving a wheel below, set down his mallet and chisel and ascended. The duke asked: *"May I ask what words you are reading?"* The duke replied: *"The words of sages."* Bian said: *"Are the sages still alive?"*

The duke said: *"They are long dead."*
Bian said: *"Then what Your Grace reads are but the dregs and husks of the ancients!"*

Duke Huan said: *"How dare a wheelwright pass judgment on what I read! If you have an explanation, speak; if not, you die!"*
Wheelwright Bian replied: *"Your servant observes this from his own craft. When carving a wheel, if the stroke is too slow, it is smooth but lacks firmness; if too swift, it is labored and fails to penetrate. Neither too slow nor too swift—it is grasped by the hand and responds to the heart, yet the mouth cannot express it. There is a rhythm that resides in the space between. I cannot make my son understand it, nor can my son receive it from me. Thus, though I am seventy and grow old carving wheels, the ancients and what they could not transmit have perished with them. Then what Your Grace reads are but the dregs and husks of the ancients!"*

**Chapter Fourteen: "Heaven’s Revolution" (from *Book Five, Part Two*)**

Does Heaven revolve? Does Earth revolve? Do the sun and moon contend for their places? Who directs this? Who binds it in order? Who propels and enacts it? Is there some mechanism that compels it, unable to cease? Or does it turn of itself, unable to halt? Do clouds make rain? Or does rain make clouds? Who sends forth this abundance? Who, dwelling in idleness, indulges in pleasure and thus urges it on? Winds rise from the north, now west, now east, drifting aloft in restless motion. Who exhales and inhales them? Who, dwelling in idleness, stirs and scatters them? I venture to ask: what is the reason for all this?

Wu Xian said: *"Come! Let me tell you."*

"Heaven possesses the Five Constants in their ultimate measure; when emperors and kings align with them, there is order, and when they oppose them, there is misfortune. The affairs of the Nine Cauldrons, when governance is perfected and virtue complete, serve as a mirror to illuminate the lands below, and the world bears them aloft—this is called the Supreme August."

The Grand Steward Dang of Shang asked Zhuangzi about *ren* (benevolence).
Zhuangzi said: "Tigers and wolves possess *ren*."
Dang asked: "What do you mean?"
Zhuangzi replied: "Father and son cherish one another—how is this not *ren*?"
Dang said: "May I ask about perfect *ren*?"
Zhuangzi said: "Perfect *ren* has no partiality."
The Grand Steward said: "I have heard that without partiality there is no love, and without love there is no filial piety. Do you mean that perfect *ren* is unfilial?"
Zhuangzi said: "Not so. Perfect *ren* is lofty indeed—filial piety is hardly sufficient to describe it. This is not a matter of surpassing filial piety, but of falling short of it. Consider one traveling south to Ying: though facing Mount Ming, they do not see it—why? Because it lies too far away. Thus it is said: Reverent filial piety is easy; loving filial piety is difficult. Loving filial piety is easy; forgetting is difficult. Forgetting one’s kin is easy; making one’s kin forget oneself is difficult. Making one’s kin forget oneself is easy; forgetting the world entirely is difficult. Forgetting the world entirely is easy; making the world forget oneself is difficult. The virtue of Yao and Shun was cast aside and not clung to; their beneficence spread across the age, yet the world took no notice—how could this be merely a sigh over *ren* and filial piety? Filial devotion, fraternal respect, *ren*, righteousness, loyalty, trustworthiness, integrity, and incorruptibility—these are all self-exertions that enslave one’s virtue, and are not worth exalting. Thus it is said: The most noble renders state rank obsolete; the most wealthy renders state wealth obsolete; the most aspiring renders fame obsolete. Only the Dao remains unchanging."

Beimen Cheng asked the Yellow Emperor, saying: "When Your Majesty performed the *Xianchi* music in the wilds of Dongting, at first I heard it and was afraid; hearing it again, I was bewildered; hearing it to the end, I was lost—adrift and silent, no longer myself."

The Emperor said: "You were indeed so! I performed it with man, harmonized it with Heaven, conducted it with ritual propriety, and established it with the Great Purity. The four seasons arise in succession, and the myriad things follow their birth; now flourishing, now declining, the patterns of civil and martial order unfold; now clear, now turbid, yin and yang blend in harmony, their radiance flowing in sound. When hibernating creatures first stir, I startle them with thunder; its end has no tail, its beginning no head; now dying, now living, now collapsing, now rising. What is constant knows no end, yet one cannot await its singular moment—this is why you were afraid.

I then performed it with the harmony of yin and yang, illumined it with the brightness of sun and moon. Its sounds could be short or long, soft or hard, transforming in unity, not bound to old constancy. Valleys fill valleys, pits fill pits; it seals crevices and guards the spirit, taking things as its measure. Its tones are expansive, its name lofty and bright. Thus do ghosts and spirits guard its depths, while sun, moon, and stars follow their courses. I halt it at the bounded, yet let it flow into the boundless. You wish to ponder it but cannot know it, to gaze upon it but cannot see it, to pursue it but cannot catch it—suddenly standing on the Way of the four voids, leaning on a withered catalpa and chanting. Your eyes fail before what you wish to see, your strength is exhausted before what you wish to pursue. I too can no longer reach it! Emptiness and stillness—only then does it come, winding and yielding. You, yielding, thus grow idle.

I then performed it with sounds of no idleness, attuned it to the mandate of nature. Thus it chases after thickets, forests rejoice yet have no form; it spreads and scatters without trailing, dark and dim yet soundless. Moving without direction, dwelling in profound obscurity—some call it life, some call it substance, some call it glory. Flowing, dispersing, shifting, it does not adhere to constant sound. The world doubts it, seeking verification from the sage. The sage penetrates to the truth of things and fulfills the mandate. The heavenly mechanism is not displayed, yet the five officials are all complete. This is called the music of Heaven, which delights the heart without words."

Thus did the Lord of Yan extol it, saying:
*"Hearing it, one does not hear its sound;
Seeing it, one does not see its form.
It fills Heaven and Earth,
Envelops the six directions."*
One wishes to listen but finds no point of contact—this is why you were bewildered.

Music begins with fear; fear gives rise to reverence. I then followed it with idleness; idleness gives rise to escape. Finally, it ends in confusion; confusion gives rise to foolishness. Foolishness gives rise to the Dao, and the Dao can be borne and journeyed with together."

Confucius journeyed west to Wei, and Yan Yuan asked Shi Jin, saying: "What do you make of our Master’s conduct?"

Shi Jin replied: "Alas! Your Master is doomed to failure!"

Yan Yuan said: "Why?"

Shi Jin said: "Before the straw dogs are set out for the sacrifice, they are stored in fine baskets, wrapped in embroidered cloth, and the invocator fasts and purifies himself to present them. But once they have been set out, passersby trample their heads and spines, and gatherers take them to burn as fuel. If one were to gather them again, place them in fine baskets, wrap them in embroidered cloth, and wander or rest beneath them, one would not dream—yet surely suffer frequent nightmares.

Now your Master has taken the straw dogs of the former kings, already set out, and gathers his disciples to wander and rest beneath them. Thus he was driven to fell a tree in Song, erase his tracks in Wei, and in Shang and Zhou—was this not his dream? Besieged between Chen and Cai, seven days without cooked food, life and death as neighbors—was this not his nightmare?

In water, nothing surpasses the use of a boat; on land, nothing surpasses the use of a cart. To take a boat, fit for water, and seek to push it on land is to spend a lifetime moving no more than a few paces. Are not past and present as different as water and land? Zhou and Lu as different as cart and boat? To insist on practicing Zhou’s ways in Lu is like pushing a boat on land—laborious yet fruitless, and one’s person will surely suffer harm. He does not understand the transmission without fixed method, responding to things without exhaustion.

Have you alone not seen the well-sweep? Pulled, it bows; released, it rises. What pulls it is not pulling *it*—thus it bows and rises without offending anyone.

The rites, righteousness, laws, and standards of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors were not prized for their sameness but for their efficacy in governance. Thus the rites, righteousness, laws, and standards of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors are like the crabapple, pear, orange, and pomelo—their flavors differ, yet all are palatable. Thus rites, righteousness, laws, and standards are things that change with the times.

If you take a monkey and dress it in the robes of the Duke of Zhou, it will bite, tear, and rend them, casting them off entirely before it is satisfied. To observe the differences between past and present is like seeing the difference between a monkey and the Duke of Zhou.

When Xishi was afflicted with heart pain and knit her brows in her village, the ugly women of her village saw it and thought it beautiful. Returning home, they too clutched their hearts and knit their brows. The rich men of the village, seeing this, barred their doors and refused to come out; others, seeing it, took their wives and children and fled. They knew that knitting the brows was beautiful but did not know *why* it was beautiful.

Alas! Your Master is doomed to failure!"

At fifty-one, Confucius had still not heard the Dao, so he journeyed south to Pei to see Lao Dan.

Lao Dan said: "Have you come? I am a worthy man of the north—have you attained the Dao?"

Confucius said: "Not yet."

Lao Dan said: "How have you sought it?"

"I sought it in measures and calculations, but after five years I had not attained it."

Lao Dan said: "How else have you sought it?"

"I sought it in yin and yang, but after twelve years I had still not attained it."

Lao Dan said: "Just so. If the Dao could be offered, there is no one who would not offer it to their lord; if it could be presented, there is no one who would not present it to their parents; if it could be told, there is no one who would not tell it to their brothers; if it could be given, there is no one who would not give it to their children and grandchildren. Yet it cannot—there is no other reason: within, there is no master to hold it, and without, no standard to guide it.

That which issues from within is not received from without—the sage does not go forth. That which enters from without finds no master within—the sage does not conceal it. Fame is a public vessel; one cannot take too much of it. Benevolence and righteousness are the temporary lodgings of the former kings—one may stay a single night but cannot dwell there long. To encounter them and demand much is to invite reproach.

The perfected people of old followed the Dao in benevolence, lodged in righteousness as a temporary abode, and roamed the wilds of carefree wandering. They ate from the fields of simplicity, stood in the gardens of non-transaction. Carefree wandering is non-action; simplicity is ease of nourishment; non-transaction is no expenditure. The ancients called this ‘the wandering of gathering authenticity.’

Those who take wealth as right can yield it; those who take prominence as right cannot yield fame."

Those who cling to power cannot share authority with others—grasping it, they tremble; relinquishing it, they grieve, yet remain utterly blind, fixated on what never ceases. These are the people Heaven condemns.

Resentment and favor, taking and giving, admonition and instruction, life and death—these eight are the instruments of governance. Only those who follow great change without obstruction can wield them. Thus it is said: "To govern is to rectify." If one’s heart does not accept this, the gate will not open.

Confucius visited Lao Dan and spoke of benevolence and righteousness. Lao Dan said: "When chaff gets in the eye, heaven and earth and the four directions seem to shift places; when a mosquito bites the skin, one cannot sleep all night. Benevolence and righteousness are just as grating—they agitate my heart, and no disorder is greater. You, sir, would have the world not lose its simplicity. Let me move like the wind and stand by virtue’s sum! Why must you strain so, like one carrying a great drum in search of a lost child?

The egret does not bathe daily yet remains white; the crow is black without dye. The simplicity of black and white is not worth debating; the spectacle of fame and reputation is not worth expanding. When the spring dries up, fish find themselves together on dry land, moistening one another with spittle—better to forget one another in rivers and lakes."

Confucius left Lao Dan and did not speak for three days.

The disciple asked, "Master, when you met Lao Dan, what counsel did you receive?"

Confucius said, "Only now have I truly seen the dragon! The dragon coalesces into form, disperses into pattern, rides the clouds and qi, and nourishes itself in yin and yang. My mouth gapes open, yet I cannot speak—what counsel could I offer Lao Dan?"

Zigong said, "Then are there indeed men who dwell like corpses yet manifest as dragons, rumble like thunder yet remain as silent as the abyss, and move like heaven and earth? Might I witness such a one?"

Thereupon, he announced Confucius’s name to Lao Dan. Lao Dan was just then reclining in his hall when he responded faintly, "My years have advanced and departed—what would you caution me about?"

Zigong said, "The governance of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors differed, yet their reputations were equally exalted. Why, then, do you alone deem them not sages?"

Lao Dan said, "Young man, come closer! Why do you say they differed?"

Zigong replied, "Yao abdicated to Shun, Shun to Yu. Yu relied on force, while Tang used arms. King Wen submitted to Zhou without daring to oppose him, and King Wu opposed Zhou without consenting to him—hence, they differed."

Lao Dan said, "Young man, come closer, and I will tell you of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors’ governance of the world. In the Yellow Emperor’s rule, the people’s hearts were unified; when their parents died, they did not weep, and none condemned them. Under Yao’s rule, he made the people’s hearts affectionate; when they killed their parents, none condemned them. Under Shun’s rule, he made the people’s hearts competitive; pregnant women bore children at ten months, and infants spoke at five months. Before they reached maturity, people began to die young. Under Yu’s rule, he made the people’s hearts mutable; men had their own minds, and weapons had their own order. Killing thieves was not considered killing—each acted for himself, and the world was thus. Therefore, the world was greatly alarmed, and the Confucians and Mohists arose. Their beginnings had some order, yet now, what can be said of women? I tell you, the governance of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors was called ‘order,’ yet no disorder was greater. The wisdom of the Three Sovereigns obscured the brightness of the sun and moon above, disrupted the essence of rivers below, and threw the seasons’ operations into chaos. Their knowledge was as cruel as the sting of scorpions and centipedes, as the fangs of venomous beasts. None could preserve their nature and destiny in tranquility, yet they still called themselves sages—is this not shameful? Truly, they knew no shame!"

Zigong stood uneasily, shifting his feet. Confucius said to Lao Dan, "I have studied the Six Classics—*The Odes*, *The Documents*, *The Rites*, *The Music*, *The Changes*, and *The Spring and Autumn*—for a long time and have thoroughly grasped their principles. I have advised seventy-two rulers, expounding the Way of the former kings and clarifying the traces of the Zhou and Shao, yet not one ruler has adopted them. How difficult it is to persuade others! Is the Dao so hard to illuminate?"

Lao Dan said, "Fortunate indeed that you have not encountered a ruler who governs! The Six Classics are but the worn traces of the former kings—how could they be the true source of those traces? What you speak of are merely traces. Traces are what the foot leaves behind—how could the foot itself be the trace? The white egrets gaze at one another, their pupils unmoving, yet the wind transforms them. Insects, the male chirping upwind, the female responding downwind, are transformed. Each species has its own male and female, and thus the wind transforms them. Nature cannot be altered, destiny cannot be changed, time cannot be halted, and the Dao cannot be obstructed. If one attains the Dao, nothing is impossible; if one loses it, nothing is possible."

Confucius did not go out for three months, then saw Laozi again and said, "I have attained it! The crows and magpies rear their young, fish scatter foam, wasps transform the slender-waisted, and when a younger brother is born, the elder weeps. For so long, I have not been one with transformation as a human! If one is not one with transformation as a human, how can one transform others?" Laozi said, "Good! You have grasped it, Qiu!"

---

**Chapter Fifteen: "Cultivating the Mind"**

Those who cultivate the mind and esteem conduct, distancing themselves from the world and differing from common customs, who engage in lofty discourse and resentful slander, do so merely to be aloof. These are the scholars of mountain valleys, not the people of the world—what those who wither and plunge into the abyss delight in.

Those who speak of benevolence and righteousness, loyalty and trust, reverence and frugality, deference and yielding, do so to cultivate themselves. These are the scholars of a peaceful age, the teachers and instructors, what those who wander and dwell in learning delight in.

Those who speak of great achievements, establishing great names, honoring ruler and minister, rectifying superior and inferior, do so merely to govern. These are the scholars of the court, the men who honor their lord and strengthen their state, what those who seek merit and annex territory delight in.

Those who approach marshes, dwell in leisure and openness, fish and idle, do so merely to be without action. These are the scholars of rivers and seas, those who avoid the world, what the leisurely delight in.

Those who blow and exhale, expel the old and draw in the new, imitate the bear’s climbing and the bird’s stretching, do so merely to prolong life. These are the practitioners of Daoist arts, those who nurture the body, what those who venerate longevity delight in.

But those who are lofty without cultivating the mind, cultivated without benevolence and righteousness, governed without merit and fame, at leisure without rivers and seas, long-lived without Daoist arts—nothing is not forgotten, yet nothing is not possessed. Serene and boundless, all beauties follow. This is the Dao of Heaven and Earth, the virtue of the sage.

Thus it is said: That which is tranquil, still, silent, and empty, without action—this is the equilibrium of Heaven and Earth and the essence of the Dao and virtue.

Thus it is said: When the sage rests in ease, he is level and yielding. Being level and yielding, he is tranquil and indifferent. Being level, yielding, tranquil, and indifferent, worries and misfortunes cannot enter, and perverse qi cannot assail. Thus his virtue is whole and his spirit is not diminished.

Thus it is said: The sage’s life follows the movement of Heaven; his death is the transformation of things. At rest, he shares the virtue of yin; in motion, he moves with the wave of yang. He does not precede fortune, nor initiate calamity. He responds only after being moved, acts only under compulsion, and rises only when there is no alternative. Abandoning knowledge and contrivance, he follows the principle of Heaven.

Thus he suffers no heavenly disasters, no encumbrances from things, no human reproach, no blame from spirits. His life is like floating; his death is like resting. He does not scheme or plan. Radiant yet not dazzling, trustworthy yet not bound by expectation. In sleep, he does not dream; awake, he has no worries. His spirit is pure and unadulterated; his soul does not weary. Empty, tranquil, and indifferent, he accords with the virtue of Heaven.

Thus it is said: Sorrow and joy are the perversions of virtue; delight and anger are the transgressions of the Dao; likes and dislikes are the loss of virtue. Thus, a heart without sorrow or joy is the perfection of virtue; unity without change is the perfection of stillness; nothing to oppose is the perfection of emptiness; no interaction with things is the perfection of indifference; nothing to resist is the perfection of purity.

Thus it is said: If the body labors without rest, it becomes exhausted; if essence is used without ceasing, it becomes fatigued; fatigue leads to depletion.

The nature of water is such that if it is not mixed, it is clear; if it is not disturbed, it is level. If it is pent up and does not flow, it cannot remain clear—this is the image of the virtue of Heaven.

Thus it is said: Pure and unadulterated, still and unchanging, indifferent and without action, moving in accordance with the movement of Heaven—this is the way to nurture the spirit.

Those who possess the swords of Gan and Yue keep them in a box and do not dare to use them—they treasure them to the utmost. When the spirit flows in all four directions, reaching everywhere without limit, above touching Heaven, below coiling upon the Earth, transforming and nurturing the myriad things without taking form—its name is "Uniting with the Lord." The Dao of purity and simplicity guards the spirit. Guard it and do not lose it, and you become one with the spirit. The essence of unity penetrates, harmonizing with the heavenly order.

A rustic saying goes: "The common people value profit; the incorruptible value fame; the worthy esteem will; the sage treasures essence." Thus, "simplicity" means having nothing mixed with it; "purity" means not diminishing one’s spirit. One who embodies purity and simplicity is called a "true person."

---

**Chapter Sixteen: "Mending the Nature"**

To mend one’s nature in accordance with custom, learning to seek to return to one’s original state; to be confused by desires in accordance with custom, thinking to seek to attain clarity—this is called being obscured and deluded.

The ancients who cultivated the Dao used tranquility to nurture knowledge. To be born and not use knowledge is called using knowledge to nurture tranquility. Knowledge and tranquility mutually nurture each other, and harmony emerges from one’s nature.

Virtue is harmony; the Dao is principle. Virtue admits of no exclusion—this is benevolence. The Dao leaves nothing unprincipled—this is righteousness. Righteousness being clear, things become intimate—this is loyalty. Being centered, pure, and returning to reality—this is joy. Trust in conduct, accommodating in form, and following pattern—this is ritual. When ritual and music are universally practiced, the world falls into chaos.

If one rectifies oneself and obscures one’s virtue, virtue will not be overbearing. If it is overbearing, things will surely lose their nature.

In ancient times, people dwelled in the midst of vagueness, attaining indifference with their age. At that time, yin and yang were harmonious and still, ghosts and spirits did not disturb, the four seasons kept to their rhythm, the myriad things were unharmed, and the multitude of living beings did not die prematurely. Though humans possessed knowledge, they had no use for it—this was called the perfection of unity. At that time, nothing was done, yet all was constantly natural.

When virtue declined, Suiren and Fuxi began to "make Heaven," thus following yet not unified. When virtue further declined, Shennong and the Yellow Emperor began to "make the world below," thus being at peace yet not following. When virtue declined still further, Yao and Shun began to "make the world," initiating the flow of governance and transformation, diluting purity and scattering simplicity, departing from the Dao to pursue goodness, risking virtue to act—then they abandoned nature and followed the heart.

When hearts and minds gained knowledge yet proved insufficient to stabilize the world, then came refinement, and with it, expansive learning. Refinement eroded substance, and breadth drowned the heart—then the people first fell into confusion and disorder, unable to return to their nature and restore their origin.

From this we see: the world lost the Dao, and the Dao was lost to the world—they perished together. How could the people of the Dao arise in such a world? How could the world arise in the Dao? If the Dao cannot arise in the world, and the world cannot arise in the Dao, then even if a sage dwells in the mountains and forests, their virtue remains hidden. Hidden, yet not by their own concealment.

The ancients spoke of recluses not as those who crouched unseen, nor as those who withheld their words, nor as those who hoarded their wisdom without expression—it was the great error of the times that compelled them. Had the times aligned with their destiny and flourished throughout the world, they would have returned to unity without effort. But when the times did not align, they deeply rooted themselves in tranquility and awaited their moment—this was the way of preserving the self.

The ancients who preserved themselves did not adorn their wisdom with eloquence, nor exhaust the world with their knowledge, nor deplete their virtue with their intellect. Serene in their place, they returned to their nature—what more was there to do?

The Dao does not thrive in petty deeds, nor does virtue in shallow understanding. Small understanding harms virtue, and petty deeds harm the Dao. Thus it is said: rectify the self and nothing more. To delight in wholeness is called "attaining one’s will."

The ancients spoke of "attaining one’s will" not in terms of carriages and caps of office, but as having nothing to add to their joy. Today’s notion of "attaining one’s will" refers to carriages and caps. Yet when these adornments rest upon the body, they are not of one’s nature or destiny—they are transient things, borrowed. Borrowed, their coming cannot be barred, nor their going halted.

Therefore, one does not indulge the will for carriages and caps, nor conform to custom in poverty and hardship. Their joy remains the same in both, and so they are without sorrow. But if, when the borrowed departs, one is no longer joyful, then even in joy, there is always desolation. Thus it is said: those who lose themselves to things and forfeit their nature to custom are called "the inverted people."

**Book Six, Part Two – Chapter Seventeen: "Autumn Floods"**

When autumn floods arrive, the hundred streams pour into the Yellow River.

When the autumn floods swell the Jing River to its fullest, the banks and islets on either side become so vast that one cannot distinguish ox from horse. At this, the River Lord rejoiced exultantly, believing all the world’s beauty to be his alone. Following the current eastward, he reached the Northern Sea. Turning to face the east, he saw the water stretching endlessly. Then the River Lord turned his gaze, looked toward Ruo of the Northern Sea, and sighed:

“There is a rustic saying: ‘Having heard the Dao a hundred times, one thinks none can compare to oneself.’ That describes me. Moreover, I once heard men belittle Confucius’s learning and dismiss Boyi’s righteousness, and I did not believe them. Now that I have witnessed the boundlessness of your waters, had I not come to your gate, I would have remained in peril—forever ridiculed by those of great understanding.”

Ruo of the Northern Sea replied:
“The well frog cannot speak of the sea, for it is confined by its hollow. The summer insect cannot speak of ice, for it is bound by its season. The narrow scholar cannot speak of the Dao, for he is shackled by his teachings. Now that you have emerged from your riverbanks and beheld the great sea, you recognize your own limitations—now you may be spoken with of great things.

Of all the waters under heaven, none is greater than the sea. The ten thousand rivers flow into it, yet it never fills, no matter when they cease. The Tail Conduit drains it, yet it never empties, no matter when it stops. Unchanged by autumn, unaware of flood or drought, it surpasses the flow of the Yangtze and Yellow River beyond all measure. Yet I have never taken pride in this, for I compare my form to heaven and earth and receive my qi from the yang. I stand between heaven and earth like a pebble or twig in a great mountain. Since I perceive my own smallness, why should I boast?

Consider the Four Seas within heaven and earth—are they not like a tiny crevice in a vast marsh? Consider the Central States within the seas—are they not like a grain of millet in a granary? We number things by the myriad, yet humanity is but one among them. People fill the Nine Provinces, where grain grows and boats and carriages travel, yet each person is but one among them. Compared to the myriad, is this not like the tip of a hair on a horse’s body?

The Five Emperors’ legacies, the Three Kings’ struggles, the benevolent men’s worries, the devoted scholars’ labors—all amount to this! Boyi rejected it for fame, Confucius discoursed on it as erudition. Their self-satisfaction—was it not like your earlier pride in your waters?”

The River Lord said, “Then may I regard heaven and earth as great and the tip of a hair as small?”

Ruo of the Northern Sea replied, “No. Things have limits, but time knows no end; their allotments are not constant, and beginnings and ends have no fixed cause. Thus the great sage observes both near and far, so that the small is not deemed insignificant, nor the great excessive—knowing the limits of things. He verifies past and present, so that the distant does not weary him, nor the immediate make him strain—knowing time’s endlessness. He examines fullness and emptiness, so that gain does not elate him, nor loss grieve him—knowing allotments’ inconstancy. He perceives the level path, so that life does not delight him, nor death bring misfortune—knowing beginnings and ends have no fixed cause.

What he knows is less than what he does not know; the time of his life is less than the time before his birth. Seeking to exhaust the realm of the infinitely great with his infinitesimal self, he becomes lost and cannot attain it. From this perspective, how can the tip of a hair suffice to define the finest limit, or heaven and earth to exhaust the realm of the infinitely great?”

The River Lord said, “Those in the world all say: ‘The most refined has no form; the greatest cannot be encompassed.’ Is this true?”

Ruo of the Northern Sea replied, “From the perspective of the small, the great cannot be fully seen; from the perspective of the great, the small cannot be clearly discerned. The refined is the subtlest of the small; the vast is the fullness of the great—thus their difference is fitting. This is the nature of things.

The refined and the coarse are terms for what has form; the formless cannot be measured by numbers; the unencompassable cannot be exhausted by calculation. What can be discussed in words is the coarse aspect of things; what can be grasped by intention is the refined aspect. What words cannot discuss, what intention cannot grasp—these do not depend on refinement or coarseness.

Thus the actions of the great man do not harm others, nor does he overvalue benevolence and kindness. He does not act for profit, nor despise servants. He does not contend for wealth, nor overvalue yielding. He does not rely on others in his affairs, nor overvalue self-reliance, nor despise the greedy. His conduct differs from the common, yet he does not overvalue eccentricity. He follows the crowd, yet does not despise flatterers. The world’s ranks are insufficient to encourage him; punishment and shame are insufficient to shame him. He knows right and wrong cannot be divided, nor fine and great be defined.

It is said: ‘The Daoist does not strive, the perfectly virtuous does not attain, the great man has no self.’ This is the utmost in moderation.”

The River Spirit said: "Beyond things, within things—where do we draw the line between noble and base? Where do we distinguish great and small?"

The Spirit of the Northern Sea replied: "Viewed through the Dao, things have their nobility and baseness. Viewed through things themselves, each deems itself noble and others base. Viewed through custom, nobility and baseness lie beyond one’s control. Viewed through differentiation, if you take what is great in something and call it great, then nothing in the world is not great; if you take what is small in something and call it small, then nothing in the world is not small. Knowing that heaven and earth are but a grain of millet, and a tip of hair is a mountain, one perceives the relativity of measure.

Viewed through function, if you take what something possesses and call it possession, then nothing in the world lacks possession; if you take what something lacks and call it lack, then nothing in the world possesses anything. Knowing that east and west are opposites yet cannot exist without each other, one grasps the division of function.

Viewed through inclination, if you take what something affirms and affirm it, then nothing in the world is not affirmed; if you take what something denies and deny it, then nothing in the world is not denied. Knowing that Yao and Jie each affirmed their own ways and denied the other’s, one perceives the nature of inclination.

In ancient times, Yao and Shun yielded the throne and became emperors, while Zhi and Kuai yielded it and met their ruin. Tang and Wu fought for it and became kings, while the Duke of Bai fought for it and perished. From this, we see that the rites of contention and yielding, the conduct of Yao and Jie, nobility and baseness—all have their time, and cannot be taken as constant.

A battering ram can breach a city wall but cannot plug a rat hole—this speaks of differing implements. The swift steeds Qi Ji and Hua Liu can gallop a thousand li in a day, yet the rat is no match for the weasel—this speaks of differing skills. The owl snatches fleas at night and discerns the tip of a hair, yet with eyes wide open it cannot see a mountain—this speaks of differing natures.

Thus it is said: Should one take ‘this’ as the standard and reject all ‘not-this,’ or take order as the standard and reject all disorder? This is to fail to understand the principles of heaven and earth and the conditions of the myriad things. It is like taking heaven as the standard and rejecting earth, or taking yin as the standard and rejecting yang—clearly, this cannot be put into practice! Yet to persist in such talk without ceasing is either folly or deception.

The abdications of emperors and kings differ; the successions of the Three Dynasties differ. Those who act against their times and go against custom are called usurpers; those who act in accord with their times and follow custom are called righteous men. Be silent, River Spirit! How could you know the gate of nobility and baseness, the house of greatness?"

The River Spirit said: "Then what should I do? What should I not do? How should I decide what to accept or reject, what to pursue or abandon?"

The Spirit of the Northern Sea replied: "Viewed through the Dao, what is noble? What is base? These are but reversals and extensions. Do not constrain your will, for it will greatly hinder the Dao. What is few? What is many? These are but shifts and exchanges. Do not act with singularity, for it will clash with the Dao.

Be solemn as a state with its ruler, impartial in virtue; be reverent as a sacrifice with its altar, impartial in blessing; be boundless as the four directions without limit, without demarcation. Embracing the myriad things, who bears the burden of their support? This is called having no fixed direction.

The myriad things are equal—who is short, who is long? The Dao has no end or beginning; things have death and life, yet do not rely on their achievements. Emptiness and fullness alternate, not fixed in form. Years cannot be held back, time cannot be stopped. Exhaustion and fulfillment, end and beginning—these are the great principles, the patterns of the myriad things.

The birth of things is like a gallop, like a race. No movement without change, no moment without shift. What should be done? What should not be done? All things will transform themselves."

The River Spirit said: "Then is the Dao to be prized?"

The Spirit of the Northern Sea replied: "One who knows the Dao must grasp its principles; one who grasps its principles must understand its adaptability; one who understands its adaptability does not let things cause harm. The one of utmost virtue—fire cannot burn them, water cannot drown them, cold and heat cannot harm them, birds and beasts cannot injure them. Not that they belittle these things, but they speak of safety and danger, tranquility amid fortune and misfortune, caution in approach and retreat—nothing can harm them.

Thus it is said: Heaven is within, man is without; virtue lies in heaven. Know the movement of heaven, root yourself in heaven, rest in attainment. Hesitate and bend or stretch, return to the essential and speak of the ultimate."

The River Spirit asked: "What is heaven? What is man?"

The Spirit of the Northern Sea replied: "The four legs of oxen and horses—this is heaven. Bridling a horse’s head, piercing an ox’s nose—this is man. Thus it is said: Do not let man obliterate heaven, do not let purpose obliterate fate, do not let gain be sacrificed for fame. Guard this carefully and do not lose it—this is called returning to one’s true nature."

Kui pitied the centipede, the centipede pitied the snake, the snake pitied the wind, the wind pitied the eye, the eye pitied the mind. Kui said to the centipede: "I hop along on one leg—there is nothing I can do about it. Now you command ten thousand legs—how do you manage?"

The centipede replied: "Not so. Have you never seen one who spits?"

The droplets fall in sizes great as pearls or fine as mist, scattering in numbers beyond count. Now I move by my heavenly mechanism, yet know not how it comes to be."

The snake said: "I walk with many feet, yet cannot match your footlessness—why is this?"

The snake replied: "When the heavenly mechanism stirs, how can one resist? What need have I for feet!"

The snake asked the wind: "I move by flexing my spine and ribs, and thus have some semblance of form. Yet you rise in a great rush from the north, rush into the southern sea, and seem as if you have no form at all—why is this?"

The wind said: "Indeed, I rise in a great rush from the northern sea and enter the southern sea. Yet if one points a finger at me, they overcome me; if one treads upon me, they overcome me. Nevertheless, to break great trees and topple mighty halls—only I can do this. To turn many small defeats into a great victory—only the sage can achieve this."

Confucius traveled in Kuang, where men of Song encircled him several layers deep, yet he continued strumming his zither and singing without pause. Zilu entered to see him and asked: "How can the Master bear this?" Confucius said: "Come, let me tell you. I have long concealed myself from adversity, yet cannot escape it—such is fate. I have long sought passage, yet cannot attain it—such is timing. In the age of Yao and Shun, there were no destitute men in the world, not because of their wisdom’s gain; in the age of Jie and Zhou, there were no unobstructed men in the world, not because of their wisdom’s loss: the circumstances simply aligned thus. He who travels by water without avoiding dragons and jiao is the courage of the fisherman; he who travels by land without avoiding rhinoceroses and tigers is the courage of the hunter; he who faces crossed blades before him and regards death as life is the courage of the stalwart warrior; he who knows adversity is governed by fate, knows passage comes with timing, and faces great peril without fear—this is the courage of the sage. Rest easy, You! My fate is constrained by something greater!"

Before long, the armored commander advanced and said: "We mistook you for Yang Hu and surrounded you; now that we see it is not him, we beg leave to withdraw."

Gongsun Long questioned Wei Mou, saying: "In my youth, I studied the Dao of the former kings; in maturity, I clarified the practice of benevolence and righteousness. I harmonized sameness and difference, separated hardness and whiteness; affirmed the negative and the positive, the permissible and the impermissible. I confounded the wisdom of the hundred schools, exhausted the eloquence of all disputants—thus I took myself to have attained ultimate comprehension. Now, having heard the words of Zhuangzi, I find them strange indeed. Do I fall short in my reasoning? Or do I simply lack his depth of understanding? Now I stand speechless; I venture to ask for your guidance."

Prince Mou sighed deeply, looked up to the heavens, and laughed: "Have you alone not heard of the frog in the shallow well? It said to the turtle of the Eastern Sea: 'How joyful I am! I leap and prance upon the well’s railing, rest in the chinks of its broken walls. When I plunge into the water, it supports my armpits and cradles my chin; when I tread in the mud, it covers my feet and submerges my ankles. I turn to the crabs and tadpoles—none can compare to me! Moreover, to command the water of a single ravine and to lord over the pleasures of this well—what more could one desire? Why not come in and see for yourself?'

Before the turtle of the Eastern Sea could set its left foot inside, its right knee was already stuck. Hesitating, it withdrew and said: 'A distance of a thousand *li* cannot encompass its vastness; a height of a thousand *ren* cannot fathom its depth. In the time of Yu, nine floods in ten years did not increase its waters; in the time of Tang, seven droughts in eight years did not diminish its shores. Unchanged by the passage of moments or ages, unmoved by the increase or decrease of waters—this is the great joy of the Eastern Sea!'

Hearing this, the frog in the shallow well was startled, bewildered, and lost in self-doubt. Now, if one does not even know the bounds of right and wrong yet still wishes to examine the words of Zhuangzi, it is like making a mosquito bear a mountain or a centipede race a thoroughbred—they will surely fail. And if one does not comprehend the ultimate subtleties of his discourse yet seeks only momentary advantage, is this not the frog in the shallow well?

Moreover, he strides upon the Yellow Springs and ascends the Great Vastness, unbound by south or north, released in all four directions, submerged in the unfathomable; unbound by east or west, beginning in profound darkness, returning to the Great Thoroughfare. Yet you seek to measure him with narrow scrutiny, to probe him with disputation—is this not like using a tube to view the heavens or a bodkin to point at the earth? Is it not too small a way?

Go now! Have you alone not heard of the young men of Shouling who went to Handan to learn its gait? Before mastering the state’s skill, they lost their own way of walking and could only crawl home on all fours. If you do not leave now, you will forget your own roots and lose your own calling."

Gongsun Long’s mouth gaped open and would not close; his tongue rose and would not descend. He fled in haste.

Zhuangzi was fishing in the Pu River when the King of Chu sent two great ministers to approach him first, saying: "I wish to trouble you with the affairs of my realm!"

Zhuangzi, rod in hand, did not turn, and said: "I have heard that in Chu there is a divine tortoise, dead for three thousand years. The king keeps it wrapped in silk and stored in a casket upon the ancestral altar. Would this tortoise prefer to be honored for its bones, or to live and drag its tail through the mud?"

The two ministers replied: "It would prefer to live and drag its tail through the mud."

Zhuangzi said: "Go then! I too shall drag my tail through the mud."

When Huizi was prime minister of Liang, Zhuangzi went to visit him. Someone said to Huizi: "Zhuangzi is coming to replace you as minister." Thereupon, Huizi searched the state for three days and three nights.

Zhuangzi went to see him and said: "In the south there is a bird called the *yuanchu*—do you know it? The *yuanchu* flies from the Southern Sea to the Northern Sea, resting only on the *wutong* tree, eating only the fruit of the *melia*, drinking only from springs of sweet wine. A kite, clutching a rotten rat, saw the *yuanchu* pass by and looked up, crying, '*Ha!*' Now, do you wish to threaten me with your state of Liang?"

Zhuangzi and Huizi strolled upon the bridge over the Hao River. Zhuangzi said: "The minnows glide forth at ease—this is the joy of fish."

Huizi said: "You are not a fish—how do you know the joy of fish?"

Zhuangzi replied: "You are not me—how do you know that I do not know the joy of fish?"

Huizi said: "I am not you, so of course I do not know you; but you are certainly not a fish, so your not knowing the joy of fish is complete!"

Zhuangzi said: "Let us return to the beginning. When you asked, *'How do you know the joy of fish?'*—you already knew that I knew it when you asked me. I knew it from here, upon the Hao."

**Chapter Eighteen: Supreme Joy**
Is there in the world a supreme joy, or is there not? Are there ways to preserve life, or are there not? What should one do? What rely upon? What avoid? Where dwell? What pursue? What delight in? What despise?

What the world honors are wealth, nobility, long life, and virtue; what it delights in are bodily ease, rich flavors, fine garments, beautiful sights, and sweet sounds. What it despises are poverty, lowliness, early death, and vice; what it suffers from are the body’s lack of ease, the absence of rich flavors, the want of fine garments, the deprivation of beautiful sights, and the loss of sweet sounds. Those who cannot obtain these things are filled with great sorrow and fear—how foolish they are in their concern for the body!

The wealthy exhaust themselves with toil, amassing riches they cannot fully use—how alienated they are from the body! The noble pass their days and nights in ceaseless thought, weighing right and wrong—how estranged they are from the body! Human life is born with care. The long-lived grow dull, enduring long sorrow without dying—what bitter suffering! How distant they are from the body! The resolute strive for fame in the world, yet it is not enough to preserve their lives. Is their goodness truly good? Or is it not good? If it is good, it is not enough to preserve life; if it is not good, it is enough to preserve others. Thus it is said: *"When loyal admonitions go unheeded, withdraw and do not contend."* Yet Wu Zixu contended and brought ruin upon his body; had he not contended, his name would not have endured. Is there truly goodness, or is there not?

As for the pursuits and pleasures of the common world, I do not know whether they are truly joy or not. I observe how the masses rush together in their pursuits, driven as if they cannot help themselves, all calling it joy—yet I find no joy in it, nor do I find it not joy. Is there truly joy, or is there not? I take non-action as true joy, yet it is what the world deems the greatest suffering. Thus it is said: *"Supreme joy is without joy; supreme praise is without praise."* The right and wrong of the world cannot truly be settled. Nevertheless, non-action can settle right and wrong.

Supreme joy preserves life—only non-action comes close to it. Let me try to speak of it: Heaven is non-active and so becomes clear; Earth is non-active and so becomes tranquil. When the two non-actions combine, the myriad things are transformed and born. Vague and indistinct, without whence they emerge! Indistinct and vague, without form they take! The myriad things flourish, all growing from non-action. Thus it is said: *"Heaven and Earth do nothing, yet there is nothing they do not do."* Who among men can attain non-action?

When Zhuangzi’s wife died, Huizi went to offer condolences. Zhuangzi was squatting with legs spread, drumming on a basin and singing. Huizi said: "To live with someone, raise a child together, and upon her death not weep would be enough—yet you drum on a basin and sing! Is this not excessive?"

Zhuangzi said: "Not so. When she first died, how could I alone not feel grief? But I examined her beginning and found she was originally without life; not only without life, but originally without form; not only without form, but originally without *qi*. Amidst the vague and indistinct, there was transformation into *qi*, *qi* transformed into form, form transformed into life. Now she has transformed again into death. This is like the passing of spring and autumn, summer and winter, in the turning of the four seasons. She now lies at peace in the great chamber, and I follow wailing after her—would this not show I do not understand fate? Thus I stopped."

Zhilishu and Huajieshu were observing the mound of Mingbo, the wasteland of Kunlun, where the Yellow Emperor once rested. Suddenly, a wen grew on Huajieshu’s elbow, and he was dismayed by it. Zhilishu said: "Do you dislike it?"

Huajieshu replied: "No, why should I dislike it? Life is a temporary borrowing. What borrows and gives rise to life is but dust and grime. Death and life are like day and night. Moreover, you and I observe transformation, and transformation has reached me—why should I dislike it?"

When Zhuangzi went to Chu, he saw an empty skull, bleached and still shaped. He tapped it with his horse whip and questioned it, saying: "Did you, sir, covet and lose the Dao, and so come to this? Or did you suffer the fall of a state, execution by axe, and so come to this? Or did you commit some shameful deed, bringing disgrace to your parents and wife, and so come to this? Or did you suffer from cold and hunger, and so come to this? Or did your years run out, and so come to this?"

Having spoken, he took the skull as a pillow and lay down. In the middle of the night, the skull appeared to him in a dream and said: "Your words resemble those of a sophist. All you speak of are the burdens of the living—death has none of these. Would you like to hear the truth of death?"

Zhuangzi said: "Yes."

The skull said: "In death, there is no ruler above, no subject below; nor are there the affairs of the four seasons. At ease, one takes heaven and earth as spring and autumn. Even the joy of a king facing south cannot surpass it."

Zhuangzi did not believe it and said: "If I had the Controller of Fate restore your form, give you bones, flesh, and skin, return you to your parents, wife, children, neighbors, and friends, would you desire this?"

The skull frowned deeply and said: "How could I abandon the joy of a king facing south and return to the toils of the human world?"

When Yan Yuan journeyed east to Qi, Confucius wore a troubled expression. Zigong stepped down from his mat and asked: "This disciple ventures to ask—Yan Yuan’s journey to Qi has brought you this troubled look. Is that so?"

Confucius said: "Excellent is your question! Long ago, Guanzi spoke words I hold in great esteem: *'A small sack cannot hold a large object; a short rope cannot draw from the deep.'* Such is the way of things—each destiny has its completion, each form its proper place, and these cannot be altered. I fear that Yan Yuan, in speaking to the Marquis of Qi of the Dao of Yao, Shun, and the Yellow Emperor, will further cite the words of Suiren and Shennong. The Marquis will then turn inward to seek within himself—and if he finds it, he will not; if he does not, he will be confounded. A man confounded is as good as dead.

"And have you alone not heard? Once, a seabird alighted in the outskirts of Lu. The Lord of Lu received it with ceremony and offered it wine in the ancestral temple, performed the *Nine Shao* as music, and prepared the grandest sacrificial feast. Yet the bird only gazed in distress and sorrow, dared not eat a single morsel, dared not drink a single cup, and died after three days. This was nourishing the bird with what nourishes oneself, not with what nourishes the bird. To nourish a bird as a bird should be nourished, one ought to let it roost in deep forests, wander open plains, float upon rivers and lakes, feed on minnows and loaches, follow its flock in flight and rest, and live at ease. Why should it hear the clamor of human words? When the music of *Xianchi* and *Nine Shao* is played across the wilds of Dongting, birds hear it and fly away, beasts hear it and flee, fish hear it and dive deep, while men, upon hearing it, gather to listen. Fish live in water and thrive, but men would drown. They must differ in kind, for their likes and dislikes are not the same. Thus the sages of old did not presume to impose their abilities, nor did they force uniformity in affairs. Names were kept to their realities, meanings were set to their proper uses—this is what is called 'orderly coherence and enduring good fortune.'"

Liezi was traveling and took a meal by the roadside when he saw a century-old skull. Pulling aside the brambles, he pointed to it and said: "Only you and I know that you have never died, and never lived. Do you truly grieve? Do I truly rejoice?"

---

Is there a single source from which all things arise? Obtain water, and they become algae; at the border of earth and water, they become the raiment of frogs and toads. Growing on mounds, they become plantain; plantain that finds damp shade becomes crow’s-foot. The root of crow’s-foot becomes a grub, its leaves become butterflies. Butterflies in time transform into insects, born beneath the stove, their form like a cast-off shell, their name *qūduó*. After a thousand days, *qūduó* becomes a bird called *gānyúgǔ*. The froth of *gānyúgǔ* becomes *sīmí*, and *sīmí* becomes *shíxī*. From *shíxī* comes *yílù*, from *jiǔyóu* comes *huángkuàng*, and from rotten silkworms comes *màoruì*. *Yángxī* joins with the unopened bamboo shoot to produce *qīngníng*, *qīngníng* gives rise to the leopard, the leopard to the horse, the horse to man—and man in turn returns into the source. All things emerge from the source, all things return to the source.

Those who comprehend the essence of life do not strive after what is of no use to life. Those who comprehend the essence of fate do not strive after knowledge that can do nothing in the face of the inevitable. Some first attend to things, yet though things are abundant, the body is not nourished; some possess life yet first lose their form, while others retain their form yet lose their life. What comes cannot be refused, what departs cannot be stopped. How lamentable! The people of the world believe that nourishing the body is sufficient to preserve life, yet if nourishing the body is truly insufficient to preserve life, what is it good for? Though it may not be sufficient, one cannot but do it—such effort is inescapable. To escape the labor of maintaining form, nothing surpasses abandoning the world. Free from encumbrances, one attains perfect equilibrium; in perfect equilibrium, one exchanges life with the Dao, and in such exchange lies nearness to the Way.

Why cling to affairs? Why forsake life? Abandoning form requires no toil, and forsaking life preserves one’s essence. When form is whole and essence restored, one becomes one with Heaven. Heaven and Earth are the parents of the myriad things: in union, they generate; in dispersion, they give rise to beginnings. When form and essence remain unharmed, this is called "the power to transform." Refining essence ever further, one returns to assist Heaven.

Master Liezi asked Guan Yin: "The perfected person walks through water without obstruction, treads fire without heat, and moves above the myriad things without fear. How does one attain this?" Guan Yin replied: "This is the preservation of pure *qi*, not the rank of cunning, skill, or boldness. Sit, and I will tell you: all that has appearance, form, and color belongs to the realm of things—how can things be far apart from one another? Why strive to be first? It is but form. Thus, things arise from the formless and rest where there is no place. Those who attain this and exhaust its depths—how can things confine them? They dwell within the measure of no excess, conceal themselves in the beginningless order, and wander where things end and begin. They unify their nature, nurture their *qi*, harmonize their virtue, and thereby commune with the source of all things. Such a one preserves Heaven intact, their spirit without flaw—how could things intrude?

Consider the drunkard who falls from a cart: though the fall is swift, he does not die. His joints are the same as others’, and he suffers the same harm, yet his spirit remains whole. He is unaware of riding, unaware of falling; life and death, terror and dread do not enter his breast. Thus, he encounters things yet remains unharmed. If one can be so whole in wine, how much more so in Heaven? The sage hides in Heaven, and thus nothing can harm them.

The avenger does not blame the blade; even one with a vengeful heart does not resent a flying tile. Thus, the world is in balance. Therefore, there are no wars of aggression, no punishments of slaughter—this arises from the Way. Do not open the Heavenly in man, but open the Heavenly of Heaven. Opening the Heavenly of Heaven gives rise to virtue; opening the Heavenly in man gives rise to harm. Do not weary of Heaven, neglect man, and the people will draw near to their true nature."

---

Confucius traveled to Chu and, passing through a forest, saw a hunchback catching cicadas as easily as if he were plucking them. Confucius said, "You are skillful—is this the Way?"

The hunchback replied, "I have my method. After five or six months of stacking two pellets without them falling, I lose only a fraction; with three pellets, I lose one in ten; with five, it is as effortless as plucking. I hold my body still as a withered stump, and my arm steady as a dry branch. Though heaven and earth are vast and the myriad things numerous, I am aware only of the cicada’s wings. I do not turn or sway, nor do I let the myriad things distract me from the cicada’s wings—how could I fail?"

Confucius turned to his disciples and said, "When the mind is undivided, it congeals in spirit. Such is the way of the hunchbacked elder!"

Yan Yuan asked Confucius, "I once crossed the deep rapids of Shang, where the ferryman handled the boat as if by magic. I asked him, ‘Is this a matter of practice?’ He said, ‘Yes. Those skilled in swimming can do it. But as for the diver, he has never seen a boat yet handles it with ease.’ I questioned him further, but he would not explain. May I ask what this means?"

Confucius said, "Those skilled in swimming can do it because they forget the water. As for the diver who handles the boat with ease without ever having seen one—he sees the depths as if they were a hill, and the capsizing of a boat as if it were his cart rolling backward. Though a thousand dangers lie before him, they cannot enter his mind—where could he find no leisure? One who stakes tiles gambles with skill; one who stakes hooks grows cautious; one who stakes gold grows flustered. The skill is the same, but when one values the external, the inner becomes clumsy. For all who value the external, the inner is inept."

Field Opening called upon Duke Wei of Zhou. The Duke said, "I have heard that Zhu Shen pursues learning. You have kept company with Zhu Shen—what have you learned from him?"

Field Opening replied, "I merely wield broom and dustpan to attend in the courtyard—what could I have learned from the Master!"

The Duke said, "Field Opening, do not decline. I wish to hear it."

Opening said, "I heard the Master say: *'One who nurtures life well is like a shepherd herding sheep—he watches those lagging behind and whips them.'*"

The Duke asked, "What does this mean?"

Field Opening said, "In Lu there was a man named Shan Bao who dwelled in the cliffs and drank from streams, sharing no profit with the people. Though he lived to seventy, his complexion remained like a child’s. Unluckily, he met a starving tiger, which killed and devoured him. There was also Zhang Yi, who frequented every high gate and low curtain, running wherever he went. At forty, he died of internal heat. Bao nurtured his inner self, yet the tiger devoured his outer; Yi nurtured his outer self, yet illness assailed his inner. These two did not whip those lagging behind."

Confucius said, "Do not hide in seclusion, do not expose yourself to excess, but stand firm in the center. If these three are attained, one reaches the highest name. Those who fear the road—if one in ten is killed, fathers and sons, elder and younger brothers will warn one another, and none will dare venture out without a great retinue. Are they not wise? Yet what they truly should fear lies in the bedding and at the dining table, and they do not guard against it—this is their error!"

The invocator, clad in dark ceremonial robes, approached the pen and addressed the pig: "Why do you hate death? I will fatten you for three months, purify you for ten days, fast for a day, spread white couch grass, and place your shoulders and haunches upon the carved sacrificial platter—would you prefer this?"

Planning for the pig, he said, "Better to eat chaff and dregs in the pen."

Planning for himself, he said, "If alive, one may enjoy the honor of carriages and caps; if dead, one may lie upon the painted bier amid the gathered mourners—this is acceptable."

For the pig, he rejects it; for himself, he accepts it. How does he differ from the pig?

Duke Huan was hunting in the marshes, with Guan Zhong driving the chariot, when he saw a ghost. The Duke grasped Guan Zhong’s hand and asked, "Zhongfu, what did you see?"

Guan Zhong replied, "Your servant saw nothing."

The Duke returned, fell ill with vexation, and did not leave his chamber for days. A Qi scholar named Huangzi Gao’ao said, "The Duke has harmed himself—how could a ghost harm him? When pent-up *qi* scatters and does not return, it causes deficiency; when it rises but does not descend, it makes one prone to anger; when it descends but does not rise, it makes one prone to forgetfulness; when it neither rises nor descends but lodges in the center of the body, at the heart, it causes illness."

Duke Huan asked, "Then are there ghosts?"

He replied, "There are. In the depths dwell *Li*."

The hearth has *Ji*. In the inner chambers’ troubled soil, *Leiting* dwells; beneath the northeast lies *Bei’e* and *Xielong*; beneath the northwest, *Yiyang* resides. In waters lurks *Wangxiang*; in mounds, *Shen*; in mountains, *Kui*; in the wilds, *Panghuang*; in marshes, *Weiyi*.

The Duke asked, "Pray tell, what is the form of *Weiyi*?"

Huangzi replied, "*Weiyi* is as large as a wheel hub, as long as a chariot shaft, clad in purple robes with a vermilion cap. As a creature, it abhors the sound of thunderous chariots—when it hears them, it clasps its head and stands erect. He who sees it is on the verge of hegemony."

Duke Huan laughed heartily and said, "This was what *I* saw!" Thereupon, he straightened his robes and cap, sat with Huangzi, and before the day was out, his illness had vanished without his notice.

Ji Shengzi reared fighting cocks for the king. After ten days, the king asked, "Are the cocks ready?"

"Not yet," he replied. "They are still hollow and arrogant, relying on their pride."

After another ten days, the king asked again. He said, "Not yet. They still respond to shadows and reflections."

After another ten days, the king asked again. He said, "Not yet. Their gaze is still fierce, and their *qi* remains exuberant."

After another ten days, the king asked once more. He said, "Nearly so. Though others may crow, they show no change. From afar, they resemble wooden cocks—their virtue is complete. No other cock dares challenge them; they turn and flee."

Confucius observed the Lüliang Falls, where the water plunged from a height of thirty *ren*, its froth surging forty *li*, a place where turtles, alligators, fish, and soft-shelled turtles could not swim. Yet a man swam there, appearing distressed and on the verge of drowning. Confucius sent his disciples to follow the current and rescue him. After several hundred paces, the man emerged, his hair unbound, singing as he strolled beneath the embankment.

Confucius approached and questioned him, saying, "I took you for a ghost, but upon closer inspection, you are a man. May I ask: is there a Dao to treading water?"

The man replied, "No, I have no Dao. I began with what is innate, grew with my nature, and fulfilled my destiny. I enter the water in harmony with its flow, emerge in unison with its rush, following the water’s Way without imposing my own will. This is how I tread it."

Confucius asked, "What do you mean by beginning with what is innate, growing with your nature, and fulfilling your destiny?"

The man said, "I was born on the hills and am at ease on the hills—that is my innate endowment. I grew up in the water and am at ease in the water—that is my nature. I do not know why I am as I am, yet I am so—that is my destiny."

Zi Qing carved wood into a *ju*, and when it was completed, those who saw it marveled as if it were the work of ghosts or spirits. The Lord of Lu saw it and questioned him, saying, "What technique do you employ?"

Zi Qing replied, "I am but a craftsman—what technique could I possess? Nevertheless, there is one thing. When I am about to make a *ju*, I never dare to squander my *qi*; I must first align my mind. After three days of alignment, I no longer dare to harbor thoughts of rewards, ranks, or emoluments. After five days, I no longer dare to entertain praise or blame, skill or clumsiness. After seven days, I suddenly forget that I possess limbs or a body. At that moment, there is no court or state. My skill becomes concentrated, and external concerns dissolve. Only then do I enter the mountain forests to observe the natural essence of things. When the form is fully realized within me, the *ju* appears of its own accord, and only then do I set my hands to it. If not, I desist. Thus, I unite Heaven with Heaven—this, perhaps, is why the vessel seems divine!"

Dongye Ji was presented to Duke Zhuang as a charioteer. His advances and retreats aligned with the plumb line, and his left and right turns followed the compass. The duke declared that even the finest embroidery could not surpass his precision. He ordered Ji to drive a hundred circuits and return. Yan He encountered him and entered to say, "Ji’s horses are about to fail." The duke remained silent and did not respond. Soon after, the horses indeed faltered and returned.

The duke asked, "How did you know this?"
He replied, "His horses’ strength was exhausted, yet he still demanded more—this is why I said they would fail."

When Gongchui turned his hand, his work surpassed compass and square. His fingers transformed with things, without deliberation in his mind, so his spirit-terrace stood unified and unshackled.

Feet forgotten—this is comfort.
Waist forgotten—this is the comfort of a belt.
Knowledge forgetting right and wrong—this is the comfort of the mind.
No inner change, no outer compliance—this is the comfort of events converging.
From the outset, one who is never not at ease—this is the comfort of forgetting comfort.

There was a man named Sun Xiu who came to the door and addressed Master Bian Qingzi, saying, "Though I live in the village, none call me unrefined; though I face peril, none call me cowardly. Yet my fields and lands do not meet with the year’s favor, my service to my lord does not meet with the age’s favor. I am shunned by my village, driven from my district—what offense have I committed against Heaven? Why has fate treated me thus?"

Master Bian said, "Have you alone not heard of the perfected person’s way of conduct? They forget their liver and gall, leave behind their ears and eyes, wander in boundless vagueness beyond the dust and grime, roam freely in the work of no-occupation—this is called acting without reliance, creating without domination. Now you adorn your knowledge to startle the foolish, cultivate your person to expose others’ filth, shining as brightly as if you carried the sun and moon in your stride. You have preserved your bodily form intact, your nine orifices complete, and have not halfway perished from deafness, blindness, lameness, or deformity to be counted among the ranks of men—this is already fortune enough. Why then do you have leisure to resent Heaven? Begone!"

Sunzi departed, and Master Bian entered.

After a while, Master Bian looked up to the heavens and sighed. His disciples asked, "Master, why do you sigh?"

Master Bian replied, "Just now, when Sunzi came, I spoke to him of the virtue of the perfected person. I fear he was startled and thus became confused."

The disciples said, "Not so. If Sunzi’s words were right, then your words were wrong—wrongness cannot confuse rightness. If your words were right, then his words were wrong—he was already confused when he came. What fault is there in this?"

Master Bian said, "Not so. Once, when I stayed at the outskirts of Lu, the Lord of Lu was delighted and prepared a grand feast to entertain me, performing the *Nine Shao* to please me. A bird, upon hearing this, grew anxious, sorrowful, and dizzy, refusing to eat. This is what it means to nourish a bird with what nourishes oneself. As for nourishing a bird with what nourishes a bird, one should let it roost in deep forests, float upon rivers and lakes, and feed on fish and insects—then it would be at ease on level ground. Now, Sunzi is a man of limited understanding and shallow knowledge. When I spoke to him of the perfected person’s virtue, it was like loading a mouse onto a chariot drawn by horses, or sounding bells and drums for it—how could he not be startled?"

As Zhuangzi walked through the mountains, he saw a great tree with luxuriant branches and leaves. A woodcutter stood beside it but did not fell it. When asked why, he replied, "It is of no use." Zhuangzi said, "This tree, by being of no use, has lived out its natural span."

Emerging from the mountains, Zhuangzi lodged at the home of an old friend. The friend, delighted, ordered his servant to kill a goose and cook it. The servant asked, "One can caw, the other cannot— which should we kill?" The master said, "Kill the one that cannot caw."

The next day, a disciple asked Zhuangzi, "Yesterday, the tree in the mountains lived out its natural span by being of no use; today, the master’s goose died for being of no use. Where will you, Master, take your stand?"

Zhuangzi laughed and said, "I will take my stand between usefulness and uselessness. Yet even between usefulness and uselessness, one is still not free from entanglement—for it is but a semblance, not the truth. Those who ride the Dao and float with virtue are not so. Without praise or blame, now a dragon, now a serpent, they transform with the times, never clinging to one form. Rising and falling, they take harmony as their measure, drifting upon the source of all things. They master things without being mastered by things—how then could they be entangled? This is the method of the divine Shennong and the Yellow Emperor.

As for the nature of the myriad things and the traditions of human relationships, it is not so. Union leads to separation, completion to destruction; sharpness invites blunting, honor invites slander, action incurs loss, worth invites schemes, and worthlessness invites deceit. How can one be certain of anything? Alas! Remember this, disciples: only in the realm of Dao and virtue can one find refuge."

Shi Nanyu Ailiao visited the Lord of Lu, who wore a troubled expression. Shi Nanyu asked, "Why does my lord appear so troubled?"

The Lord of Lu replied, "I study the Way of the former kings and cultivate the legacy of my predecessors. I revere the spirits, honor the worthy, and practice these virtues without ceasing—yet I cannot escape misfortune. This is why I am troubled."

Shi Nanyu said, "My lord’s method of avoiding misfortune is too shallow! The sleek fox and the patterned leopard dwell in the mountains and forests, crouch in rocky caves, and remain still. They move by night and rest by day, ever vigilant. Though hungry and thirsty, they still venture to rivers and lakes in search of food, ever composed. Yet they cannot escape the snares and traps—what fault is theirs? Their very hides bring them disaster."

"Is not the state of Lu your very hide? I wish for you to flay your form and shed your skin, to cleanse your heart and cast off desire, then wander where no man dwells. In the southern lands of Yue, there is a city called the Land of Established Virtue. Its people are simple and unadorned, with few selfish desires. They know how to act but not how to hoard; they give without seeking repayment. They do not know where duty leads, nor where ritual guides. Wild and unconstrained, they tread the great Dao. Their lives are joyful, and their deaths may be buried. I wish for you to abandon your state and forsake custom, to walk alongside the Dao as your companion."

The Lord said, "That Way is distant and perilous, with rivers and mountains in between. I have no boat or carriage—what am I to do?"

Shi Nanyu replied, "Be without arrogance in your form, without lingering in your dwelling, and this will serve as your carriage."

The Lord said, "That Way is secluded and remote, with no people—with whom shall I be neighbors? I have no provisions, no food—how can I reach it?"

Shi Nanyu said, "Lessen your expenditures, reduce your desires, and though you have no grain, you will yet have enough. Cross the rivers and float upon the sea; though you see its shores from afar, the farther you go, the less you will know its end. Those who see you off will all turn back from the shore. From there, you will be far indeed! Thus, he who possesses others is burdened, and he who is possessed by others is troubled. Therefore, Yao was not one who possessed others, nor was he possessed by others. I wish to rid you of your burdens, remove your troubles, and wander alone with the Dao in the land of nothingness. When a double-hulled boat crosses the river, and an empty boat drifts into it, even a hot-tempered man would not grow angry. But if there were a person aboard, he would call out to them."

Once called and unheard, twice called and unheard—then at the third call, surely harsh words follow. Before, there was no anger; now, there is. Before, it was empty; now, it is full. If one can empty oneself and wander through the world, who could harm them?

When Beigong She levied taxes for Duke Ling of Wei to cast bells, he built an altar outside the outer gate of the capital. In three months, the upper and lower chimes were complete. Zi Qingji saw this and asked, "What method did you employ?"

She replied, "In the space between intention and action, I dared not impose anything. I have heard: *'Carved and polished, it returns to simplicity.'* In their ignorance, they were careless and hesitant; gathered in confusion, they sent off the old and welcomed the new. Those who came were not forbidden; those who left were not stopped. I followed their strength, adapted to their crookedness, and let them exhaust themselves. Thus, though taxes were levied morning and night, not a hair was harmed—how much less those on the great thoroughfares!"

Confucius was besieged between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food.

Taigong Ren went to offer his condolences and asked, "Were you nearly dead?"

"Yes," Confucius replied.

"Do you detest death?"

"Yes."

Taigong Ren said, "I once spoke of the Way of not dying. In the Eastern Sea there is a bird called *Yidai*. As a bird, it is slow and sluggish, appearing incapable. It flies only when supported by others and perches only when pressed. It dares not advance first nor lag behind; it dares not eat before others but must take the leftovers. Thus, it is never expelled from the flock, and outsiders never harm it—so it avoids disaster. The sweetest well is the first to run dry. You, perhaps, adorn your wisdom to startle the foolish, cultivate yourself to expose others’ flaws, and stride forth as if bearing the sun and moon—how could you escape harm? Long ago, I heard the Perfect One say: *'Those who boast of themselves achieve no merit; those who achieve merit fall; those who achieve fame suffer loss.'* Who discards merit and fame to return to the common fold? The Dao flows unseen; dwelling in attainment yet leaving no trace; pure and constant, one seems mad. Erase your tracks, abandon influence, and do not seek merit or fame. Thus, you bear no blame from others, and they bear none from you. The Utmost Person remains unknown—why do you delight in fame?"

Confucius said, "Excellent!" He parted from his acquaintances, left his disciples, and fled to the great marshes. He wore coarse furs and ate acorns and chestnuts. Among beasts, he did not disrupt their herds; among birds, he did not disturb their flocks. Birds and beasts did not shun him—how much less would people?

Confucius asked Zisang Hu, "I was twice exiled from Lu, had a tree felled over me in Song, was forced to erase my tracks in Wei, was destitute in Shang and Zhou, and besieged between Chen and Cai.
