I have endured these many hardships, yet my close associates grow more distant and my followers more scattered. Why is this?"

Zisang Hu replied, "Have you not heard of the man who fled the state of Jia? Lin Hui abandoned a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces to carry a baby on his back and flee. Someone asked, *'For its value? The baby’s worth is little. For its burden? The baby’s burden is great. Why abandon a jade disc worth a thousand gold pieces to carry a baby?' Lin Hui said, 'That was bound by profit; this is bound by Heaven.’* Those bound by profit abandon each other in hardship and disaster; those bound by Heaven gather together in hardship and disaster. The difference between gathering and abandoning is vast. Moreover, the friendship of the noble is as bland as water; the friendship of the petty is as sweet as wine. The noble grow closer in blandness; the petty grow estranged in sweetness. Those who unite without cause will part without cause."

Confucius said, "I respectfully receive your instruction!" He returned home leisurely, abandoned his studies, and discarded his books. His disciples no longer bowed before him, yet their affection deepened. Another day, Zisang Hu said, "When Shun was about to die, he sincerely warned Yu: *'Beware! In form, follow along; in feeling, be natural. Following along avoids separation; being natural avoids exhaustion. Without separation or exhaustion, one does not seek adornment to match form; without seeking adornment to match form, one fundamentally does not depend on things.'*"

Zhuangzi wore coarse cloth patched with hemp, tied his shoes with plain straps, and passed by the King of Wei. The king said, "Why is the Master so weary?" Zhuangzi replied, "It is poverty, not weariness. A scholar possesses the Dao and virtue yet cannot put them into practice—*that* is weariness. Worn clothes and threadbare shoes are poverty, not weariness. This is what is called not encountering the right time. Has Your Majesty never seen the leaping monkeys? When they find themselves among nanmu, catalpa, and camphor trees, they grasp the vines and swing through the branches, reigning as kings among them—even Yi or Peng Meng could not take their eyes off them. Yet when they find themselves among thorny brambles, zhe trees, and bitter orange thickets, they tread cautiously, glancing sideways, trembling and quaking. This is not because their sinews and bones have suddenly stiffened and lost their suppleness, but because their circumstances are unfavorable, and they cannot fully display their abilities. Now, living among benighted rulers and chaotic ministers, how can one avoid weariness? This is like the warning of Bi Gan’s heart being carved out!"

Confucius was stranded between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food. Leaning on a withered tree with his left hand and striking its dry branches with his right, he sang the air of the *Biao* clan. There was the structure of the melody but not its precise measures, the sound but not its proper modes. Yet the sound and the human voice together plowed deeply into the hearts of those who heard. Yan Hui stood with hands folded, turning his gaze to observe. Fearing that Yan Hui might overreach in emulating him and thus court greatness, or cherish himself and thus invite sorrow, Confucius said, "Hui, to bear the harm of Heaven is easy; to bear the benefit of man is hard. There is no beginning that is not an end—this is the way of man and Heaven. Who, then, is the one singing now?"

Yan Hui said, "May I ask why bearing the harm of Heaven is easy?" Confucius replied, "Hunger, thirst, cold, and heat—being trapped and unable to move—these are the workings of Heaven and Earth, the ebb and flow of things. To speak of going along with them is to say one moves with them. As a minister, one does not dare to turn away. If even the Way of the minister is like this, how much more so the way one awaits Heaven?"

"‘What does it mean that bearing the benefit of man is hard?’"

Confucius replied, "At first, one is employed in all four directions, and ranks and emoluments arrive without end. What things deem profitable is not one’s own—my fate lies beyond me. The noble man does not steal, the worthy does not pilfer; if I were to take these, what would I be? Thus it is said: no bird is wiser than the swallow. Where its eyes ought not to alight, it glances swiftly, and though the fruit falls, it abandons it and flees. Fearing men, it yet nests among them. The altars of soil and grain endure—this alone!

"‘What does it mean that there is no beginning that is not an end?’"

The Master said, "Transforming the myriad things yet not knowing who succeeds whom—how can one know its end? How can one know its beginning? One simply aligns oneself and awaits it.

"‘What does it mean that man and Heaven are one?’"

Confucius replied, "There is man—this is Heaven; there is Heaven—this, too, is Heaven. Man cannot possess it—such is his nature. The sage, at ease, embodies the passing and comes to rest."

Zhuang Zhou roamed the thickets of Diao Ling when he saw a strange magpie coming from the south. Its wings spanned seven feet, its eyes measured a full inch, and it brushed his forehead before alighting in the chestnut grove. Zhuang Zhou said, "What bird is this! Its wings are vast yet it does not fly, its eyes are large yet it does not see." Hitching up his robes, he strode forward, bow in hand, and waited.

He saw a cicada, basking in the fine shade and forgetting its own body. A mantis, seizing the opportunity, struck at it, seeing only its gain and forgetting its form. The strange magpie, in turn, seized upon this, seeing only its advantage and forgetting its true nature. Zhuang Zhou started and said, "Ah! Things indeed entangle one another—two kinds summoning each other’s doom." He cast aside his bow and turned to flee, but the warden of the grove pursued him.

Zhuang Zhou returned home and for three days remained withdrawn. Lin Ju followed and asked him, "Master, why have you been so unsettled these past days?" Zhuang Zhou replied, "I guarded my form yet forgot my body; I gazed upon muddied waters and became lost in the clear abyss. Moreover, I have heard the Master say, ‘When entering their customs, follow their customs.’ Today, I wandered in Diao Ling and forgot myself; the strange magpie struck my forehead, and I wandered in the chestnut grove and forgot my true nature. The warden of the grove took me for a thief—this is why I have withdrawn."

Yangzi traveled to Song and lodged at an inn.

At the inn, the innkeeper had two concubines—one beautiful, the other plain. The plain one was favored, while the beautiful one was slighted. Yangzi asked the reason, and the innkeeper’s young servant replied, “The beautiful one is beautiful in her own eyes, but I do not see her beauty; the plain one is plain in her own eyes, but I do not see her plainness.”

Yangzi said, “Disciples, take note: act with worthiness yet abandon the conduct of self-regard—wherever you go, will you not be beloved?”

---

*Chapter 21, "Tian Zifang," Book Seven (Part Two)*

Tian Zifang sat in attendance upon Marquis Wen of Wei and repeatedly praised Xi Gong. The Marquis said, “Is Xi Gong your teacher?”

Zifang replied, “No, he is merely a villager from Wuze. He speaks of the Dao with such apt frequency that Wuze praises him.”

The Marquis said, “Then have you no teacher?”

Zifang said, “I do.”

The Marquis asked, “Who is your teacher?”

Zifang said, “Dongguo Shunzi.”

The Marquis said, “Then why have you never spoken of him?”

Zifang said, “His way of being is true. His appearance is human, yet his mind is empty as heaven; he follows things while preserving his authenticity, pure yet accommodating all. When things are absent, he responds with proper bearing to awaken them, dissolving the intentions of others. How could Wuze be worthy to speak of him?”

Zifang departed, and the Marquis was left in a state of wonder, remaining silent for the rest of the day. He stood before his ministers and said, “How distant indeed is the gentleman of perfect virtue! I once believed that the words of sagely wisdom and the conduct of benevolence and righteousness were the ultimate. But now, hearing of Zifang’s teacher, my form feels unraveled and unwilling to move, my mouth clamped shut and unwilling to speak. What I have learned is nothing but clods of earth! Truly, Wei has become a burden to me!”

---

Wenbo Xuezi traveled to Qi and lodged in Lu.

A man of Lu requested an audience with him, but Wenbo Xuezi said, “It cannot be. I have heard that the gentlemen of this state are enlightened in ritual and propriety yet coarse in understanding the human heart. I do not wish to meet him.”

When he arrived in Qi and returned to lodge in Lu, the same man again requested an audience. Wenbo Xuezi said, “Before, he sought to see me, and now he seeks to see me again—he must have some way to stir me.” He went out to meet the guest, then returned inside. The next day, he met the guest again, then returned and sighed. His servant asked, “Every time you meet this guest, you return and sigh. Why is that?”

He replied, “I had already warned you: ‘The people of the Central States are enlightened in ritual and propriety yet coarse in understanding the human heart.’ The one who came to see me before withdrew, forming his compass and setting his square, moving with the ease of a dragon or a tiger. His admonitions to me were like those of a son, his guidance like that of a father—this is why I sighed.”

Confucius met him but did not speak. Zilu said, “You have long wished to see Wenbo Xuezi. Now that you have met him, why do you not speak?”

Confucius replied, “In the presence of such a man, the Way is present the moment one’s gaze falls upon him—how could words even find room?”

Yan Yuan asked Confucius, “When you walk, I walk; when you hasten, I hasten; when you run, I run; when you race ahead, leaving dust in your wake, I can only stare blankly behind. Why is this?”

Confucius said, “Hui, what do you mean?”

Yan Yuan replied, “When you walk, I walk; when you speak, I speak; when you hasten, I hasten; when you debate, I debate; when you run, I run; when you discourse on the Way, I discourse on the Way. Yet when you race ahead, leaving all behind, I can only stare blankly—because you do not speak yet command trust, do not align yourself yet encompass all, bear no vessel yet the people surge before you. And this is simply how it is.”

Confucius said, “Alas! How could one fail to examine this? There is no greater sorrow than the death of the heart—yet the death of the body is only secondary to it.”

The sun reaches its zenith and sinks into the westernmost edge—all things align themselves accordingly. Those with eyes and feet depend on it to achieve their ends. When it rises, they exist; when it sets, they too enter rest. All things are thus: some wait for it to die, some wait for it to live. I, having received my completed form, do not transform but await its end. I emulate things, moving through day and night without pause, yet know not where it all concludes. My form takes shape as if by steaming, yet even knowing fate cannot constrain what lies before me. I, Qiu, daily dwindle thus. To grasp you for but an instant and then lose you—is this not sorrow? You cling to what I cling to. That has already vanished, yet you seek it as if it still exists—this is like searching for a horse in an empty market. I have so thoroughly forgotten you, and you have so thoroughly forgotten me. Yet why should you fret? Though you may resemble the old me, there remains something I have not forgotten."

Confucius visited Lao Dan, who had just bathed and was letting his hair loose to dry, motionless as if not quite human. Confucius withdrew and waited. When he was granted an audience, he said, "Am I deluded? Or is this truly so? Just now, Master, your form stood like withered wood, as if you had left all things and humanity behind, standing alone."

Lao Dan replied, "I let my heart wander in the beginning of things."

Confucius asked, "What do you mean?"

Lao Dan said, "The heart is confounded and cannot know; the mouth gapes and cannot speak. Let me try to discuss it with you. The utmost yin is solemn and still; the utmost yang blazes and surges. The solemn stillness emerges from heaven; the blazing surge arises from earth. The two intermingle, harmonize, and things are born. Some serve as their guiding principle, yet none see its form. They wax and wane, fill and empty, darken and brighten, daily transform and monthly change—all have their functions, yet none see their workings. Life has its sprouting; death has its return. Beginning and end revolve without end, yet none know their source. If not this, then what else could be their origin?"

Confucius said, "May I ask about wandering in this?"

Lao Dan replied, "To attain this is the utmost beauty and joy. To attain the utmost beauty and wander in the utmost joy—this is called the Perfected Person."

Confucius said, "I wish to hear of it."

Lao Dan said, "Beasts that eat grass do not fret over changing marshes; creatures that live in water do not fret over changing streams. They undergo small changes yet do not lose their great constancy. Joy and sorrow do not enter their breasts. The world is where the myriad things are one. To attain this oneness and be at one with it—then your four limbs and hundred bones will be but dust and grime, and life and death, beginning and end will be like day and night, unable to disturb you. How much less could gain and loss, fortune and misfortune affect you! Those who discard things are like those who discard mud and mire, knowing the body is more precious than a mere servant. The precious lies within me and does not lose itself to change. Moreover, the myriad transformations have never had an end—what could trouble the heart? Those who have mastered the Way understand this."

Confucius said, "Master, your virtue matches heaven and earth, yet you still borrow the finest words to cultivate it. Of the ancients, who could escape this?"

Lao Dan said, "Not so. Water, when it ripples, does nothing yet naturally displays its talent. Humans, in their virtue, do not cultivate it, yet things cannot depart from it. Like heaven’s natural height, earth’s natural depth, the sun and moon’s natural brightness—what cultivation is needed?"

Confucius left and told Yan Hui, "In the Way, I am like a vinegar fly! Were it not for the Master’s lifting of my covering, I would not have known the great completeness of heaven and earth."

Zhuangzi visited Duke Ai of Lu.

Duke Ai said, "Lu has many Confucian scholars, yet few follow your teachings."

Zhuangzi said, "Lu has *Ru* scholars."

Duke Ai said, "The entire state of Lu wears the Ru garb—how can you say there are few?"

Zhuangzi said, "I have heard that Ru scholars who wear the round cap understand the seasons; those who wear square-toed shoes understand the earth’s forms; those who wear loose jade pendants decide matters as they arise. A noble person who possesses the Way may not wear its garb; one who wears its garb may not know the Way. If Your Grace truly doubts this, proclaim throughout the state: ‘Those who lack this Way yet wear its garb shall be put to death!’"

Thereupon, Duke Ai issued the decree for five days, and no one in Lu dared wear the Ru garb. Only one man, clad in Ru attire, stood at the palace gate. The Duke summoned him and questioned him on state affairs, testing him with a thousand turns and ten thousand shifts, yet he was never at a loss.

Zhuangzi said, "With the whole of Lu, there is but one Ru scholar—can this be called many?"

Baili Xi cared nothing for rank or salary, so when he fed cattle, the cattle grew fat. This made Duke Mu of Qin forget his lowly status and entrust him with governance. One who is unmoved by life or death can thus move others.

The Lord of Song Yuan was about to have paintings made. All the scribes arrived, bowed in reception, and stood ready, licking their brushes and mixing ink, with half still to come. One scribe arrived late, sauntering without haste. He received the bow but did not stand; instead, he went to his quarters. The Duke sent someone to observe him, and there he was, stripped to the waist, legs sprawled. The Duke said, "He will do—this is a true painter."

King Wen was inspecting Zang when he saw an old man fishing, yet his fishing was no fishing. He did not hold his rod as one who fishes, but fished as a matter of course. The King wished to raise him and entrust him with governance, but feared his great ministers and elders would not accept it. He wished to leave him be, yet could not bear the thought of the people being without heaven’s protection.

Then he addressed his ministers, saying: "Formerly, I dreamed of a worthy man—dark-complexioned and bearded—riding a dappled horse with one crimson hoof, who cried out: *'Entrust your governance to this elder, and perhaps the people may yet find relief!'*"

The ministers started in alarm and said: "It was our late lord, the former king."

King Wen said: "Then let us divine."

The ministers replied: "The command of the former king leaves no room for doubt—what need is there for divination?"

Thereupon they welcomed the elder from Zang and entrusted him with governance. No statutes were established; no decrees were issued. After three years, King Wen surveyed the realm and found: the knights had abandoned their ranks and scattered like herds; officials of high rank failed to cultivate virtue; and grain measures dared not cross the four borders. When knights abandon their ranks and scatter, it is because they esteem unity; when high officials fail to cultivate virtue, it is because they share a common purpose; when grain measures dare not enter the four borders, it is because the feudal lords harbor no divided loyalties.

King Wen then made him Grand Preceptor. Facing north, he asked: "Can governance extend to all under heaven?"

The elder remained silent and gave no answer, then vaguely declined, issuing orders by morning and fleeing by night, never to be heard of again.

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "Was King Wen still lacking? Why then did he rely on a dream?"

Confucius said: "Hush! Say no more! King Wen had already perfected it—why criticize further? He merely followed the moment's necessity."

Lie Yukou drew his bow for Bohun Wuren, pulling it to full draw. He placed a cup of water on his elbow and loosed the arrow—each shaft followed the last, each arrow found its mark. In that moment, he was like a wooden figure.

Bohun Wuren said: "This is the archery of shooting, not the archery of non-shooting. Suppose I were to climb a high mountain with you, tread upon perilous stones, and stand at the edge of a hundred-fathom abyss—could you shoot then?"

Thereupon, Bohun Wuren ascended the high mountain, trod upon the perilous stones, and stood at the brink of the abyss. Turning his back to the void, with two parts of his feet dangling over the edge, he beckoned Lie Yukou forward. Lie Yukou prostrated himself upon the ground, sweat streaming down to his heels.

Bohun Wuren said: "The perfected person gazes into the azure sky, descends into the yellow springs, roams to the eight limits, yet their spirit and qi remain unchanged. Now you tremble with the intent of a startled eye—how can you hope to hit the mark?"

Jian Wu asked Sunshu Ao: "You have thrice served as Prime Minister without pride, and thrice been dismissed without sorrow. At first, I doubted, but now I see your countenance radiant—how do you cultivate your heart-mind?"

Sunshu Ao said: "How do I surpass others? I know that what comes may be refused, but what departs cannot be halted. I regard gain and loss as no concern of mine, and so I show no sorrow. How do I surpass others? I do not know whether it lies with them or with me. If it lies with them, it is nothing to me; if it lies with me, it is nothing to them. I am ever in hesitation, ever looking about—how could I have leisure to consider the nobility or baseness of others?"

Confucius heard of this and said: "The true persons of old could not be swayed by the wise, nor seduced by the beautiful, nor coerced by others, nor befriended by Fuxi or the Yellow Emperor. Life and death are great matters, yet they wrought no change in themselves—how much less would rank and emolument! Such persons traverse the great mountains without obstruction, enter the deepest springs without getting wet, dwell in lowly and mean places without weariness. They fill all between heaven and earth, and though they give to others, they themselves remain replete."

The King of Chu sat with the Lord of Fan. After a while, the King’s attendants announced three times: "Fan is no more."

The Lord of Fan said: "That Fan is no more does not mean I am no more. If Fan’s demise cannot destroy my existence, then Chu’s existence cannot preserve its own. From this perspective, Fan never began, nor has Chu ever existed."

---

*Chapter 22: "Knowledge Wanders North"*

Knowledge wandered north to the Dark Waters, ascended the Hill of Hidden Hands, and there encountered Non-Action Speaking.

Knowledge said to Non-Action Speaking: "I wish to ask you: what thoughts, what reflections lead to the Dao? What dwelling, what conduct brings repose in the Dao? What following, what path attains the Dao?"

Three times Knowledge asked, yet Non-Action Speaking did not answer. Not that he refused to answer—he did not know how to answer.

Knowledge, unable to obtain an answer, returned south of the White Waters, ascended Fox Tower, and there beheld Mad Qu. Knowledge repeated his questions to Mad Qu.

Mad Qu said: "Alas! I know the answer and will tell you." But midway through speaking, he forgot what he wished to say.

Knowledge, still unable to obtain an answer, returned to the Celestial Palace and questioned the Yellow Emperor.

The Yellow Emperor said: "Only without thought, without reflection does one begin to know the Dao. Only without dwelling, without conduct does one begin to find repose in the Dao. Only without following, without path does one begin to attain the Dao."

Knowledge asked the Yellow Emperor: "You and I know this, but they do not—who is correct?"

The Yellow Emperor said: "Non-Action Speaking is right; Mad Qu approximates it. You and I never come close. The knowing do not speak; the speaking do not know. Thus the sage practices the teaching without words. The Dao cannot be conveyed; virtue cannot be reached. Benevolence may be practiced, righteousness may be diminished, ritual is mutual pretense. Therefore it is said: 'Lose the Dao and virtue follows; lose virtue and benevolence follows; lose benevolence and righteousness follows; lose righteousness and ritual follows. Ritual is the flower of the Dao and the beginning of chaos.'

Thus it is said: 'Those who practice the Dao diminish daily, diminishing further and further until they reach non-action. With non-action, there is nothing that is not done.' Now that you have become a thing, to return to the root is not easy! How simple it is for the great person!

Life is the migration of death; death is the beginning of life—who knows their regulator? Human life is the gathering of qi. When it gathers, there is life; when it scatters, there is death. If life and death are but migrations, what cause have I for sorrow? Thus all things are one. What we deem wondrous is divine and marvelous; what we deem foul is rank and rotten. Yet the rank and rotten transform into the divine and marvelous, and the divine and marvelous transform into the rank and rotten. Therefore it is said: 'The world is but a single qi.' Thus the sage values oneness."

Empty of self, Know addressed the Yellow Emperor, saying: "I questioned Non-Action, and Non-Action did not respond to me—was it not I to whom he did not respond? Or did he truly not know how to respond? I questioned Mad Qu, and Mad Qu wished to tell me but did not tell me—was it that he did not tell me? Or did he wish to tell me but forgot? Now I ask you, and you know—why then are you not near?"

The Yellow Emperor replied: "He is truly so because he does not know; this one is so because he has forgotten. You and I will never be near because we know."

Mad Qu heard this and took the Yellow Emperor to be one who understood words.

Heaven and Earth possess great beauty yet do not speak; the four seasons follow bright patterns yet do not discuss them; the myriad things attain their principles yet do not explain them. The sage mirrors the beauty of Earth and penetrates the principles of the myriad things. Thus the perfected person practices non-action, the great sage does not act—this is called observing the Dao of Heaven and Earth.

Now that numinous clarity and utmost essence—though the hundred transformations, though things die and live, square and round—none know their root. Yet all things have existed since antiquity, self-so. The Six Directions form the vast, yet they do not depart; the tip of an autumn hair is minute, yet it awaits completion to become a body. The world knows not one thing that does not rise and sink, yet all endure without permanence. Yin and yang, the four seasons revolve, each keeping to its order. As if absent yet present, effortlessly formless yet spirit-like, the myriad things are nurtured without awareness. This is called the root-source—by it one may observe Heaven!

Nie Que asked the Way of Clothed-in-Skins. Clothed-in-Skins said: "Correct your form, unify your gaze, and the harmony of Heaven will arrive. Gather in your knowledge, still your measurements, and spirit will come to dwell. Virtue will become your beauty, the Dao will become your dwelling. Your eyes will be bright as a newborn calf’s, seeking nothing of the old."

Before the words were finished, Nie Que fell into slumber. Clothed-in-Skins rejoiced greatly, singing as he departed:

"Form like withered bones,
Heart like dead ashes,
True in their actual knowing,
Not clinging to the old.
Vague and obscure,
Without mind—who can scheme with them?
What manner of person is this!"

Shun asked his minister: "Can the Dao be possessed and held?"
The minister replied: "Your body is not your own possession—how could you possess the Dao?"

Shun said: "If my body is not my own, then whose is it?"
The minister replied: "It is Heaven and Earth’s entrusted form. Life is not your possession—it is Heaven and Earth’s entrusted gift. Nature and destiny are not your possession—they are Heaven and Earth’s entrusted compliance. Children and descendants are not your possession—they are Heaven and Earth’s entrusted shedding. Thus, in walking, you know not where you go; in dwelling, you know not what you hold; in eating, you know not the taste. It is the vigorous yang qi of Heaven and Earth—how could it be possessed and held?"

Confucius asked Lao Dan: "Today I have leisure, and venture to inquire about the supreme Dao."
Lao Dan said: "Purify your rituals, dredge your channels, cleanse your spirit, and shatter your knowledge! The Dao—how profound and indescribable it is! I shall tell you its broad outlines."

The luminous arises from the obscure, order emerges from formlessness, spirit is born of the Dao, and physical form originates from essence—while the myriad things beget one another through form. Those with nine orifices are born from the womb; those with eight, from eggs. Their coming leaves no trace, their going no boundary; no gate, no chamber—yet they radiate boundlessly in all four directions. Those who wander thus possess supple limbs, penetrating insight, keen ears, and sharp eyes. Their minds do not toil, yet their responses know no limit. Heaven cannot but be high, Earth cannot but be vast; sun and moon cannot but move, and the myriad things cannot but flourish—such is the Dao!

Moreover, vast knowledge does not necessarily bring understanding, nor does eloquence guarantee wisdom. The sage has already settled this! That which, when augmented, does not increase, and when diminished, does not decrease—this is what the sage preserves. Profound as the sea, towering in its endless return to the beginning, it measures and sustains the myriad things without exhaustion. Thus the Dao of the noble person—does it lie outside these things? The myriad things all turn to it for sustenance, yet it is never depleted. Such is the Dao!

Within the Central Kingdom there is one who is neither yin nor yang, dwelling between Heaven and Earth, merely human for a time before returning to the Source. Viewed from its origin, life is but a fleeting coagulation of things. Though lifespans vary, how great is the difference? A mere instant—why debate the right and wrong of Yao and Jie? Fruits and melons have their patterns, human relations their complexities, yet these are but ways to order things. The sage encounters them without resistance, passes through them without clinging. To harmonize and respond is virtue; to coincide and respond is the Dao. From this arise emperors and kings.

Born between Heaven and Earth, one passes like a white colt through a gap—gone in an instant. Suddenly, vigorously, all things emerge; softly, smoothly, all things return. Transformed into life, then transformed into death. Living things mourn it, humankind grieves it. Yet Heaven’s quiver is unstrung, its celestial robe cast off. Twisting and turning, the soul departs, and the body follows. Thus the great return!

Not to be shaped by form, nor to shape the formless—this is what all people know, yet not what the realized strive for. This is the common discourse of the multitude. Those who have arrived do not debate; those who debate have not arrived. To perceive clearly yet find nothing to grasp, to speak without falling silent—this is not the great attainment. The Dao cannot be heard; to hear it is not as good as deafness: this is called the supreme attainment.

Dongguo Zi asked Zhuangzi: "Where does the so-called Dao reside?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is nowhere absent."
Dongguo Zi said: "You must specify."
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the ants."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why so lowly?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the weeds."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why even lower?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in the tiles."
Dongguo Zi said: "Why more extreme still?"
Zhuangzi said: "It is in excrement and urine."
Dongguo Zi fell silent.

Zhuangzi said: "Your question fundamentally misses the mark. When Zheng and Huo asked the market inspector about the weight of a pig, the answer grew more precise with each lower part. You must not insist on a fixed locus—the Dao eludes nothing. The supreme Dao is like this, and so too is grand discourse. The three terms—pervasiveness, universality, and totality—differ in name but share the same reality; their intent is one. Let us wander together in the Palace of Nothingness, merging our discussions without end! Let us practice non-action together—serene and still, vast and clear, at ease and idle! My will is already empty; I go nowhere without knowing my destination, depart and return without knowing where I stop. I have come and gone without knowing my end, roaming in boundless expanse. Great knowledge enters here, yet knows no limit.

That which shapes things is not separate from things, yet things have boundaries—these are the boundaries of what shapes them. The boundary of the unbounded is the boundary of what is not a boundary. We speak of fullness and emptiness, decline and decay, yet what is full and empty is not fullness and emptiness; what declines and decays is not decline and decay. What has root and branch is not root and branch; what accumulates and disperses is not accumulation and dispersal."

Nü Ke studied with Shen Nong under Old Dragon Ji. One day, Shen Nong was resting with his head on a table, the door closed, napping in broad daylight. Nü Ke pushed open the door and entered, saying: "Old Dragon is dead!"
Shen Nong, still leaning on the table, grasped his staff and rose. Then, letting it clatter to the ground, he laughed and said: "Heaven knew my stubbornness and sloth, so it cast me aside and let him die. Alas, Master! You have died without ever unleashing your wild words!"

Yan Gangdiao heard this and said: "The embodiment is what all gentlemen under Heaven are bound to. Now, in the Dao, one has not even attained a ten-thousandth part of the tip of an autumn hair, yet still knows to conceal one’s wild words—how much more so, then, for one who embodies the Dao! It is seen yet has no form; heard yet makes no sound. Those who discourse on it call it the obscure and impenetrable, yet what they discourse on is not the Dao."

Thereupon Tai Qing asked Wu Qiong: "Do you know the Dao?"
Wu Qiong said: "I do not know."
Then he asked Wu Wei, who said: "I know the Dao."
Tai Qing asked: "In your knowing the Dao, is there a method?"
Wu Wei said: "There is."
Tai Qing asked: "What is this method like?"
Wu Wei said: "I know the Dao can be esteemed, can be debased, can be gathered, can be dispersed—this is the method by which I know the Dao."

Tai Qing took these words and asked Wu Shi: "If this is so, then between Wu Qiong’s not-knowing and Wu Wei’s knowing, which is right and which is wrong?"
Wu Shi said: "Not-knowing is profound; knowing is shallow. Not-knowing is internal; knowing is external."

Thereupon Tai Qing sighed deeply and said: "Is not-knowing then knowing? Is knowing then not-knowing? Who knows the knowing of not-knowing?"
Wu Shi said: "The Dao cannot be heard—what is heard is not it. The Dao cannot be seen—what is seen is not it. The Dao cannot be spoken—what is spoken is not it. Do you know that the formless cannot be formed? The Dao cannot be named."

Wu said: "One who is asked about the Dao and responds does not know the Dao. Even one who asks about the Dao has not heard the Dao. The Dao admits no questions; questions admit no answers. To question what admits no questions is to question the inscrutable. To answer what admits no answers is to lack inwardness. Confronting the inscrutable with what lacks inwardness—such a one outwardly does not observe the cosmos, inwardly does not know the Great Beginning. Thus he does not pass beyond Kunlun, nor roam in the Great Void."

Guang Yao asked Wu You: "Do you exist, Master? Or do you not exist?"
Guang Yao could not frame his question, and gazed intently at Wu You’s form—vast and empty. He looked at him all day yet saw nothing; listened yet heard nothing; grasped yet could not seize him.

Guang Yao said: "This is perfection! Who could attain this? I can attain being and non-being, yet I have not attained non-being of non-being. How could one reach this from non-existence?"

The master smith of Great Horse, though eighty years old, never missed by a hair’s breadth. Great Horse said: "Is it skill, or is there a Dao to it?"

The smith replied: "I have my discipline. At twenty, I took delight in forging hooks, and to all else I was blind—nothing but hooks did I scrutinize. What I employed was the unutilized, thereby prolonging its use. How much more so for that which is never not used! What thing does not draw upon it?"

Ran Qiu asked Confucius: "Can one know what preceded heaven and earth?"
Confucius said: "One can. The past is like the present."
Ran Qiu, failing to grasp the answer, withdrew.

The next day, he returned and said: "Yesterday I asked, 'Can one know what preceded heaven and earth?' You replied, 'One can. The past is like the present.' Yesterday I saw clearly, but today I am benighted. May I ask what this means?"
Confucius said: "Yesterday’s clarity came from the spirit first receiving it. Today’s benightedness comes from seeking it with what is not spirit! There is no past, no present, no beginning, no end. Before you existed, could you have had descendants?"

Ran Qiu did not respond.
Confucius said: "Enough! No further answer is needed. Do not use life to give birth to death, nor use death to give birth to life. Do life and death depend on one another? All share a single body. Was there something that existed before heaven and earth? That which gives rise to things is not itself a thing. Things do not emerge before that which gives rise to them—yet there are things. Since there are things, there is no end. The sage’s love for others never ends, for he draws from this principle."

Yan Yuan asked Confucius: "I once heard you say, 'Do not send off, do not welcome.' I venture to wander in this."
Confucius said: "The people of old transformed outwardly but remained unchanging within. The people of today transform inwardly but remain unchanging without. Those who transform with things are one with the unchanging. How can one be at ease with change or non-change? How can one be at ease with aligning with them? One must align with them without excess.

In the gardens of the Shiwei clan, the parks of the Yellow Emperor, the halls of Lord Yu, the chambers of Tang and Wu—gentlemen, like the masters of the Ru and Mo schools, still used right and wrong to vie with one another. How much more so the people of today! The sage dwells among things without harming them. One who does not harm things cannot be harmed by them. Only one who harms nothing can send off and welcome others.

Mountains and forests! Marshes and wilds! They make me joyful and glad! But before the joy is complete, sorrow follows. When sorrow and joy come, I cannot resist them; when they depart, I cannot stop them. Alas! People of the world merely take things as transient lodgings.

They know what they encounter but not what they do not; they know what they can do but not what they cannot. Those without knowledge or ability are what people cannot avoid. To strive for what people cannot—is this not also lamentable?

Ultimate words discard words; ultimate action discards action. To equalize what knowledge knows is shallow indeed!"

**Chapter Twenty-Three: Gengsang Chu**

Among the disciples of Lao Dan was one named Gengsang Chu, who had partially grasped the Master’s Dao. He went north to dwell in the mountains of Weilei. Those among his retainers who were scheming and calculating he sent away; those among his concubines who were ostentatiously kind he kept at a distance. Only the dull and the clumsy remained with him, and the restless and the harried served him.

After three years, Weilei flourished. The people of Weilei said to one another: “When Master Gengsang first arrived, we found him strange and unsettling. Now, when we reckon our days, we find them lacking, but when we reckon our years, we find them abundant. He must be a sage! Why do you not join together to offer him sacrifices as to an ancestral spirit, to honor him as you would the gods of soil and grain?”

When Gengsang Chu heard this, he turned southward, his countenance troubled. His disciples were perplexed. Gengsang Chu said: “Why are you puzzled by me? When the spring qi stirs, the hundred grasses sprout; when autumn arrives in due season, the ten thousand treasures ripen. Are spring and autumn themselves responsible for this? The Dao of Heaven has simply run its course. I have heard that the perfected person dwells like a corpse in a walled chamber, while the common people run wild, not knowing where to turn. Now these petty folk of Weilei secretly wish to place me among the worthy, offering me sacrificial vessels. Am I truly the sort of person to be measured thus? This is why I cannot let go of the words of Lao Dan.”

His disciples said: “This is not so.”

In a mere ditch of ordinary breadth, the great fish has no room to turn its body, while the newt and loach rule there; on a hillock of but seven feet, the mighty beast cannot conceal its frame, while the wretched fox is deemed auspicious. Moreover, to honor the worthy and empower the capable, to put goodness and profit first—this has been so since the days of Yao and Shun, let alone the folk of Weilei! Master, you should heed this!”

Gengsang Chu replied: “Come here, my disciples! The beast that fills a carriage, once armored and parted from the mountains, cannot escape the snares and nets; the fish that swallows a boat, once stranded on dry land, is tormented by ants. Thus birds and beasts do not scorn the heights, nor do fish and turtles scorn the depths. For one who preserves his bodily life, to conceal himself is nothing less than to shun all that is shallow and near!

As for those two sages, what are they worth extolling? Their sophistry is like recklessly hacking at walls to plant mugwort and wormwood. Combing their hair to a fine sheen, counting grains of rice to cook—how can such petty fussing ever save the age? Where worth is exalted, the people contend; where cunning is employed, the people steal. These few things are not enough to enrich the people.

The people’s zeal for profit is extreme: sons kill fathers, ministers kill rulers; in broad daylight they rob, at midday they bore through walls. I tell you: the root of great disorder must have arisen in the age of Yao and Shun, and its branches will endure for a thousand generations. A thousand generations hence, there will surely be men who devour one another.”

Nanrong Chu straightened himself and sat upright, saying: “As for one like me, Chu, my years are already advanced—how then may I entrust myself to this learning and attain these words?”

The Master said: “Preserve your form whole, embrace your life, and let not your thoughts and cares flutter restlessly. If you do these three things for three years, then you may attain these words.”

Nanrong Chu said: “The eye and the form—I do not know their difference, yet the blind cannot see themselves. The ear and the form—I do not know their difference, yet the deaf cannot hear themselves. The heart and the form—I do not know their difference, yet the mad cannot grasp themselves. The form and the form are surely distinct, yet why do things come between them? I seek to unite them but cannot attain it. Now you tell me, Chu: ‘Preserve your form whole, embrace your life, let not your thoughts and cares flutter.’ I strain to hear the Dao, yet it only reaches my ears!”

Gengsangzi said: “My words are exhausted. The running bee cannot transform the hollyhock caterpillar; the Yue chicken cannot hatch the swan’s egg—yet the common hen can! The hen and the hen: their virtue is not different. That some can and some cannot is simply that their capacities are inherently great or small. Now my capacity is small—it is just enough to transform you. Why do you not go south to see Laozi?”

Nanrong Chu gathered provisions and, after seven days and seven nights, arrived at Laozi’s abode. Laozi said: “Have you come from the region of Chu?” Nanrong Chu replied: “Yes.” Laozi said: “Why have you brought so many people with you?” Nanrong Chu looked around in alarm. Laozi said: “Do you not understand what I meant?” Nanrong Chu bowed his head in shame, then raised it and sighed: “Now I have forgotten my answer to your question.”

Laozi said: “What do you mean?” Nanrong Chu said: “If I do not know, people call me foolish. If I know, I am filled with sorrow. Without ren, I harm others; with ren, I bring sorrow upon myself. Without yi, I injure others; with yi, I bring sorrow upon myself. How can I escape this? These words are what trouble me, Chu. I wish to ask you through the example of Chu.”

Laozi said: “Just now, I saw it in the space between your brows and lashes—I had already grasped you. Now you speak again and confirm it. You are like one who has lost his parents, raising a pole to search the sea. You are a lost man—how bewildered! You wish to return to your true nature but have no way in. How pitiful!”

Nanrong Chu asked to enter and rest in a dwelling. He summoned what he loved and cast aside what he hated. For ten days he brooded, then saw Laozi again. Laozi said: “You have cleansed yourself—how deeply troubled you still are! Yet within, there still lingers something foul. Those bound by external constraints cannot be grasped by force; they must be inwardly restrained. Those bound by internal constraints cannot be seized by deception; they must be outwardly restrained. Those bound by both internal and external constraints—even the power of the Dao and virtue cannot uphold them, let alone those who wander beyond the Dao!”

Nanrong Chu said: “When a villager is ill, another villager asks after him. If the sick one can describe his illness, then though he speaks of it, his illness is as if it were not an illness. As for my hearing of the great Dao, it is like taking medicine that only worsens the sickness. I wish only to hear the classic of preserving life.”

Laozi said: “The classic of preserving life—can you embrace the One? Can you not lose it? Can you know good and ill fortune without divination? Can you stop? Can you cease? Can you set aside what others seek and seek it within yourself? Can you be free and unconstrained? Can you be open and unassuming? Can you be like a child? A child wails all day yet its throat does not grow hoarse—such is the perfection of harmony. It clutches all day yet its hand does not cramp—such is the sharing of its power. It gazes all day yet its eyes do not blink—its focus is not on externals. It walks without knowing where it goes, dwells without knowing what it does, moves with things in perfect accord, riding their waves. This is the classic of preserving life.”

Nanrong Chu said: “Then is this the virtue of the perfected person?” Laozi said: “It is not.”

What is called the thawing of ice—can you do this? The perfected person shares sustenance with the earth and rejoices with heaven, untouched by the gains and harms of others, not given to marveling, scheming, or contriving. They come and go with effortless grace, open and unassuming. This is the classic of preserving life.”

Nanrong Chu said: “Then is this perfection itself?”
Laozi said: “It is not. I have already told you: ‘Can you be like a child?’ A child moves without knowing what it does, walks without knowing where it goes. Its body is like a withered branch, its heart like dead ashes. In this state, misfortune does not arrive, nor does fortune. With neither misfortune nor fortune, how could there be calamity?”

When the mind is settled in great peace, it radiates the light of heaven. One who radiates this light is seen as human. Those who cultivate themselves now possess constancy. Constancy is what others abandon and heaven aids. What people abandon is called the people of heaven; what heaven aids is called the son of heaven.

The learner studies what cannot be learned; the doer acts in what cannot be acted; the debater argues what cannot be argued. To know when to stop at what cannot be known—this is the utmost. If there is any who does not conform to this, the balance of heaven will undo them.

Prepare things to sustain the form, conceal the unforeseen to nurture the mind, and honor the center to reach the other. If, despite this, myriad evils still arrive, they are from heaven, not from man—they cannot disturb completion or enter the sacred chamber. The sacred chamber has its support, yet one does not know what supports it, nor can it be grasped.

When sincerity stirs within and manifests, each manifestation may be amiss. Once karma takes hold, it does not release, and with each turn, one strays further. To do evil in the light is for people to condemn; to do evil in the dark is for ghosts to condemn. Only one who is clear about people and clear about ghosts can walk alone.

Within the contract, act without name; beyond the contract, aspire to costly expectations. Acting without name is the way of the common; aspiring to costly expectations is the way of the merchant. People see their striving and deem it grand. Yet one who exhausts things is entered by things; one who contends with things cannot even contain themselves—how could they contain others? One who cannot contain others has no kinship; one with no kinship is forsaken by all.

No weapon is sharper than the will; even the finest blade is inferior. No foe is greater than yin and yang—there is no escape from them between heaven and earth. It is not yin and yang that harm, but the heart that employs them.

The Dao flows—its division is its completion, its completion is its undoing. What is despised in division is that it leads to preparation; what is despised in preparation is that it implies possession. To depart and not return is to become a ghost; to depart and gain is to gain death. To vanish yet retain substance is one aspect of ghosts. By using the formed to model the formless, one may attain certainty!

To emerge without origin, to enter without aperture—possessing substance yet no place, having length yet no root or tip. That which emerges without aperture possesses substance. Possessing substance yet no place is space; having length yet no root or tip is time.

There is existence, there is death; there is existence, there is entry. To enter and exit without revealing form is called the Gate of Heaven. The Gate of Heaven is non-being—all things emerge from non-being. Being does not take being as being; it must emerge from non-being, and non-being itself is not a non-being. The sage conceals himself herein.

The ancients—how far their knowledge reached! Where did it reach? Some held that there had never yet been anything—this is the utmost, the limit, beyond which nothing can be added. Next were those who acknowledged things, seeing life as loss and death as return—thus they made distinctions. Then came those who said that at first there was nothing, then suddenly there was life, and life swiftly turned to death. They took non-being as the head, life as the body, and death as the tail. Whoever knows the unity of existence, non-existence, death, and life—I befriend them. Though these three differ, they belong to the same clan.

The Zhaos and Jings were illustrious and honored; the Jia clan was illustrious and enfeoffed—yet they were not one. There is life, yet it is said to shift. I have spoken of this shifting, but it is not what I meant. Still, it cannot be known.

The sacrificial meats of the winter sacrifice can be scattered, yet they cannot be scattered. The observer of a chamber circles the ancestral temple, then proceeds to the private quarters—this is called "shifting the this." Let me try to speak of this shifting. Taking life as the root, knowledge as the teacher, one rides upon right and wrong. If there is indeed substance, one uses oneself as the measure, making others conform to one’s standards and even dying to uphold them. Such a one takes utility as wisdom, non-utility as folly, thoroughness as fame, and failure as disgrace. This shifting is the way of people today—like the cicada and the fledgling bird, they are the same in their sameness.

To tread on a market-goer’s foot, one apologizes with haughty words; to a brother, with gentle ones; to a parent, one says nothing at all. Thus it is said: The utmost ritual has no "no"; the utmost righteousness does not treat things as things; the utmost knowledge does not scheme; the utmost benevolence has no kinship; the utmost trust dispenses with metal.

Dispel the turbulence of the will, untangle the knots of the heart, cast off the burdens of virtue, and clear the blockages of the Dao. Wealth, honor, prominence, prestige, fame, and profit—these six are the turbulence of the will. Appearance, movement, complexion, pattern, qi, and intent—these six are the knots of the heart. Aversion, desire, joy, anger, sorrow, and delight—these six are the burdens of virtue. Withdrawal, approach, taking, giving, knowledge, and ability—these six are the blockages. When these four sets of six no longer churn in the breast, one is upright; upright, one is still; still, one is clear; clear, one is empty; empty, one does nothing yet nothing is left undone.

The Dao is the reverence of virtue; life is the vessel of virtue; nature is the essence of life.

The movement of nature is called *wei*; the artificiality of *wei* is called loss.

Knowledge is contact; wisdom is planning. What the wise do not know is like a sidelong glance.

Movement from inevitability is called virtue; movement without self is governance. Names oppose each other, yet realities align.

Yi excelled at hitting the smallest mark but was clumsy at preventing others from praising him. The sage excels at Heaven but is clumsy with people. Only the whole person can excel at Heaven while being adept with people.

Though creatures may act as creatures, they act as Heaven. Does the whole person despise Heaven? Do they despise the Heaven in people? How much more, then, the Heaven in oneself and others!

When a sparrow comes to Yi, Yi must catch it—this is fate. If one takes the world as a cage, no sparrow can escape. Thus, Tang caged Yi Yin with human means, and Duke Mu of Qin caged Bai Li Xi with five sheepskins. Therefore, nothing can be caged except by means of what it loves.

The cripple draws boundaries, indifferent to external praise or blame. The convict climbs high without fear, having forgotten life and death. One who recovers from stumbling without resentment has forgotten others; forgetting others, one thereby becomes Heaven.

Thus, one who is revered yet feels no joy, or insulted yet feels no anger—only one in harmony with Heaven’s accord is so. Anger arises from non-anger; action arises from non-action.

To still the mind, calm the qi; to be spirit-like, follow the heart. In action, to be fitting is to act from inevitability. The way of the sage lies in the category of inevitability.

---

**Chapter Twenty-Four: Xu Wu Gui** *(from Book Eight, "Middle")*

Xu Wu Gui, through Nü Shang, was received by Marquis Wu of Wei. The marquis greeted him, saying, "Master, you must be weary—exhausted by the toils of mountain and forest, and thus willing to meet with me."

Xu Wu Gui replied, "It is I who weary you, my lord—what weariness have you for me? If you indulge your desires and prolong your likes and dislikes, the natural emotions of your nature will sicken. If you suppress your desires and cast off likes and dislikes, your ears and eyes will sicken. I weary you, my lord—what weariness have you for me?"

The marquis, startled, made no reply. After a moment, Xu Wu Gui continued, "Let me tell you, my lord—I read the signs in dogs. The inferior kind stops when its belly is full—this is the virtue of the wildcat..."

The superior kind acts as though it has lost its singular purpose. I read the signs in dogs, yet this is not to be compared with how I read the signs in horses. When I read the signs in horses: the straight aligns with the marking-line, the curved fits the hook, the square meets the carpenter’s square, the round matches the compass—these are the horses of the state, yet they do not compare to the horses of the world. The horses of the world possess a perfected nature: as though grieving, as though lost, as though they have forgotten their singular purpose. Such are they who transcend the dust, leaving no trace of their passing."

Marquis Wu was greatly pleased and laughed. When Xu Wu Gui departed, Nü Shang asked, "Master, how did you alone delight our lord? When I seek to please him, I expound horizontally with the *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*, or I expound vertically with the *Golden Tablets* and *Six Strategies*. Those who serve him with great merit are beyond counting, yet our lord has never once parted his lips in response. How is it that you have delighted him so?"

Xu Wu Gui replied, "I simply told him of how I read the signs in dogs and horses."

Nü Shang said, "Is that all?"

Xu Wu Gui continued, "Have you not heard of the exiles of Yue? When they leave their state for a few days, they rejoice at seeing those they know. When they leave for a month, they rejoice at seeing those they once saw in their state. After a year, they rejoice even at seeing those who merely resemble people. Is it not true that the longer one is parted from others, the deeper the longing grows? Those who flee into the emptiness, who wander the paths of foxes and weasels among the brambles and thorns, stumbling through the void—when they hear the sound of human footsteps, they rejoice. How much more so for the voices and sighs of brothers and kin at their side! How long has it been since the words of a true person have sighed at our lord’s side?"

Xu Wu Gui was received by Marquis Wu, who said, "Sir, you dwell in the mountains and forests, eating acorns and chestnuts, weary of scallions and leeks, yet you deign to visit this lonely one. Are you now aged? Do you crave the taste of wine and meat? Or does this lonely one possess some blessing of state and altars?"

Xu Wu Gui replied, "Wu Gui was born in poverty and has never dared to partake of your lordship’s wine and meat. I have come to offer you labor."

The Marquis said, "What labor? How would you labor this lonely one?"

Xu Wu Gui answered, "To labor your spirit and form."

Marquis Wu said, "What do you mean?"

Xu Wu Gui replied, "The nurturance of Heaven and Earth is one—ascending high does not make one tall, nor descending low make one short. Yet you alone, as lord of ten thousand chariots, afflict the people of your state to nourish your ears, eyes, nose, and mouth. The spirit does not act for itself. The spirit loves harmony and detests perversity. Perversity is sickness, and thus it labors you. Why do you alone suffer from this sickness?"

Marquis Wu said, "I have longed for you, sir! I wish to cherish the people and practice righteousness by laying down arms. Is this possible?"

Xu Wu Gui said, "It is not. To cherish the people is to harm them; to practice righteousness and lay down arms is to plant the root of warfare. If you proceed thus, you will scarcely succeed. All perfected beauty is a baleful instrument. Though you act with righteousness, you will verge on falsity! Form inevitably begets form; completion inevitably invites attack; change inevitably provokes external strife. You must not array your finest cranes between the towering watchtowers. Do not stable your swiftest steeds in the palace of carved eaves. Do not harbor treachery in success! Do not seek to overcome others with skill, nor with strategy, nor with war. Those who slay others, who seize others’ lands to nourish their own desires and spirits—how can one know which side is good in such strife? And what is the good of victory? If you must persist, then cultivate sincerity within your breast to respond to the feelings of Heaven and Earth, and do not disturb them. The people are already freed from death—why then would you lay down arms?

When the Yellow Emperor sought to meet the Great Hidden One on Mount Juci, Fang Ming drove his chariot, Chang Yu rode at his side, Zhang Ruo and Xi Peng led the horses, and Kun Gu Ji followed in the rear carriage. When they reached the wilds of Xiangcheng, the Seven Sages all lost their way and had nowhere to ask directions. They happened upon a boy herding horses and asked him the way. 'Do you know Mount Juci?' they said. 'I do,' he replied. 'Do you know where the Great Hidden One dwells?'"

The boy said, "I do." The Yellow Emperor said, "How extraordinary, this little boy! Not only do you know Mount Juci, but you also know where the Great Hidden One dwells. I beg to ask you about governing the world." The boy replied, "Governing the world is just like this—what more is there to do? When I was young, I wandered freely within the six directions. Then I fell ill with dizziness, and an elder taught me, saying, 'Ride the chariot of the sun and wander the wilds of Xiangcheng.' Now that my illness has slightly improved, I am about to wander again beyond the six directions. Governing the world is just like this—what more is there to do?"

The Yellow Emperor said, "Governing Heaven may indeed not be your concern, yet still, I beg to ask about governing the world." The boy declined. The Yellow Emperor asked again. The boy said, "What makes governing the world different from herding horses? Simply remove what harms them—that is all." The Yellow Emperor bowed twice, touching his forehead to the ground, and called him "Heavenly Master" before withdrawing.

Scholars who lack the changes of thought find no joy; debaters who lack the order of discourse find no joy; inspectors who lack the task of reproach—all are confined by things. Those who summon the age serve the court; those of middling ability hold office with honor. The strong boast of overcoming difficulties; the brave rush to meet calamity; soldiers delight in war; the withered cling to fame; legalists expand governance; ritualists refine their demeanor; the noble of benevolence and righteousness seek connections.

Farmers without the task of clearing wild land do not thrive; merchants without the bustle of the marketplace do not thrive. Commoners with daily work are diligent; those skilled in tools and machines are robust. Those who hoard no wealth worry the greedy; those who lack prominence grieve the boastful. Followers of power and things delight in change. When the times call for use, they cannot be without action—all these align with the seasons, not with the constancy of transformation. They exhaust their nature and form, sinking into the ten thousand things, never to return.

Zhuangzi said, "If an archer hits the mark without aiming beforehand, is he a good shot? If so, then everyone in the world is Yi—is that acceptable?" Huizi said nothing. Zhuangzi continued, "The world has no shared truth, and each affirms what they affirm. If so, then everyone in the world is Yao—is that acceptable?" Huizi said nothing.

Zhuangzi said, "Then among the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists—five in all with you—who is truly right? Or is it like Lu Ju? His disciple said, 'I have grasped the Master’s Way! I can boil a cauldron in winter and make ice in summer!' Lu Ju replied, 'This merely summons yang with yang and yin with yin—it is not what I call the Way. Let me show you my Way.' Thereupon, he tuned two zithers, placing one in the hall and one in the chamber. When the *gong* note was struck, the *jiao* note resonated; when the *jue* note was struck, the *jiao* note responded—their tones harmonized. But if one string were adjusted so that it no longer fit the five notes, and all twenty-five strings were struck, the sounds would not differ, yet the ruler of tones would already be present. Is it like this?"

Huizi said, "Now the Confucians, Mohists, Yangists, and Bingists all debate, clashing with words and overpowering with voices, yet none admit they are wrong—how is this?" Zhuangzi said, "A man of Qi sent his son to Song to serve as a gatekeeper, but did not provide him with proper provisions. When he sought a bronze bell, he bound it; when he sought Tang’s son, he never left the domain—such are the remnants of folly! A man sent as a gatekeeper to lodge in another’s home, who in the dead of night fights with the boatman when no one is around, has not yet left the shore yet stirs up resentment."

Zhuangzi was attending a funeral and passed by Huizi’s grave. Turning to his followers, he said, "A man of Ying had a speck of plaster on the tip of his nose no thicker than a fly’s wing. He called a craftsman to remove it. The craftsman swung his axe with the force of the wind, listening as he struck, removing all the plaster without harming the nose. The man of Ying stood unmoved. When the Lord of Song Yuan heard of this, he summoned the craftsman and said, 'Try doing it for me.' The craftsman replied, 'Your servant once could do this, but the material for my skill has long been dead.' Since Huizi’s death, I have had no material for my skill, no one to speak with."

Guan Zhong fell ill, and Duke Huan asked him, "Your illness is grave. If I may ask, when it reaches its worst, to whom should I entrust the state?" Guan Zhong said, "Whom does the Duke wish to appoint?" The Duke said, "Bao Shuya." Guan Zhong replied, "He is not suitable. He is a man of purity and integrity, a good scholar. Those who are not like him, he does not associate with. Moreover, once he hears of another’s fault, he never forgets it. If you appoint him to govern, above he will probe the ruler, and below he will oppose the people. It will not be long before he offends you."

The Duke said, "Then who is suitable?" Guan Zhong replied, "If you must choose, then Xi Peng is suitable. His character is such that he forgets those above and distances himself from those below. He feels ashamed that he is not like the Yellow Emperor and pities those who are not like himself. To share virtue with others is called sageliness; to share wealth with others is called worthiness. To rule others with worthiness never wins their hearts; to humble oneself with worthiness never fails to win them. In governing the state, he does not hear what he should not; in managing his household, he does not see what he should not. If you must choose, then Xi Peng is suitable."

The King of Wu floated upon the river and ascended the Mountain of the Macaques. The macaques, seeing him, in alarm abandoned their perches and fled, vanishing into the dense thickets. One, however, slithered and scratched, displaying its cunning before the king. The king shot at it, but the macaque nimbly caught the swift arrow. The king then ordered his attendants to hasten and shoot it, and the macaque was seized and killed. The king turned to his minister Yan Buyi and said, "This macaque, priding itself on its skill and relying on its agility, dared to mock me—thus it met its doom. Take heed! Alas, never let your demeanor make others proud."

Yan Buyi returned home and took Dong Wu as his teacher. He uprooted his arrogance, abandoned pleasure, and declined honors. After three years, the people of the state praised him.

Nanbo Ziqi leaned upon his armrest and sat, gazing upward and sighing. Yan Chengzi entered and said, "Master, you are the most extraordinary of beings—can your form be made like withered bones, and can your heart truly be made like dead ashes?"

He replied, "Once I dwelled in a mountain cave. At that time, when the fields and crops caught a single glimpse of me, the multitudes of Qi all thrice congratulated one another."

I must first possess it, and thus they know it; I must first offer it for sale, and thus they sell it. If I do not possess it, how can they know it? If I do not sell it, how can they peddle it? Alas! I grieve for those who lose themselves, and I grieve yet more for those who grieve for them. And I grieve still further for those who grieve over the grief of others—ever receding into the distance!

When Confucius traveled to Chu, the king of Chu offered him wine. Sunshu Ao stood holding the goblet, while Shinan Yiliao received the wine and made a libation, saying, "How ancient are these words!" Then he said, "I, Qiu, have heard of the speech that is without words, yet have never spoken it—until now. Shinan Yiliao juggles balls, a puzzle hard to unravel; Sunshu Ao sleeps sweetly, holding a feather fan, while the people of Ying lay down their weapons. I wish I had a beak three feet long!"

That is called the Dao that is not the Dao, the eloquence that is without words. Thus virtue gathers in the unity of the Dao, and speech rests where knowledge cannot reach—this is perfection. What the Dao unifies, virtue shares; what knowledge cannot know, eloquence cannot express. Names like "Confucian" or "Mohist" bring only misfortune.

Thus the sea does not refuse the eastward flow—this is the utmost in greatness. A person embraces heaven and earth, bestowing blessings upon the world, yet none know their name. Therefore, in life they bear no rank, in death no posthumous title; they accumulate no wealth, nor do they seek fame—this is called the Great Person. A dog is not good because it barks well, nor is a person worthy because they speak well—how much less so for the great! To be great is not enough to be called great, how much less so for virtue! Yet greatness is complete—none surpasses heaven and earth. But what do they seek? And yet they are complete!

Those who know completeness seek nothing, lose nothing, discard nothing, and do not exchange themselves for things. Turning inward, they are inexhaustible; following the ancient, they do not wear away—this is the way of the Great Person!

Ziqi had eight sons. He lined them up before him and summoned Jiufang Yin, saying, "Examine my sons for me and tell me which is most auspicious." Jiufang Yin replied, "Kun is auspicious." Ziqi started in surprise and joy, asking, "In what way?" He replied, "Kun will share the ruler’s table for the rest of his life." Ziqi’s face fell, and tears streamed down as he said, "What has my son done to reach such an extreme?" Jiufang Yin said, "One who shares the ruler’s table bestows blessings upon three clans—how much more so upon parents! Now that you have heard this and weep, you reject good fortune. Your son is auspicious, but you are not."

Ziqi said, "Yin, how could you understand? Is Kun truly auspicious? His fate is bound to meat and wine, entering only through nose and mouth—how can one know its origin? I have never herded sheep, yet a ewe was born in the inner chamber; I have never hunted, yet a quail hatched in the rafters. If this is not strange, then what is? What I share with my sons is wandering in the realm of heaven and earth. With them, I roam in joy beneath the sky and feast upon the earth. I do not involve them in affairs, nor in schemes, nor in strangeness. With them, I ride the sincerity of heaven and earth, not entangling them with things. With them, I drift and flow, not binding them to the concerns of the world. Yet now, this has brought worldly recompense!

Whenever there are strange omens, there must be strange conduct—how perilous! This is not the fault of my sons or me, but of heaven! That is why I weep."

Before long, Kun was sent to Yan. Bandits seized him on the road. Selling him whole would be difficult, but maiming him would make it easy. So they cut off his feet and sold him in Qi, where he ended up in the employ of Duke Qu, eating meat for the rest of his life.

Nie Que met Xu You and asked, "Where are you going?" He replied, "I am fleeing Yao." "What do you mean?" Xu You said, "Yao is so earnestly benevolent—yet I fear he will become a laughingstock to the world. Later generations may even turn to cannibalism! The people are not hard to gather: love them, benefit them, and they come; praise them, and they are encouraged. But impose what they hate, and they scatter. Love and benefit arise from benevolence and righteousness, yet those who discard benevolence and righteousness are few, while those who profit from them are many. The practice of benevolence and righteousness lacks sincerity—it is but a vessel for the greedy and predatory. Thus, when one person’s judgment controls the world’s benefits, it is like a single glance. Yao knows that worthy men benefit the world, but he does not know they also harm it—only those outside worthiness understand this."

There are those who are *nuan shu*, those who are *ru xu*, and those who are *juan lou*.

The *nuan shu* learn the words of a single master, then glow with self-satisfaction, believing themselves complete—yet they do not know that nothing has truly begun. Thus they are called *nuan shu*.

The *ru xu* are like lice on a pig: sparse bristles become their vast palace and park. The curves of hooves and knees, the spaces between teats and thighs—these they take as safe dwellings and advantageous places. They do not know that the butcher, with a single wave of his arm, spreads straw and wields fire, and they will be roasted along with the pig. They advance and retreat within their domain—this is what is called *ru xu*.

The *juan lou* is like Shun. Mutton does not long for ants, but ants long for mutton—because mutton is rank. Shun had rank conduct, and the people delighted in him. Thus, when he moved to Chengdu, by the time he reached the ruins of Deng, a hundred thousand households had gathered. Yao, hearing of Shun’s worthiness, raised him from the barren land, saying, "I hope to receive his blessings." Shun was raised from the barren land, and though his years grew long and his hearing and sight waned, he could not retire. This is what is called *juan lou*.

The divine person detests the crowd’s arrival, for when the crowd arrives, they do not harmonize; when they do not harmonize, no benefit comes of it.

Thus, one who is neither overly intimate nor overly distant, who embraces virtue and warms harmony to align with all under heaven—this is called the *True Person*. To ants, wisdom is discarded; to fish, calculation is fitting; to sheep, intent is abandoned.

With the eye, one sees the eye; with the ear, one hears the ear; with the heart, one returns to the heart. Such a one is level as a plumb line, and in change, follows the natural course. The *True Person* of old! With heaven, they await humanity; they do not force humanity into heaven. The *True Person* of old! In gaining it, they live; in losing it, they die. In gaining it, they die; in losing it, they live.

Medicines—such as aconite, platycodon, chicken pox, and pig’s rot—are the tools of emperors. How can they all be enumerated! Goujian, with a thousand shields and spears, took refuge at Kuaiji. Only Wen Zhong could know how ruin would become survival, yet even he did not know why his own person would suffer sorrow.

Thus it is said: The owl’s eye has its proper place; the crane’s leg has its proper length—severing them brings sorrow. Thus it is said: When wind passes over a river, it diminishes it; when the sun passes over a river, it diminishes it. If only wind and sun were to guard the river together, the river would deem them no disturbance at all, for it relies on its source and flows onward.

Thus, water’s adherence to earth is precise; its adherence to humanity, and things’ adherence to things, are likewise precise. The eye’s reliance on clarity is perilous; the ear’s reliance on acuity is perilous; the heart’s pursuit of desire is perilous. All faculties, when bound to their storehouses, become perilous, and once peril is formed, it cannot be swiftly remedied. Disaster’s growth is dense and clustered; its reversal depends on merit; its fruition awaits time.

That people regard this as their treasure—is it not lamentable! Thus, the ruin of states and the slaughter of people never cease, for they do not inquire into the cause. The foot’s tread upon the earth is firm, yet it relies on what it does not tread to walk broadly. Humanity’s knowledge is scant, yet it relies on what it does not know to comprehend heaven.

To know the Great Oneness, the Great Yin, the Great Vision, the Great Equality, the Great Square, the Great Faith, the Great Constancy—this is perfection! The Great Oneness unites them; the Great Yin dissolves them; the Great Vision sees them; the Great Equality follows them; the Great Square embodies them; the Great Faith verifies them; the Great Constancy upholds them.

There is the Heavenly Cycle, the Illuminating Obscurity, the Pivot of Beginning, the That-and-This. In dissolving them, one seems not to dissolve; in knowing them, one seems not to know. Only by not knowing does one come to know. In questioning them, one cannot set limits, yet cannot be without limits. Though tangled and slippery, they hold truth. Past and present alternate, yet cannot be diminished—can this not be called great clarification?

Why not simply inquire into this, and what confusion remains? To be confused by what is not confusing, and return to what is not confusing—this is the highest non-confusion.

---

Zeyang traveled to Chu, and Yi Jie spoke of him to the king, but the king did not summon him. Yi Jie returned home. Pengyang met Wang Guo and said, "Why did the Master not recommend me to the king?" Wang Guo replied, "I am not as good as Gong Yuxiu."

Pengyang asked, "What does Gong Yuxiu do?" Wang Guo said, "In winter, he spears turtles in the river; in summer, he rests in the mountain thickets. If someone passes by and asks, he says, ‘This is my dwelling.’ Yi Jie could not match him—how much less could I! I am not even Yi Jie’s equal.

"Yi Jie’s character is virtuous and wise, yet he does not presume. His interactions with the spirit-like are steadfast, firmly rooted in the realm of wealth and honor. If not for mutual aid in virtue, they would only erode one another. The frozen borrow clothes from spring; the overheated turn back to winter’s cold wind.

"His demeanor is dignified and stern. In matters of punishment, he pardons none, like a tiger. If not for a cunning man’s rectification of virtue, who could sway him? Thus, when the sage is in poverty, he makes his family forget their want; when he is in prominence, he makes kings and lords forget their ranks and transform into humility.

"Toward things, he delights in them; toward people, he rejoices in their communion while preserving himself. Thus, sometimes without speaking, he harmonizes others; standing beside them, he transforms them, as naturally as the proper relationship between father and son. Such is his way of returning. Dwelling in stillness, he observes the scope of his influence. His effect on the human heart is so distant.

"Thus, it is said, ‘Await Gong Yuxiu.’"

The sage’s comprehension is thorough and all-encompassing, yet he does not know it as such—it is his nature. He responds to fate and acts, taking heaven as his teacher, while others follow and name him. Anxious in knowledge, his actions seldom endure. When they cease, what then?

One born beautiful is given a mirror by others; if not told, they do not know their beauty surpasses others. Whether they know or do not know, hear or do not hear, their joy is endless, and others’ fondness for them is likewise endless—it is their nature.

The sage’s love for people is such that others name it for him; if told, they do not know his love for people. Whether they know or do not know, hear or do not hear, his love is endless, and people’s contentment in it is likewise endless—it is his nature.

An old state, an old capital—gazing upon them brings delight. Even if nine-tenths were overgrown with hills, grass, and trees, one would still delight in them. How much more so when seeing and hearing what is familiar, like a ten-fathom banner amidst the crowd!

Ran Xiangshi grasped the center of the ring and followed completion, moving with things without end or beginning, without nearness or time. One who daily transforms with things is one with them. Why ever abandon this?

To take heaven as one’s teacher and yet fail to do so, to be enslaved with things—how can one make this one’s task? The sage has never begun, never begun with humanity, never begun with beginning, never begun with things. They move with the world without ceasing, their actions complete yet not excessive. How could they be otherwise?"

Tang obtained his steward, Gatekeeper Yin Deng Heng, to serve as his tutor, following the teacher without constraint—thus attaining the freedom of following completion. He oversaw names: when names exceeded the law, he discerned both sides.

Confucius, in exhausting his thoughts, served as his tutor. The House of Rong Cheng said: *"Remove days, and there are no years; no inner, no outer."*

When Wei Ying made a pact with Marquis Tian Mou, the latter betrayed it. Enraged, Wei Ying intended to send an assassin. Xishou Gongsun Yan, hearing of this, felt shame and said: *"You are a lord of ten thousand chariots, yet you would avenge yourself like a common man. Allow me to lead two hundred thousand armored troops to attack him, seize his people, his cattle and horses, and make his lord’s heart burn within until his back erupts in sores.
