Then I will take his state. When Ji flees, I will strike his back and break his spine."*

The Master, hearing this, felt shame and said: *"To build a city wall seventy feet high, only to tear it down once completed—this is the toil of convict laborers. Now war has raged for seven years; this is the foundation of your kingship. Yan is a disturber of order; do not heed him."*

Hua Zi, hearing this, found it disgraceful and said: *"He who speaks well of attacking Qi is a disturber; he who speaks well of not attacking is also a disturber. To say, ‘Whether to attack or not—both are disturbers’—is yet another disturber."*

The lord asked: *"Then what should be done?"*
He replied: *"Seek only the Dao!"*

Upon hearing this, the lord went to see Dai Jinren. Dai Jinren said: *"There is a creature called the snail—do you know it?"*
*"Yes,"* the lord replied.
*"On the left horn of the snail is a state called Chus; on the right horn, a state called Mans. They constantly fight over land, with tens of thousands falling in battle, pursuing the routed for fifteen days before returning."*
The lord exclaimed: *"Alas! Is this an empty tale?"*

Dai Jinren said: *"Allow me to make this real for you. When Your Lordship’s thoughts roam the four directions and above and below, do they reach a limit?"*
The lord replied: *"They do not."*
*"If you know to let your mind wander in the boundless, yet return to the states that lie within reach—do they not seem as if they exist, as if they do not?"*
The lord said: *"So it is."*
*"Among the states within reach lies Wei; within Wei lies Liang; within Liang lies Your Lordship. Is there any difference between you and the Mans?"*
The lord said: *"There is no difference."*
The guest departed, and the lord sat in a daze as if something had been lost.

After the guest left, Master Hui came to see him. The lord said: *"That guest is a great man—even a sage cannot compare to him."*
Master Hui said: *"When one blows into a large bamboo pipe, there is a deep sound; when one blows into the hilt of a sword, there is but a faint whistle. Yao and Shun are those whom men praise. Yet to speak of Yao and Shun before Dai Jinren is like that faint whistle."*

When Confucius traveled to Chu, he lodged at the thatched hut of Ant Mound. His neighbor had a household of husband, wife, servants, and concubines who had climbed onto the roof. Zilu said: *"What is this bustling crowd doing?"*
Zhongni said: *"They are the servants of a sage. They bury themselves in the wilds, conceal themselves in the margins. Their voices fade, their wills are boundless, their mouths may speak, but their hearts never utter a word. They go against the world and disdain to follow it. These are the ones who sink into obscurity—is this not Shi Kuang of the southern market?"*
Zilu asked to go and summon them. Confucius said: *"Enough! He knows that I am aware of him, that I am traveling to Chu, and that I would surely have the King of Chu summon him. He would take me for a flatterer. If he feels shame even to hear the words of a flatterer, how much more to meet him in person! And how could he remain here?"*
Zilu went to look, but the house was empty.

The warden of Changwu asked Zilao: *"In governing, do not be reckless; in ordering the people, do not be careless. In the past, when I planted grain, if I plowed recklessly, the harvest repaid me in kind; if I weeded carelessly, the harvest repaid me in kind. The next year, I changed my ways, deepened the plowing, and carefully covered the seeds—the grain grew lush and abundant, and I had my fill for the whole year."*
When Zhuangzi heard of this, he said: *"Nowadays, those who cultivate their bodies and order their minds often resemble what the warden describes. They flee from their Heaven, depart from their nature, diminish their emotions, and lose their spirit, all to follow the crowd. Thus, those who recklessly harm their nature: the sprouts of desire and aversion become their nature. Like reeds and rushes, they first support our form, then soon strangle us. They burst forth in sores and ulcers, erupting without choice, festering as boils, scabs, and abscesses—internal heat and oozing fat are the result."*

Boju studied under Lao Dan and said, "I request to wander the world."
Lao Dan replied, "Enough! The world is everywhere the same."
Boju asked again, and Lao Dan said, "Where will you begin?"
He replied, "I will begin in Qi."

Arriving in Qi, he saw a man who had been executed. He pushed forward, straightened the body, removed his court robes to cover it, wailed to Heaven, and wept, saying: "You! You! The world suffers great calamity—why must you alone be the first to meet it? They say, *'Do not steal! Do not kill!'* Yet once honor and disgrace are established, one sees what brings suffering; once wealth and property are amassed, one sees what sparks strife. Now, to establish what brings people suffering, to amass what sparks their strife, to exhaust their bodies without respite—how could one hope to avoid reaching this point?

In ancient times, those who ruled over others took success as belonging to the people and failure as their own; they took uprightness as belonging to the people and crookedness as their own. Thus, if even one person lost their proper form, they would withdraw and blame themselves. Now, it is not so: they conceal their deeds, feigning ignorance of the people’s folly; they impose great difficulties yet punish those who dare not comply; they heap heavy burdens yet penalize those who cannot bear them; they lengthen the path yet execute those who do not reach the end. When the people’s strength is exhausted, they resort to deception to sustain it. With falsehoods multiplying daily, how can scholars and commoners not also resort to falsehood?

When strength is insufficient, one resorts to pretense; when wisdom is insufficient, one resorts to deceit; when wealth is insufficient, one resorts to... Who can be held accountable for the deeds of theft and banditry?"

Qū Bóyù reached sixty years of age and transformed sixty times, never failing to begin with what he deemed right at the time, only to end by rejecting it as wrong. How could one know that what is deemed right today is not the wrong of fifty-nine years past? All things have life, yet none know their root; all emerge, yet none see their gate. People all honor the knowledge of what they know, yet none know to rely on the knowledge of what they do not know before attaining true knowledge—can this not be called great delusion? Enough! Enough! There is no escape from this. Is this what is meant by "so it is" and "is it so"?

Confucius asked the Grand Historian Dàtāo, Bó Chángqiān, and Xī Wéi: "Duke Líng of Wèi indulged in wine and revelry, neglecting the governance of his state; he hunted and shot without responding to the affairs of the feudal lords. How then was he called Líng?" Dàtāo said: "It was because of this." Bó Chángqiān said: "Duke Líng had three wives, with whom he bathed promiscuously. Yet when Historian Qiū presented himself in attendance, bearing the ceremonial silk, Duke Líng supported him with reverence. Though his arrogance was so extreme, his respect for the worthy was so solemn—this is why he was called Líng." Xī Wéi said: "When Duke Líng died, divination for burial at the ancestral tomb was auspicious, and divination for burial at Shāqiū was also auspicious. Digging several fathoms deep, they found a stone outer coffin. Washing it and examining it, they saw an inscription that read: *'Not relying on his son, Duke Líng seized and buried it here.'* Duke Líng’s numinousness had long been so! What could those two understand of it?"

Little Knowledge asked Great Master Tiáo: "What is meant by 'village words'?" Great Master Tiáo said: "A village combines ten surnames to form its customs, unites differences into sameness, and disperses sameness into differences. Now, if you point to the hundred parts of a horse and cannot obtain the horse, yet the horse stands before you, it is because you call the hundred parts a horse. Thus, hills accumulate the lowly to become high; rivers combine waters to become great; the great man unites all to become impartial. What enters from without has a master but is not grasped; what issues from within has a norm but is not resisted. The four seasons have distinct qi, yet Heaven does not bestow favor, and so the year is completed; offices have distinct duties, yet the ruler does not show partiality, and so the state is well-governed; the civil and martial virtues of the great man are not bestowed, and so his power is complete; the myriad things have distinct principles, yet the Dao does not show partiality, and so it is nameless. Nameless, it is non-action; through non-action, there is nothing it does not do. Time has endings and beginnings; the world has changes and transformations. Fortune and misfortune flow ceaselessly, sometimes thwarting what should be fitting, sometimes following different paths; what is corrected may still err. Compared to a great marsh, all materials find their measure; observed in a great mountain, trees and stones share the same ground. This is what is meant by 'village words.'" Little Knowledge said: "Then can this be called the Dao?" Great Master Tiáo said: "No. The number of things is not limited to ten thousand, yet we call them 'the ten thousand things' because we name and refer to them by their multitude. Thus, Heaven and Earth are the greatest of forms; yin and yang are the greatest of qi; the Dao is their impartiality. To name and refer to it by its greatness is permissible, but to claim you have grasped it—how could you compare to it? If you argue thus, it would be like comparing dogs and horses—far from sufficient."

Little Knowledge said: "Within the four seas, within the six directions, where do the myriad things arise?" Great Master Tiáo said: "Yin and yang illuminate and cover each other, govern and regulate each other; the four seasons succeed one another, give birth to and destroy each other. Desires and aversions, departures and approaches arise from this; the union of male and female becomes customary through this. Safety and danger alternate; fortune and misfortune give birth to each other; urgency and leisure rub against each other; gathering and scattering complete each other. These are the names and realities that can be recorded, the essences that can be noted. Following the order of succession, the mutual employment of bridging movements, exhaustion returns to beginning, end returns to start—this is what things inherently possess. What words can exhaust, what knowledge can reach, is merely the limits of things. Those who perceive the Dao do not follow its decay, do not trace its origin—this is where discussion stops."

Little Knowledge said: "Jì Zhēn’s 'non-action' and Jiēzǐ’s 'or causation'—which of these two doctrines accords with reality, which pervades principle?" Great Master Tiáo said: "The crowing of roosters and the barking of dogs are what people know; yet even the greatest wisdom cannot describe in words their spontaneous transformations, nor anticipate in thought what they will do. Analyzing this, the essence reaches the unparalleled, the greatness reaches the unbounded."

"‘Or causation’ and ‘non-action’—both avoid entanglement with things yet are ultimately deemed erroneous. ‘Or causation’ is substantive, ‘non-action’ is vacuous. Having name and reality is where things reside; lacking name and reality lies in the void of things. What can be spoken or conceived only grows more so with words. The unborn cannot be shunned, death cannot be prevented. Life and death are not distant, yet their principle cannot be perceived. ‘Or causation’ and ‘non-action’—these are but borrowed terms for doubt.

I observe their root: its going is without end. I seek their end: its coming is without cease. Without end, without cease—words fail here, yet this is the same principle as things. ‘Or causation’ and ‘non-action’ are the root of this. They accompany things from beginning to end.

The Dao cannot be possessed, possession cannot be devoid of it. The Dao as a name is but a borrowed means to proceed. ‘Or causation’ and ‘non-action’ are but one corner of things—how could they suffice for the great square?

If words were sufficient, then speaking all day would exhaust the Dao. If words were insufficient, then speaking all day would only speak of things. The ultimate of Dao and things cannot be borne by speech or silence. Neither speech nor silence—discussion reaches its limit here."

External things cannot be relied upon—thus Longfeng was executed, Bigan was slain, Jizi feigned madness, Elai met his death, and Jie and Zhou perished. A ruler does not desire his ministers’ loyalty, yet loyalty does not necessarily earn trust; thus Wu Yun was cast into the river, Chang Hong died in Shu, and his blood, stored for three years, turned to jade-green. A parent does not desire a child’s filial piety, yet filial piety does not necessarily bring love; thus Xiaoji grieved and Zeng Shen mourned.

When wood rubs against wood, it ignites; when metal is held to fire, it melts. When yin and yang clash in their courses, heaven and earth are thrown into great turmoil—then thunder and lightning arise, fire appears in water, and even the great locust tree is consumed. There is such sorrow when one is trapped between two perils with no escape.

The mantis cannot complete its strike, the heart hangs suspended between heaven and earth, comfort and distress intermingle, profit and harm grind against each other, and the fire of life burns fiercely. The multitude scorches harmony, and the moon is no match for fire—thus the Way is exhausted and collapses.

Zhuang Zhou was poor, so he went to borrow grain from the Marquis of the River. The marquis said, “Very well. When I receive the tax silver from my fief, I will lend you a hundred gold pieces—will that do?” Zhuang Zhou flushed with anger and said, “Yesterday, as I came, I heard a voice calling from the middle of the road. I looked and saw a crucian carp in the rut of a cart wheel. I asked it, ‘Crucian carp! What are you doing here?’ It replied, ‘I am a wave-minister of the Eastern Sea. Could you spare a dipper or two of water to keep me alive?’ I said, ‘Very well. I am about to travel south to persuade the kings of Wu and Yue, and I will divert the waters of the Western River to bring you home—will that do?’ The crucian carp flushed with anger and said, ‘I have lost my usual element, I have nowhere to go. I could be kept alive with a dipper or two of water. Yet you speak like this—you might as well have looked for me sooner in a dried-fish shop!’”

Lord Ren made a great hook with a thick black line, baited it with fifty steers, crouched on Mount Kuaiji, and cast his rod into the Eastern Sea. He fished day after day for a year without catching anything. Then a great fish took the bait, dragged the hook down, and plunged into the depths, thrashing its fins and churning white waves like mountains. The sea trembled, its roar matching the gods, and its dreadful power spread for a thousand miles. Lord Ren caught the fish, butchered it, and dried it. From the east of the Yellow River to the north of Cangwu, there was no one who did not feast on it.

Yet later, the petty and clever talkers all marveled and spread the tale. Those who raise a flimsy rod, rush to irrigation ditches, and watch for minnows and crucian carp—how could they ever catch a great fish? Those who embellish trivial words to seek high office—how far are they from great understanding? Thus, those who have never heard of the customs of the Ren clan are far indeed from being able to navigate the world.

Confucians use the *Odes* and *Rites* to rob graves. A great Confucian intoned, “The east is stirring—how goes the affair?” A lesser Confucian replied, “The skirt and jacket are not yet removed, but there is a pearl in the mouth.” The *Odes* indeed say: *“The green-green wheat grows on the hillside. If one does not give in life, why hold a pearl in death?”* They seized the hair, pressed the chin, and with a metal mallet, struck the jaw to pry open the cheeks—all without harming the pearl in the mouth!

A disciple of Lao Laizi was gathering firewood when he encountered Confucius. He returned and reported, “There is someone over there—tall above, hastening below, with ears set back, gazing as if surveying the four seas. I do not know whose son he is.” Lao Laizi said, “That is Qiu. Summon him.” Confucius arrived, and Lao Laizi said, “Qiu, rid yourself of your bodily pride and your cultivated knowledge—then you may become a gentleman.” Confucius bowed and withdrew, his expression troubled. He asked, “May I advance in my work?” Lao Laizi said, “To be unable to bear the harm of one age and yet rush headlong into the calamities of ten thousand ages—is this not stubborn and narrow? Or is it that your plans fall short? Kindness that takes delight in recklessness is a lifelong disgrace. The conduct of the common people advances thus, drawing one another by reputation, binding one another with... Better to forget both Yao’s praise and Jie’s blame, and close off the very source of praise. To turn back is not to be free of harm; to act is not to be free of error. The sage hesitates to initiate affairs, yet each one succeeds. Why then do you persist in bearing your pride to the end?”

*(End of Scroll 9, Part 26: External Things)*

Duke Yuan of Song dreamed in the dead of night of a man with disheveled hair peering through the side gate. The man said, “I come from the abyss of Zailu. I was sent by the Clear River as an emissary to the River Lord, but the fisherman Yu Ju caught me.” The Duke awoke and had the dream interpreted. The diviner said, “This is a spirit turtle.” The Duke asked, “Is there a fisherman named Yu Ju?” His attendants replied, “There is.” The Duke said, “Summon Yu Ju to court.”

The next day, Yu Ju appeared. The Duke asked, “What did you catch?” Yu Ju answered, “I cast my net and caught a white turtle, five feet in circumference.” The Duke said, “Present your turtle.” When the turtle arrived, the Duke wavered—once wanting to kill it, once wanting to spare it. His mind unsettled, he consulted the oracle, which said, “Killing the turtle for divination will bring good fortune.” So they carved the turtle open, and in seventy-two drillings, not a single prediction failed.

Confucius said, “The spirit turtle could appear in the Duke’s dream yet could not escape Yu Ju’s net. It could foresee seventy-two divinations without error yet could not avoid the calamity of having its entrails carved out. Thus, even knowledge has its limitations, and even the divine has its shortcomings. Though one may possess supreme wisdom, ten thousand schemes are needed. Fish do not fear nets but fear pelicans. Discard petty knowledge, and great knowledge becomes clear; abandon contrived goodness, and true goodness arises of itself. An infant, born without a teacher of stone, learns to speak by dwelling among those who can.”

Huizi said to Zhuangzi, “Your words are useless.” Zhuangzi replied, “Only when one understands uselessness can we speak of use. The heavens are neither narrow nor small, yet what man uses is no more than the space his feet occupy. If one were to dig beneath his feet to the Yellow Springs, would there still be use?” Huizi said, “No.” Zhuangzi said, “Then the usefulness of the useless is also clear.”

Zhuangzi said, “If a man has the ability to wander freely, can he choose not to? If a man lacks the ability, can he wander at all? To drift and flee, to cut off all ties—alas, is this not beyond the capacity of even the wisest and most virtuous? To fall and not turn back, to rush like fire and not look back—though they may be ruler and minister for a time, their relationship is transient, shifting without mutual contempt. Thus it is said: the perfected person does not linger in his actions. Those who exalt the past and belittle the present follow the current of scholars. Yet if we observe the present age through the lens of the age of the Divine Farmer, who can avoid being swept along? Only the perfected person can wander through the world without bias, following others without losing himself. Their teaching and learning do not impose upon one another.

Eyes that penetrate see clearly; ears that penetrate hear keenly; noses that penetrate smell acutely; mouths that penetrate taste sweetness; minds that penetrate know; knowledge that penetrates becomes virtue. The Dao does not wish to be obstructed—obstruction leads to choking, and if choking persists, it becomes stumbling, from which a multitude of harms arise. Beings with knowledge rely on breath; if it does not flow smoothly, it is not heaven’s fault. Heaven pierces them day and night without ceasing, yet man clogs his own apertures.

The womb has its layered chambers; the mind has its heavenly wanderings. If the room is never empty, mother and daughter-in-law will quarrel; if the mind has no heavenly wandering, the six senses will jostle one another. The great forests and mountains are good for man, yet even the spiritually endowed cannot fully master them.

Virtue overflows into fame, fame overflows into violence, schemes stagnate in cunning, knowledge emerges from strife, barriers arise from clinging to office, and affairs are settled by what suits the crowd. When spring rains fall in season, grasses and trees flourish, and the hoe and plow begin their work. Yet more than half of the plants grow upside down, unaware of their own inversion.

Stillness and silence can heal illness; rubbing the corners of the eyes can rejuvenate the old; tranquility can halt haste. Yet even so, those who toil never inquire of those at leisure. The sage’s way of startling the world is something the spiritually realized never inquire about; the worthy’s way of startling the age is something the sage never inquires about; the noble’s way of startling the state is something the worthy never inquire about; the petty man’s way of conforming to the times is something the noble never inquires about.

In Yanmen, there was a man whose parent died. Because he mourned so well, he was appointed an official tutor. Half of his fellow villagers followed his example and mourned themselves to death. Yao offered the world to Xu You, but Xu You fled; Tang offered it to Wu Guang, but Wu Guang grew angry; Ji Ta heard of this and led his disciples to crouch by the Kuang River, awaiting condolences. Three years later, Shentu Di drowned himself in the Yellow River.

The fish trap exists for the sake of catching fish; once the fish is caught, the trap is forgotten. The rabbit snare exists for the sake of catching rabbits; once the rabbit is caught, the snare is forgotten. Words exist for the sake of meaning; once the meaning is grasped, the words are forgotten. Where can I find a man who has forgotten words, that I might speak with him?”

Nineteen in ten of words are parables; seventeen in ten are reiterations; goblet words emerge daily, harmonized by the Heavenly Equilibrium.

Nineteen in ten of words are parables, relying on external voices to expound. A loving father does not act as matchmaker for his son. If a father praises his own son, it is not as credible as if a non-father did so. This is no fault of his own—it is the fault of others. When others align with oneself, one responds; when they do not align, one resists. What aligns with oneself is deemed right; what differs is deemed wrong.

Seventeen in ten are reiterations, used to affirm one’s own words. These are the words of elders, those whose years precede ours. Yet if they lack the warp and weft of fundamental principles to guide those who follow, they are in error. A person who cannot surpass others lacks the human Way; one who lacks the human Way is called a withered remnant.

Goblet words emerge daily, harmonized by the Heavenly Equilibrium, flowing and unfolding to exhaust the years. Without words, all is equal; equality with words is not equality, and words with equality are not equality. Thus it is said: "Nothing." Words without words—throughout a lifetime, one speaks, yet never truly speaks; throughout a lifetime, one does not speak, yet never truly ceases to speak. There is that by which something is permissible, and that by which it is not; there is that by which something is so, and that by which it is not so.

Whence comes "so"? It is so in being so. Whence comes "not so"? It is not so in being not so. Whence comes "permissible"? It is permissible in being permissible. Whence comes "impermissible"? It is impermissible in being impermissible. Things inherently have that by which they are so; things inherently have that by which they are permissible. Yet there is nothing that is not so, nothing that is not permissible.

Without goblet words emerging daily, harmonized by the Heavenly Equilibrium, who could endure for long? All things are seeds, succeeding one another through differing forms, beginning and end like a ring, attaining their proper order—this is called the Heavenly Equilibrium. The Heavenly Equilibrium is the Heavenly Equilibrium.

Zhuangzi said to Huizi: "Confucius lived sixty years, and in sixty years he transformed. What he initially affirmed, he ultimately negated. Who knows whether what he called 'right' at sixty was not the 'wrong' of fifty-nine?" Huizi replied: "Confucius diligently applied his will and employed his knowledge." Zhuangzi said: "Confucius has renounced such things; he never spoke of them. Confucius said: 'One receives one’s endowment from the great root and restores the numinous to life. To sing in accord with the pitch-pipes, to speak in accord with the laws—profit and righteousness arrayed before one, yet likes and dislikes, right and wrong merely subdue men’s mouths. To make men submit with their hearts and not dare to resist, to establish the settled order of all under Heaven—ah! Alas! I shall never attain his level!'"

Master Zeng took office twice, and his heart transformed twice. He said: "When I first served while my parents were alive, a stipend of three *fu* brought joy to my heart; later, when I served after their passing, three thousand *zhong* could not reach them, and my heart grieved." A disciple asked Confucius: "Can one say that Shen here is free of blame?" Confucius replied: "He is already blameworthy! How could one free of blame feel such sorrow? To him, three *fu* or three thousand *zhong* were like watching sparrows, mosquitoes, or gadflies pass before him."

Yan Chengzi You said to Dongguo Ziqi: "Since I heard your words, in one year I became rustic; in two, I followed; in three, I penetrated; in four, I became one with things; in five, they came to me; in six, spirits entered; in seven, Heaven was accomplished; in eight, I knew neither death nor life; in nine, I attained the great mystery. Life is active; death is loss. You hold that death has its source, while life—yang as it is—has none. Is this truly so? Where does it go? Where does it not go? Heaven has its celestial reckoning, earth has its human foundations—where should I seek them? If none knows its end, how can one say there is no fate? If none knows its beginning, how can one say there is fate? When there is resonance, how can one say there are no spirits? When there is no resonance, how can one say there are spirits?"

The shadowy wraiths asked the shadow: "Earlier you bowed your head, now you raise it; earlier your hair was bound, now it is loose; earlier you sat, now you stand; earlier you walked, now you halt—why?" The shadow replied: "Such trifling matters—why ask at all? I act, yet know not why. I am like the cicada’s molted husk, the snake’s sloughed skin—resembling them yet not them. Fire and sunlight are my gathering; darkness and night are my succession. Do I depend on these? How much more so those who depend on what depends! When it comes, I come with it; when it goes, I go with it; when it is strong and yang, I am strong and yang with it. What question is there in strong yang?"

Yang Ziju journeyed south to Pei, while Lao Dan traveled west to Qin. They met on the outskirts of Liang, where Laozi sighed to the sky mid-journey: "At first I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot." Yang Ziju made no reply. Upon reaching their lodging, he presented water for washing, a towel and comb, removed his shoes outside the door, and advanced on his knees, saying: "Earlier, your disciple wished to ask you a question, but you were too occupied to pause—so I dared not. Now, may I inquire the reason?" Laozi said: "Your haughty gaze, your overbearing mien—who would dwell with you? The purest white seems tarnished; the greatest virtue seems insufficient." Yang Ziju’s expression changed abruptly, and he said: "I humbly receive your instruction."

When he had first arrived, the innkeeper had welcomed him with deference, the master of the house had laid out his mat, the wife had held his towel, the guests had yielded their seats, and those tending the stove had stepped aside. Upon his return, the innkeeper contended with him for his seat.

Chapter Twenty-Eight: Yielding the Throne

Yao offered the empire to Xu You, who declined. He then offered it to Zizhou Zhipu, who said: "If you deem me fit, it would be acceptable. Yet I happen to suffer from a deep melancholy, and am just now treating it—I have no leisure to govern the empire." The empire is the weightiest of burdens, yet he would not let it harm his life—how much less other things! Only one who takes no account of the empire can be entrusted with it.

Shun offered the empire to Zizhou Bo, who said: "I happen to suffer from a deep melancholy, and am just now treating it—I have no leisure for the empire." Thus the empire is a great vessel, yet one does not exchange life for it. This is how those who possess the Way differ from the common.

Shun offered the empire to Shan Juan, who said: "I stand amid the cosmos: in winter I wear furs, in summer I wear hemp; in spring I plow and sow, and my strength suffices for labor; in autumn I reap and gather, and my body suffices for rest and sustenance."

With the sun’s rising, I rise to work; with the sun’s setting, I rest—roaming free between heaven and earth, my heart content. Why should I take the empire for myself? Alas, how little you understand me!

Thus he refused it. Then he departed and entered the deep mountains, where he dwelled.

Shun offered the empire to his friend, the farmer of Stone Gate. The farmer of Stone Gate said: “How toilsome is the lord’s conduct! He exhausts his strength in service.” He deemed Shun’s virtue not yet perfected. Thereupon, he shouldered his goods, his wife carried their belongings on her head, and they led their child into the sea, never to return.

King Tai of Danfu dwelled in Bin. The Di tribes attacked him. He offered them furs and silks, but they refused; he offered them dogs and horses, but they refused; he offered them pearls and jades, but they refused. What the Di sought was land. King Tai of Danfu said: “To dwell with a man’s elder brother and kill his younger brother, to dwell with a man’s father and kill his son—I cannot bear it. Go, all of you, and dwell as you will! What difference is there between serving me and serving the Di? Moreover, I have heard that one must not harm what nourishes for the sake of nourishment.” Thereupon, he took up his staff and departed. The people followed him in a continuous stream, and he established a state beneath Mount Qi.

King Tai of Danfu was one who honored life. Those who honor life, though noble and wealthy, do not let nourishment harm the body; though poor and lowly, do not let profit burden the form. Nowadays, those who occupy high office all fear losing it. Seeing profit, they lightly cast aside their lives—how deluded they are!

The people of Yue murdered their ruler for three generations. Prince Sou, distressed by this, fled into the cinnabar caves. When Yue was left without a ruler, they sought Prince Sou, following him to the cinnabar caves. Prince Sou refused to emerge, so the people of Yue smoked him out with mugwort. They bore him away in the royal carriage. Prince Sou grasped the reins and mounted the chariot, gazing up to heaven and crying: “Lordship! Lordship! Can you not spare me this?” Prince Sou did not hate being lord; he hated the calamity of being lord. Such was Prince Sou—one who would not let the state harm his life. This was precisely what the people of Yue desired in a lord.

Han and Wei contended over encroached land. Master Hua Zi visited Marquis Zhao of Han, who wore a troubled expression. Master Hua Zi said: “Suppose the world inscribed a pledge before you, saying: ‘If you seize it with your left hand, your right hand will be lost; if you seize it with your right hand, your left hand will be lost. Yet whoever seizes it will gain the world.’ Would you seize it?” Marquis Zhao said: “I would not.” Master Hua Zi said: “Excellent! From this we see that your two arms are weightier than the world, and your body is weightier than your two arms. Han’s standing in the world is far lighter than this, and what you now contend over is even lighter still. Yet you grieve and harm your life over what you cannot obtain!” Marquis Zhao said: “Well said! Many have instructed me, but never have I heard this.” Master Hua Zi had made him understand the weight of things.

The Lord of Lu heard that Yan He was a man who had attained the Way, and sent an envoy bearing gifts to precede him. Yan He dwelled in a humble lane, wearing coarse hempen clothes and herding oxen himself. When the Lord of Lu’s envoy arrived, Yan He received him in person. The envoy said: “Is this Yan He’s home?” Yan He replied: “This is He’s home.” The envoy presented the gifts, but Yan He said: “I fear a misunderstanding may bring blame upon the envoy—it would be better to verify.” The envoy returned to confirm, then came back to seek him, but Yan He could no longer be found.

Thus, one like Yan He truly despised wealth and honor. Therefore it is said: The true essence of the Way is for governing the self; its remnants may be used for the state; its dregs for governing the world. From this we see that the achievements of kings are but the leavings of sages, not what perfects the self or nourishes life. Yet today’s worldly gentlemen often endanger their bodies and abandon their lives to pursue things—how lamentable!

Whenever sages act, they must examine their motives and their aims. Suppose there were a man here who, to shoot a bird a thousand yards away, used the Pearl of Marquis Sui—all would surely laugh at him. Why? Because what he valued was heavy, and what he sought was light. Life—is it not far weightier than the Pearl of Marquis Sui?

Master Lie was poor, and his countenance bore the hue of hunger. A guest mentioned this to Zi Yang of Zheng, saying: “Lie Yukou is a scholar of the Way, yet he dwells in this state in poverty. Does my lord not love scholars?” Zi Yang of Zheng immediately ordered an official to send him grain. When Master Lie saw the envoy, he bowed twice and declined. The envoy departed, and Master Lie entered his home. His wife, striking her breast, said: “I have heard that the wife of one who possesses the Way lives in ease and joy. Yet you bear the hue of hunger. The lord sent you food, but you refused it—is this not fate?” Master Lie laughed and said to her: “The lord does not know me. He sent me grain on another’s word, and if he were to punish me, it would also be on another’s word. This is why I did not accept.” Later, the people indeed rebelled and killed Zi Yang.

King Zhao of Chu lost his state, and the butcher Yue followed him in exile. When King Zhao returned to his state, he wished to reward those who had followed him, including the butcher Yue. Yue said: “When the great king lost his state, I lost my butchery; when the great king returned, I returned to my butchery. My rank and emolument are restored—what reward is there?” The king said: “Insist!” The butcher Yue said: “When the great king lost his state, it was not my fault, so I dared not accept punishment; when the great king returned, it was not my merit, so I dared not claim reward.” The king said: “Let me see him.”

The butcher Yue said: “According to the laws of Chu, only those with great merit may be granted an audience. Now, my wisdom was not sufficient to preserve the state, nor was my courage sufficient to die resisting the enemy. When the Wu army entered Ying, I fled from danger and did not follow the great king by design. If the great king now wishes to set aside the law and violate precedent to receive me, this is not what I wish to be heard throughout the world.” The king said to Marshal Zi Qi: “Though the butcher Yue’s station is lowly, his principles are lofty. Zi Qi, offer him a position with the three standards of rank.” The butcher Yue said: “The position with three standards—I know it is nobler than my butcher’s stall; the salary of ten thousand bushels—I know it is richer than my butcher’s profit. Yet how could I covet rank and emolument and thereby cause my lord to gain a reputation for reckless bestowal? I dare not accept. I wish only to return to my butcher’s stall.” Thus he declined.

Yuan Xian lived in Lu in a walled hut with a roof of thatch; the gate was made of tangled brambles, incomplete; the door pivot was of mulberry wood; two earthen jars served as windows, sealed with reeds. Above, the roof leaked; below, the floor was damp. Yet he sat upright and played the zither. Zigong arrived in a grand carriage, his inner robe dark blue and his outer robe white, the axle of his high chariot too wide to pass through the lane. He went to see Yuan Xian. Yuan Xian, wearing a hat of woven rushes and shoes with untied laces, leaned on a staff of bramble and answered the door. Zigong said: “Alas! Master, what afflicts you?” Yuan Xian replied: “I have heard that to be poor without complaint is not poverty, but to study without putting it into practice is affliction. Now I am poor, not afflicted.” Zigong retreated, his face flushed with shame. Yuan Xian said: “To act for the sake of fame, to befriend for the sake of cliques, to study for others, to teach for oneself, to conceal one’s virtue with benevolence and righteousness, to adorn oneself with carriages and horses—these are things I cannot bear to do.”

Zengzi lived in Wei, his padded robe unlined, his complexion swollen and sallow, his hands and feet calloused. For three days he did not light a fire, for ten years he did not make new clothes. When he put on his cap, the ties snapped; when he grasped his lapel, his elbows showed; when he pulled on his shoes, the heels split. Yet he dragged his hem and sang the *Odes of Shang*, his voice filling heaven and earth, resonant as metal and stone. The Son of Heaven could not make him a subject, nor could the feudal lords make him a friend. Thus, those who cultivate the will forget the body; those who cultivate the body forget profit; those who attain the Dao forget the mind.

Confucius said to Yan Hui: “Hui, come! Your family is poor and your station low—why do you not enter official service?” Yan Hui replied: “I do not wish to serve. Outside the outer wall, I have fifty *mu* of fields, enough to provide gruel and porridge; within the outer wall, I have ten *mu* of fields, enough to provide silk and hemp. Playing the zither is enough to amuse myself; what I have learned of the Master’s Dao is enough to bring me joy.”

"Hui does not wish to serve in office." Confucius’s expression turned solemn, and he said: "Excellent, Hui’s resolve! I have heard it said: ‘Those who know contentment are not burdened by profit; those who are secure in self-possession do not fear loss; those who cultivate inner refinement feel no shame in low station.’ I have recited these words for a long time, but only now, through Hui, do I truly see them—this is my own gain."

Prince Mou of Zhongshan said to Zhanzi: "Though my body dwells by rivers and seas, my heart remains beneath the palace gates—what is to be done?" Zhanzi replied: "Value life. Value life, and profit becomes light." Prince Mou said: "Though I understand this, I cannot overcome myself." Zhanzi said: "If you cannot overcome yourself, then follow your inclination—what harm is there to the spirit? But if you cannot overcome yourself yet force yourself not to follow, this is called double injury. Those who suffer double injury have no place among the long-lived."

Prince Mou, a prince of ten thousand chariots, found it harder to retreat into mountain caves than a commoner—though he had not yet attained the Dao, his intent was commendable.

Confucius was stranded between Chen and Cai, going seven days without cooked food, subsisting on a thin soup of wild greens without grain. His complexion was gaunt, yet he still plucked the zither and sang in his chamber. While picking vegetables, Zilu and Zigong said to each other: "Our Master was twice driven from Lu, his traces erased in Wei, his tree felled in Song, impoverished in Shang and Zhou, besieged in Cai. Those who would kill him incur no guilt; those who would trample him face no prohibition. Yet he still strums the zither and sings, never ceasing his music—can a noble person be so shameless?"

Yan Hui had no reply. He went inside and told Confucius. Confucius pushed aside his zither and sighed deeply, saying: "You and Ci are petty men. Come here, and I will speak to you." Zilu and Zigong entered.

Zilu said: "In such circumstances, one may truly be called destitute!"
Confucius replied: "What words are these! The noble person who is at one with the Dao is called 'at one'; the one who is cut off from the Dao is called 'destitute.' Now I, Qiu, embrace the Dao of benevolence and righteousness yet encounter the calamities of a disordered age—how can I be called destitute? Inwardly examining myself, I am not cut off from the Dao; facing adversity, I do not lose my virtue. When the cold arrives and frost and snow descend, only then do I know the flourishing of the pine and cypress. The straits of Cai—are they not my good fortune?"

Confucius then struck the zither with a clear sound and resumed his singing. Zilu, grasping his robe, danced with vigor. Zigong said: "I did not know the height of heaven or the depth of earth."

The ancients who attained the Dao found joy in adversity and joy in success—their joy was not in adversity or success. When virtue and power reside within, then adversity and success become as the sequence of cold and heat, wind and rain.

Thus, Xu You delighted in Yingyang, and Gong Bo found contentment on Gongshou. Shun offered the world to his friend Beiren Wuze, who said: "How strange is this ruler’s conduct! Dwelling in the furrows of the fields, yet wandering at Yao’s gate! Not content with this alone, he now seeks to defile me with his shameful conduct—I am ashamed to see him." Thereupon he cast himself into the Clear and Cold Abyss.

When Tang was about to attack Jie, he consulted Bian Sui, who said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who then is suitable?" Bian Sui replied: "I do not know." Tang then consulted Mao Guang, who said: "This is not my affair." Tang asked: "Who then is suitable?" Mao Guang replied: "I know." Tang said: "What of Yi Yin?" Mao Guang answered: "He is strong and enduring, patient of disgrace—I know nothing else of him." Thereupon Tang conspired with Yi Yin to attack Jie, defeated him, and offered the throne to Bian Sui. Bian Sui declined, saying: "When the ruler plotted against Jie, he consulted me—he must have thought me a villain. Now that he has defeated Jie and offers me the throne, he must think me greedy. I was born in a disordered age, and this man without the Dao twice seeks to defile me with his conduct—I cannot bear to hear of it again!" He then cast himself into the Zhou River and died.

Tang then offered the throne to Mao Guang, saying: "The wise plan, the martial execute, the benevolent rule—this is the ancient Way. Why do you not ascend?" Mao Guang declined, saying: "To depose a ruler is not righteous; to rule the people is not benevolent; others face danger while I enjoy the benefits—this is not integrity. I have heard it said: 'One who does not act righteously does not accept its emoluments; in an age without the Dao, one does not tread its soil.' How much less should I accept such honor! I cannot bear to see this any longer."

He then strapped a stone to his back and drowned himself in the Lu River.

In the rise of the Zhou, there were two scholars dwelling in Guzhu, named Boyi and Shuqi. The two conferred, saying: "We have heard that in the west there is a man who seems to follow the Dao—let us go and observe him." Arriving at Qiyang, King Wu heard of them and sent Shu Dan to meet them. He made a covenant with them, saying: "I will grant you wealth two ranks higher and appoint you to an official post of the first order." They slaughtered an animal and buried it as an oath. The two looked at each other and laughed, saying: "Alas, how strange! This is not what we call the Dao. In the days when Shennong ruled the world, sacrifices were made with utmost reverence, yet no blessings were sought. In his dealings with others, loyalty and trust governed all, with no demands made. He delighted in governance and took joy in bringing order. He did not build himself up by others’ ruin, exalt himself by others’ lowliness, or profit from the times. Now the Zhou, seeing the disorder of the Yin, hastily seizes power. They scheme above and bribe below, rely on arms to maintain authority, and slaughter animals to swear oaths as a pledge of trust. They go about persuading the masses and resort to killing and warfare to seize advantage. This is but replacing one chaos with another. We have heard that the scholars of old, when encountering an age of order, did not shirk their duty, and when facing an age of disorder, did not cling to mere survival. Today the world is benighted, the virtue of the Zhou has declined—if we were to align ourselves with the Zhou, it would only sully our conduct. Better to avoid them and keep our ways pure." They then went north to Mount Shouyang and starved to death there.

As for Boyi and Shuqi, when it came to wealth and rank, if they could have done without, they would never have relied upon them. Their lofty integrity and unyielding conduct brought them joy in their own resolve, and they would not serve the world. Such was the integrity of these two scholars.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: Robber Zhi (Part Nine, Lower Scroll)**

Confucius was a friend of Liuxia Ji. Liuxia Ji’s younger brother was named Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi led a band of nine thousand followers, rampaging across the world and violently oppressing the feudal lords. They broke into homes, drove off people’s cattle and horses, and seized their wives and daughters. Greed made them forget their kin; they paid no heed to parents or brothers and did not sacrifice to their ancestors. Wherever they passed, great states fortified their cities, small states took refuge in their strongholds, and the common people suffered bitterly.

Confucius said to Liuxia Ji: "A father must be able to instruct his son, and an elder brother must be able to teach his younger brother. If a father cannot instruct his son, or a brother cannot teach his brother, then the bond of father and son, elder and younger brother, is worthless. Now you, sir, are a man of talent in this age, yet your brother is Robber Zhi, a scourge to the world, and you cannot correct him. I am secretly ashamed for you. Let me go and persuade him on your behalf."

Liuxia Ji replied: "You say that a father must be able to instruct his son, and an elder brother must be able to teach his brother. But if a son does not heed his father’s instruction, or a younger brother does not accept his elder brother’s teaching, even with your eloquence, what can you do? Moreover, Zhi’s nature is such that his mind is like a gushing spring, his thoughts like a whirlwind. His strength is enough to resist enemies, and his eloquence is enough to gloss over wrongs. He is pleased when things go his way and enraged when they do not. He is quick to insult others with words. You must not go."

Confucius did not listen. Yan Hui drove the carriage, and Zigong rode on the right as they went to see Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi was just then resting his troops on the sunny side of a great mountain, eating a meal of minced human liver. Confucius stepped down from the carriage and approached, saying to the herald: "I am Kong Qiu. Hearing of the general’s lofty integrity, I respectfully bow twice to the herald." The herald went in to announce him.

When Robber Zhi heard this, he flew into a great rage. His eyes blazed like stars, and his hair stood on end beneath his cap. He said: "Is this not that crafty, deceitful man Kong Qiu from Lu? Tell him this: 'You fabricate words and invent phrases, falsely invoking the names of the former kings. You wear a cap of twigs and a belt of dead oxhide, spouting a torrent of twisted arguments. You do not plow yet eat, do not weave yet wear fine clothes. You flap your lips and wag your tongue, creating false distinctions to delude the rulers of the world, leading the scholars astray so they do not return to their roots. You preach false filial piety and brotherly duty, all to angle for enfeoffment and wealth. Your crimes are grave and weighty—flee at once! If not, I will add your liver to my midday meal.'"

Confucius again sent word, saying: "I, Qiu, have found favor with the Ji family and wish only to gaze upon your tent-flap." The attendant relayed this message. Robber Zhi said: "Bring him forward!" Confucius hastened in, stepped back from his mat, and bowed twice to Robber Zhi. Robber Zhi flew into a great rage, stretching out his legs, pressing his sword, glaring fiercely, and roaring like a nursing tiger: "Qiu, come forward! If your words align with my will, you shall live; if they oppose my heart, you shall die."

Confucius said: "I, Qiu, have heard that under Heaven there are three virtues: to be born and grow tall, surpassingly beautiful and peerless, so that young and old, noble and base all delight in seeing you—this is the highest virtue; to comprehend the bounds of Heaven and Earth and to discern all things—this is the middle virtue; to be brave, fierce, resolute, and daring, gathering multitudes and leading troops—this is the lowest virtue. Any man possessing even one of these virtues is worthy to face the world and call himself solitary. Now, General, you combine all three: your body stands eight feet and two inches tall, your face and eyes radiate light, your lips are like gleaming cinnabar, your teeth like aligned cowries, your voice attuned to the Yellow Bell, yet you are called Robber Zhi. I, Qiu, secretly feel shame on your behalf for not claiming what is rightfully yours.

If you are inclined to heed your servant, I shall go south to Wu and Yue, making them submit to Lu; east to Song and Wei; west to Jin and Chu, and have them build for you a great city of several hundred *li*, establish a metropolis of hundreds of thousands of households, and honor you as a feudal lord. Together with the world, you may begin anew, disband your troops, rest your soldiers, gather and nurture your brothers, and jointly sacrifice to your ancestors. This is the conduct of a sage and a man of talent, the very wish of all under Heaven."

Robber Zhi flew into a rage, shouting: "Come forward, Qiu! Those who can be swayed by profit or admonished by words are but fools and the common rabble. My great beauty—men see it and delight in it—is the legacy of my parents' virtue. Even if you do not praise me, do I not know myself? Moreover, I have seen how those who flatter to one's face also slander behind one's back. Now you tell me of great cities and teeming multitudes—this is to lure me with profit and treat me as a herdsman tends his flock. Can such a thing endure?

Of all great cities, none surpasses the world itself. Yao and Shun possessed the world, yet their descendants had not even a place to drive a nail. Tang and Wu became Son of Heaven, yet their lineages perished—was this not because the profit was too great?

I have heard that in ancient times, beasts were many and men few, so people dwelt in nests to avoid them. By day they gathered acorns, by night they roosted in trees, and so they were called 'the people of the Nest Builder.' Later, people learned to make clothing, piling firewood in summer and warming themselves in winter, and so they were called 'the people who knew how to live.' In the age of Shen Nong, they lay at ease and rose without care. People knew their mothers but not their fathers, living among deer, plowing for food, weaving for clothing, without any thought of harming one another. Such was the height of perfect virtue.

Yet the Yellow Emperor could not preserve this virtue. He fought Chi You on the plains of Zhuolu, and blood flowed for a hundred *li*. Yao and Shun arose and appointed ministers; Tang exiled his lord, and King Wu killed Zhou. From then on, the strong oppressed the weak, and the many bullied the few. Since the time of Tang and Wu, all have been disturbers of the world.

Now you propagate the Way of Wen and Wu, wielding the world's eloquence to instruct later generations. You dress in fine robes with narrow sashes, speak with affected words and false deeds, to delude the rulers of the world and seek wealth and honor. There is no greater thief than you! Why does the world not call you 'Robber Qiu' instead of calling me 'Robber Zhi'?

You sweet-talked Zilu into following you, making him remove his warrior's cap, set aside his long sword, and receive your teachings. The world says, 'Confucius can stop violence and forbid wrongdoing.' Yet in the end, Zilu tried to kill the lord of Wei and failed, his body pickled and displayed above the eastern gate of Wei—this was the result of your inadequate teaching.

Do you still call yourself a man of talent and a sage? You were twice exiled from Lu, your traces erased in Wei, impoverished in Qi, besieged in Chen and Cai, and found no refuge under heaven. You taught Zilu to meet such a fate, unable to secure even your own safety, let alone that of others. Is your Way truly worthy of esteem?

Of all those the world exalts, none surpasses the Yellow Emperor, yet even he could not preserve his virtue, fighting at Zhuolu until blood flowed for a hundred *li*. Yao was unfilial, Shun was undutiful, Yu was crippled, Tang exiled his lord, Wu killed Zhou, and King Wen was imprisoned at Youli. These six, whom the world reveres—when examined closely, all were deluded by profit, violently distorting their true nature. How shameful!

The world calls Boyi and Shuqi worthy men. They renounced the lordship of Guzhu and starved to death on Mount Shouyang, their bones left unburied. Bao Jiao adorned his conduct to condemn the world and died embracing a tree. Shentu Di remonstrated in vain, carried a stone into the river, and was eaten by fish and turtles. Jie Zitui was utterly loyal, cutting flesh from his thigh to feed Duke Wen, yet later the duke betrayed him. Enraged, Zitui left and burned to death in a tree. Weisheng arranged to meet a woman beneath a bridge; when she did not come and the waters rose, he held fast to the pillar and drowned. These six men—no different from dismembered dogs, drowned pigs, or beggars with gourd ladles—all pursued fame and scorned death, heedless of preserving life. Are they not fools?

The world calls Prince Bigan and Wu Zixu loyal ministers. Wu Zixu was drowned in the river, Bigan had his heart cut out—these two are called loyal ministers, yet in the end, they were mocked by all under heaven. From the highest perspective, even they are not worthy of esteem.

As for what you tell me: if you speak of ghostly matters, I cannot know them; if you speak of human affairs, it is nothing beyond what I have already heard. Let me tell you of human nature: the eyes desire to see beauty, the ears to hear sound, the mouth to taste flavors, and the will to be fulfilled. The longest-lived reach a hundred years, the average eighty, the shortest sixty. Subtract illness, emaciation, death, mourning, and sorrow, and in a month, one may open one’s mouth to laugh only four or five days at most.

Heaven and earth are without end, but human life has its time. Clutching the tools of existence and entrusting oneself to the boundless, one passes in a flash, no different from a fine steed galloping past a crack in the wall. Those who cannot fulfill their aspirations and nurture their lives are not masters of the Dao.

All your words are what I reject. Begone at once! Do not speak again! Your Way is frantic and grasping, a craft of emptiness and deceit—it cannot preserve the true. What is there to discuss?"

Confucius bowed twice, hurried away, exited the gate, mounted his carriage, and three times lost hold of the reins. His eyes were dazed and sightless, his complexion ashen, and he leaned against the crossbar, unable to catch his breath. Returning to the east gate of Lu, he encountered Liuxia Ji.

Liuxia Ji said: "You have been absent for days. Your carriage and horses show signs of travel—did you by chance go to see Zhi?"

Confucius looked up to the sky and sighed: "Indeed."

Liuxia Ji said: "Did Zhi perhaps offend you as before?"

Confucius said: "Indeed. What I call needlessly cauterizing oneself without illness. Racing to measure the tiger’s head, braiding its whiskers—how nearly did I escape its jaws!"

Zizhang asked Mang Goude: "Why not act? Without action, there is no trust; without trust, no appointment; without appointment, no— Thus, when one observes reputation and calculates profit, righteousness is truly at stake. If one abandons fame and gain, returning to the heart, then the scholar’s conduct cannot but be daily practiced!"

Mang Goude said: "The shameless grow rich; the deceitful gain prominence. The greatest of fame and profit lie in shamelessness and deceit. Thus, when one observes reputation and calculates profit, trust is truly at stake. If one abandons fame and gain, returning to the heart, then the scholar’s conduct embraces their Heavenly nature!"

Confucius said: "In ancient times, Jie and Zhou were honored as Sons of Heaven, wealthy with all under heaven. Now, if one were to say to Zang Ju, ‘Your conduct is like Jie’s or Zhou’s,’ he would flush with shame and harbor resentment—such is the base man’s contempt. Yet when Confucius and Mo Di, impoverished commoners, are held up to a prime minister, saying, ‘Your conduct is like Confucius’ or Mo Di’s,’ he would alter his expression and protest his inadequacy—such is the scholar’s true nobility. Thus, to hold the power of the Son of Heaven does not necessarily confer nobility; to be a destitute commoner does not necessarily mean baseness. The distinction between noble and base lies in the beauty or ugliness of one’s conduct."

Mang Goude said: "Petty thieves are imprisoned, while great thieves become lords; at the gates of lords, benevolence and righteousness reside. Formerly, Duke Huan of Qi, Xiaobai, killed his brother and took his sister-in-law, yet Guan Zhong served as his minister. Tian Chengzi murdered his lord and usurped the state, yet Confucius accepted his gifts."

"To condemn in speech yet subordinate in action—this is to have the true nature of words and deeds warring within one’s breast. Is this not perverse? Thus the *Book* says: ‘Who is vile? Who is noble? The successful become the head, the unsuccessful the tail.’"

Zizhang said: "If you do not act accordingly, kinship and affection will lack order, noble and base will lack righteousness, elder and younger will lack sequence. How then can the Five Constants and Six Positions be distinguished?"

Mang Goude replied: "Yao executed his eldest son, Shun exiled his younger brother—was there order in kinship and affection? Tang banished Jie, King Wu killed Zhou—was there righteousness in noble and base? Wang Ji was the rightful heir, yet the Duke of Zhou killed his elder brother—was there sequence in elder and younger? The Confucians’ hypocritical words, Mozi’s universal love—how do they distinguish the Five Constants and Six Positions? You correct for the sake of fame, I correct for the sake of profit. The reality of fame and profit neither accords with principle nor reflects the Dao.

Daily you dispute with Wu Yue, saying: ‘The petty man dies for wealth, the gentleman dies for fame—their means of altering their essence and changing their nature differ, yet in abandoning what they do to die for what they do not, they are the same.’ Thus it is said: Do not act as the petty man, and return to Heaven. Do not act as the gentleman, and follow Heaven’s principle. Whether crooked or straight, observe and reach the celestial extreme. Face the four directions, ebb and flow with the times. Whether thus or so, grasp the revolving mechanism. Form your intent alone, and wander with the Dao. Do not act with contrivance, do not establish righteousness through achievement—you will lose what you seek. Do not rush toward wealth, do not sacrifice for success—you will abandon Heaven.

Bi Gan had his heart carved out, Wu Zixu had his eyes gouged out—such were the calamities of loyalty. Zhigong testified against his father, Weisheng drowned holding to his word—such were the disasters of trust. Zili was executed, Shenzi did not defend himself—such were the harms of integrity. Confucius did not see his mother, Kuangzi did not see his father—such were the failures of righteousness. These are what the ancients transmitted and later ages recount, teaching that the scholar must align his words with his deeds, or else bear the misfortune and suffer the calamity."

Wu Zu asked Zhihe: "There has never been a man who did not pursue fame and seek profit. The wealthy attract followers; followers subordinate themselves, thus honoring them. To see those who subordinate and honor them is the Way to longevity, bodily ease, and joyful contentment. Now you alone have no such intent—is your knowledge insufficient? Or do you know but lack the strength to act? Or do you reject correctness as delusion?"

Zhihe replied: "Nowadays, those who consider themselves contemporaries and fellow villagers regard those who transcend custom and surpass the world as men without proper standards. They alone lack a guiding principle, yet claim to survey the ages and distinguish right from wrong, conforming to the俗 while abandoning what is most weighty and discarding what is most honorable, all for the sake of their own conduct. How distant they are from the Way of longevity, bodily ease, and joyful contentment!

The pain of illness, the peace of serenity—these are not reflected in the body. The fear of alarm, the joy of delight—these are not reflected in the heart. Knowing what to do yet not knowing why—this is why one may be honored as the Son of Heaven, possess all under Heaven, yet still not escape misfortune."

Wu Zu said: "Wealth is universally advantageous. It exhausts beauty and exhausts power, surpassing what even the perfected cannot attain, the worthy cannot reach. It harnesses others’ courage and strength to make itself mighty, wields others’ wisdom and schemes to make itself discerning, and adopts others’ virtue to make itself excellent. Without ruling a state, it commands reverence like a lord or father.

Moreover, the allure of sounds, colors, flavors, and power—these delight the heart without need of learning, comfort the body without need of imitation. Desires and aversions, approach and avoidance—these do not wait for a teacher, for they are human nature. Though all under Heaven may condemn me, who can renounce them?"

Zhihe replied: "The actions of the wise move with the people, not transgressing their measure. Thus, having enough, they do not contend; having no purpose, they do not seek. Lacking enough, they contend in all directions yet do not consider themselves greedy. Having a purpose, they decline it, abandoning all under Heaven yet do not consider themselves incorrupt. The reality of incorruptibility and greed is not imposed from without—it is a reflection of one’s inner measure.

Though one may hold the power of the Son of Heaven, one does not use nobility to lord over others. Though one may possess all under Heaven, one does not use wealth to toy with others. Calculating the harm, considering the consequences, one deems it injurious to one’s nature and thus does not accept it—not to seek fame and reputation.

Yao and Shun ruled as emperors and were revered, bringing benevolence to all under Heaven, yet did not let beauty harm their lives. Shanjuan and Xu You were offered the throne yet declined—not out of empty words of refusal, but to avoid letting affairs harm themselves. Pursuing benefit and declining harm, all under Heaven calls them worthy—yet they did not do so to seek fame and reputation."

Wu Zu said: "To insist on fame, to torment the body and reject sweetness, to restrain nourishment to preserve life—is this not to suffer long illness and endless hardship without dying?"

Zhihe replied: "Equilibrium is fortune; excess is harm—nothing is exempt, and wealth is the most extreme case. Now, the rich man’s ears are filled with the sounds of bells, drums, pipes, and flutes; his mouth savors the taste of fine meats and sweet wines, stirring his mind and causing him to neglect his affairs—this is chaos. Indulging in arrogance, he is like one carrying a heavy burden uphill—this is suffering. Coveting wealth, he takes comfort in it; coveting power, he exhausts himself. At rest, he is drowned in dissipation; in movement, he is swollen with arrogance—this is illness. Pursuing desire and seeking profit, he is stuffed like a blocked ear, unaware of danger, pressing forward without cease—this is disgrace. Wealth accumulates yet goes unused; he clings to it without release, his heart filled with sorrow and resentment, endlessly seeking more—this is anxiety. Within, he fears the threats of extortion and demands; without, he dreads the harm of bandits and thieves. His towers are high, his gates barred—he dares not walk alone outside—this is fear."

These six are the greatest harms under heaven, yet all are abandoned and left unexamined. When their calamities arrive, one exhausts one’s nature and depletes one’s wealth, yet even a single day of carefree ease cannot be regained.

Thus, when one gazes upon their names, they are unseen; when one seeks them, they are unattainable. To strain the mind and break the body in contention for these—is this not delusion?

---

**Chapter 30: Discourse on Swords (Volume 10, Part 1)**

In former times, King Wen of Zhao was fond of swords. Over three thousand swordsmen thronged his gates as retainers, clashing day and night before him. Each year, a hundred perished or were wounded, yet his passion knew no satiation. After three years of this, the state declined, and the feudal lords plotted against him.

Crown Prince Kui grew anxious and offered a reward to his attendants: “Whoever can persuade the king to cease his obsession with swordsmen shall receive a thousand gold pieces.” His attendants replied, “Zhuangzi is the one for this.” The prince then sent envoys bearing a thousand gold to present to Zhuangzi.

Zhuangzi declined the gift but accompanied the envoys to see the prince. He asked, “What instruction does the prince have for me, that he bestows a thousand gold?” The prince said, “I have heard that you, Master, are enlightened and sagely. I humbly offer this gold to honor your attendants. If you refuse it, how dare I speak further?”

Zhuangzi replied, “I have heard that the prince wishes to employ me to end the king’s fondness for swords. If I persuade the king but oppose his will, or fail to please the prince, I would face execution. What use would gold be to me then? But if I succeed in persuading the king and satisfy the prince, what in Zhao could I not obtain?”

The prince said, “Indeed. My father the king sees only swordsmen.” Zhuangzi said, “Very well. I am skilled in the sword.” The prince said, “Yet the swordsmen the king favors all have unkempt hair, bristling sideburns, slouching caps, tassels of coarse silk, short-backed robes, glaring eyes, and harsh speech. The king delights in them. If you, Master, appear before him in Confucian garb, the matter will surely fail.”

Zhuangzi said, “Permit me to prepare a swordsman’s attire.” After three days, he had his sword attire ready and was presented to the prince. The prince then brought him before the king, who stood with bared blade awaiting him.

Zhuangzi entered the palace gates without haste and did not bow to the king. The king asked, “What instruction do you bring, that the prince precedes you?” Zhuangzi replied, “I have heard of your fondness for swords, so I have come to present myself as a swordsman.”

The king asked, “What can your sword do to restrain others?” Zhuangzi said, “My sword cuts down one man every ten paces, leaving no trace in its path.” The king was greatly pleased and exclaimed, “Then you are invincible under heaven!”

Zhuangzi said, “The way of the sword is to present emptiness, open the advantage, strike after the opponent moves, and arrive before him. I wish to demonstrate this.” The king said, “Rest now, Master. Return to your lodging and await my summons. I shall arrange a contest and invite you.”

The king then assembled swordsmen for seven days. Over sixty were killed or wounded, and five or six were selected. These were ordered to present their swords at the palace steps, whereupon the king summoned Zhuangzi.

The king said, “Today, let the swordsmen test their skill in earnest.” Zhuangzi replied, “I have long awaited this.” The king asked, “What length of weapon do you prefer, Master?” Zhuangzi said, “Any I am given will suffice. Yet I possess three swords, to be wielded as the king wishes. Permit me to describe them before demonstrating.”

The king said, “I wish to hear of them.” Zhuangzi said, “There is the sword of the Son of Heaven, the sword of the feudal lords, and the sword of the commoner.”

The king asked, “What is the sword of the Son of Heaven like?” Zhuangzi replied, “The sword of the Son of Heaven has its blade forged from Yanxi and Shicheng, its edge from Mount Tai of Qi, its spine from Jin and Wei, its guard from Zhou and Song, and its grip from Liang. It is bound by the Four Barbarians, wrapped in the Four Seasons, encircled by the Bohai Sea, and girded by Mount Chang.”

This sword is governed by the Five Phases, judged by punishment and virtue; opened by yin and yang, held by spring and summer, and wielded by autumn and winter. When this sword is drawn, none stand before it; when raised, none surpass it; when pressed downward, none lie beneath it; when swung, none stand beside it. Above, it parts the floating clouds; below, it severs the earth’s foundations. Once this sword is employed, the feudal lords are brought to order, and all under heaven submit. This is the sword of the Son of Heaven.”

King Wen, overwhelmed, lost himself in thought and said, “What is the sword of the feudal lords like?”

Zhuangzi replied, “The sword of the feudal lords has its blade forged from the wise and brave, its edge from the pure and resolute, its spine from the worthy and good, its guard from the loyal and sagely, and its grip from the heroic and outstanding. This sword, when drawn, likewise has none before it; when raised, none above it; when pressed downward, none beneath it; when swung, none beside it. Above, it models the round heavens, following the Three Luminaries; below, it models the square earth, following the Four Seasons. Within, it harmonizes the people’s will, bringing peace to the four directions. Once this sword is employed, it strikes like thunder, and within the four borders, none fail to submit and heed its command. This is the sword of the feudal lords.”

The king asked, “What is the sword of the commoner like?”

Zhuangzi said, “The sword of the commoner is wielded by those with unkempt hair and bristling sideburns, wearing drooping caps with loose, tangled cords, short-backed robes, glaring fiercely as they argue, clashing before one another. Above, they sever necks and throats; below, they pierce livers. This sword of the commoner differs not from fighting cocks—once their lives are spent, they serve no purpose in the affairs of state. Now, Your Majesty, possessing the position of the Son of Heaven, yet delighting in the sword of the commoner—I privately deem this unworthy of you.”

The king then led Zhuangzi by the hand and ascended the hall. The royal chef presented the meal, and the king circled it thrice. Zhuangzi said, “May Your Majesty sit composed and calm your qi. The matter of the swords has been presented in full!”

Thereupon, King Wen did not leave the palace for three months, and the swordsmen all died by their own hands where they stood.

---

Confucius wandered in the forest of black curtains and rested upon the Apricot Terrace. His disciples read aloud, while Confucius plucked the zither and sang. The melody was not yet half-finished when an old fisherman came ashore, his beard and brows snowy white, hair unbound, sleeves swaying as he walked up from the marsh. He stopped at the edge of the land, left hand resting on his knee, right hand supporting his chin, listening. When the song ended, he beckoned to Zilu and Zigong, who both stepped forward.

The stranger pointed at Confucius and asked, “Who is that man?”

Zilu replied, “A gentleman of Lu.”

The stranger asked of his lineage, and Zilu answered, “The Kong clan.”

“And what does the Kong clan govern?”

Zilu did not respond, but Zigong said, “The Kong clan cultivates loyalty and trust in their nature, practices benevolence and righteousness in their conduct, refines ritual and music, and orders human relationships. Above, they are loyal to the rulers of the age; below, they transform the common people, all to benefit the world. This is what the Kong clan governs.”

The stranger asked again, “Is he a lord with lands?”

Zigong said, “No.”

“Is he an advisor to kings and marquises?”

Zigong said, “No.”

The old man laughed and turned away, saying as he walked, “Benevolent, indeed, he is—but I fear he will not escape harm to himself. With toil of mind and strain of body, he endangers his true nature. Alas!”

“How far removed he is from the Dao!”

Zilu returned and reported to Confucius. Confucius pushed aside his zither and rose, saying, “Could he be a sage?” He then descended to seek him. Reaching the riverbank, he saw the old man leaning on his staff and drawing his boat. The old man turned, saw Confucius, and stood facing him.

Confucius retreated, bowed twice, and stepped forward. The old man asked, “What do you seek?”

Confucius replied, “Earlier, Master left with words unfinished. I, Qiu, unworthy as I am, did not grasp their meaning. I waited humbly in your wake, hoping to hear even the sound of your cough or the fall of your spittle, that I might at last receive your guidance.”

The old man exclaimed, “Ah! How earnest is your love of learning!”

Confucius bowed twice and rose, saying, “I, Qiu, have cultivated learning to this day, now sixty-nine years old, yet have never heard such profound instruction. How dare I not remain open in heart?”

The old man said, “Like attracts like, and sound answers sound—this is the principle of Heaven. I shall now set forth what I possess and expound upon what concerns you. What concerns you is human affairs. When the Son of Heaven, feudal lords, ministers, and commoners—these four ranks—each rectify themselves, governance attains its beauty. But when these four abandon their stations, no disorder is greater.

When officials fulfill their duties, and each person attends to their affairs, none overstep their bounds. Thus, when fields lie fallow and homes stand exposed, when clothing and food are insufficient, when taxes and levies are unmet, when wives and concubines are discordant, when elders and juniors lack order—these are the concerns of the common people.

When one’s abilities fall short of their office, when official duties go unmanaged, when conduct is not pure and bright, when subordinates grow idle and neglectful, when meritorious deeds go unrecognized, when ranks and emoluments are not secured—these are the worries of ministers.

When the court lacks loyal ministers, when the state is benighted and disordered, when skills are unrefined, when tribute and duties are unfulfilled, when spring and autumn audiences fall behind schedule, when one does not submit to the Son of Heaven—these are the concerns of feudal lords.

When yin and yang are not in harmony, when cold and heat arrive untimely, harming the myriad things; when feudal lords act with violence and disorder, seizing and attacking one another, ravaging the people; when ritual and music lack moderation, when resources are exhausted, when human relationships are not ordered, when clans engage in licentiousness and disorder—these are the worries of the Son of Heaven’s officials.

Now, you possess neither the authority of a lord or official above, nor the office of a minister with its duties below, yet you presume to adorn ritual and music, select and order human relationships, and transform the common people. Is this not overreaching in affairs?

Moreover, people have eight flaws, and affairs have four perils—these cannot be overlooked.

To meddle in affairs not one’s own is called *zong* (presumption).
To advance without regard is called *ning* (obsequiousness).
To speak ingratiatingly to curry favor is called *chan* (flattery).
To speak without discerning right or wrong is called *yu* (sycophancy).
To delight in speaking of others’ faults is called *chan* (slander).
To sever friendships and estrange kin is called *zei* (treachery).
To praise the wicked and deceive the good is called *te* (depravity).
To lack discernment of good and bad, to accommodate both sides, and to seize what one desires is called *xian* (perfidy).

These eight flaws externally confuse others and internally harm oneself. The noble person does not befriend such, and the enlightened ruler does not employ them.

The four perils are these:
To delight in managing great affairs, altering and changing constants, and clinging to fame and merit is called *tao* (overreaching).
To monopolize knowledge, arrogate affairs, encroach upon others, and act self-righteously is called *tan* (greed).
To see one’s faults yet not change, and to hear remonstrance yet grow more obstinate, is called *hen* (obduracy).
To approve only what aligns with oneself and to reject what differs, even if good, is called *jin* (arrogance).

These are the four perils. Only when one has shed the eight flaws and is free of the four perils can one begin to be taught.”

Confucius sighed deeply, bowed twice, and rose, saying, “I, Qiu, was twice driven from Lu, had my traces erased in Wei, was felled like a tree in Song, and was besieged between Chen and Cai. I do not know what faults I committed to incur these four slanders.”

The old man’s expression turned sorrowful as he said, “How difficult it is for you to awaken! When a man flees from his shadow and shuns his footprints, the more he lifts his feet, the more footprints he leaves; the faster he runs, the more his shadow clings. Believing himself still too slow, he exhausts his strength and dies. Yet he does not know that by resting in the shade, his shadow will vanish, and by stillness, his footprints will cease. How foolish!
