You scrutinize the space between benevolence and righteousness, examine the boundaries of sameness and difference, observe the changes of movement and stillness, measure the degrees of giving and receiving, order the passions of likes and dislikes, and harmonize the moderation of joy and anger—yet you nearly escape none of this. Be diligent in cultivation, guard your true nature carefully, and return things to others, and you will have no encumbrances. Now, you do not cultivate yourself but seek from others—is this not misplaced?”

Confucius sighed deeply and said, “May I ask what is meant by *zhen* (the real)?”

The old man replied, “*Zhen* is the utmost of essence and sincerity. Without essence and sincerity, one cannot move others. Forced weeping, though sorrowful, is not mournful; forced anger, though stern, is not awe-inspiring; forced affection, though smiling, is not harmonious. True sorrow is silent yet mournful; true anger is unexpressed yet awe-inspiring; true affection is unsmiling yet harmonious. When the real resides within, the spirit moves without—this is why the real is prized.

In its application to human principles:
With parents, it is kindness and filial piety;
with rulers, it is loyalty and integrity;
in drinking, it is joy;
in mourning, it is sorrow.

Loyalty and integrity take merit as their essence;
drinking takes joy as its essence;
mourning takes sorrow as its essence;
serving parents takes comfort as its essence.

When merit is achieved, no trace of it remains;
when serving parents brings comfort, no means are sought;
when drinking brings joy, no particular vessels are chosen;
when mourning brings sorrow, no inquiry into ritual is made.

Ritual is what the common customs devise;
the real is what one receives from Heaven, naturally and unalterably.

Thus, the sage models Heaven and prizes the real, not constrained by custom. The foolish do this not. Unable to model Heaven and anxious about others, they do not know the value of the real. They drift along, swayed by custom, and thus are unworthy. Alas! You have long been immersed in human artifice and only now hear of the great Dao!”

Confucius bowed twice and rose, saying, “Today I, Qiu, have met you as if by Heaven’s favor.”

The master did not disdain to liken himself to a servant and personally instruct him. I venture to ask where you dwell, that I may follow and receive your teaching to complete my study of the great Dao.”

The master said, “I have heard that one may accompany those who are fit to journey with, but as for the subtle Dao, those unfit to journey with cannot comprehend it. Be cautious not to share it with them, lest you bring harm upon yourself. Strive on! I leave you now, I leave you now!” With that, he poled his boat away, gliding along the reeds.

Yan Yuan turned the chariot around, while Zilu handed the reins. Confucius did not look back, waiting until the water’s ripples stilled and the sound of the oar vanished before daring to board. Zilu stepped beside the chariot and asked, “I have long served you, yet never have I seen you meet another with such reverence. Even the lords of ten thousand chariots or the rulers of a thousand, when they meet you, do not fail to observe proper courtesy, yet you still maintain a dignified bearing. Now this old fisherman, leaning on his oar and standing upright, and you bowed at the waist like a chime stone, responding with words and bows—was this not excessive? Your disciples all find it strange. How could this fisherman deserve such treatment?”

Confucius leaned on the crossbar and sighed, saying, “How deeply entrenched your difficulty in transformation is! Though you have been steeped in ritual and propriety for some time, your rustic and coarse heart remains unchanged to this day. Come closer, let me tell you: to fail to show respect when meeting an elder is impropriety; to fail to honor the worthy is a lack of benevolence. If he were not a perfected person, he could not humble himself before others. Without perfecting humility, one cannot attain the real, and thus one’s person suffers lasting harm. Alas! There is no greater misfortune for a person than a lack of benevolence, and you alone cling to it. Moreover, the Dao is the path by which all things proceed. Those who lose it die; those who attain it live. Acting against it brings failure; following it brings success. Therefore, wherever the Dao resides, the sage honors it. This old fisherman may be said to possess it—how could I dare not show respect?”

---

When Liezi of Lie Yukou journeyed to Qi, he turned back midway and encountered Bohun Maoren. Bohun Maoren asked, “Why have you returned?”

Liezi said, “I was alarmed.”

“By what?”

“I dined at ten inns, and at five of them, the broth was served before the rice.”

Bohun Maoren said, “If that is so, why were you alarmed?”

Liezi replied, “Though my inner sincerity was not yet dissolved, my outward demeanor shone with self-importance, thereby unsettling others’ hearts and causing them to treat the honored and the aged lightly, stirring up the very troubles they seek to avoid. Now, the broth-sellers merely trade in food and drink, with no great profit to spare. Their influence is slight, their power light, yet even so, they acted thus—how much more so the lords of ten thousand chariots? Their bodies are wearied by the state, their knowledge exhausted by affairs. They would entrust me with tasks and demand results from me. That is why I was alarmed.”

Bohun Maoren said, “Excellent observation!”

"Now abide thus, and others will come to preserve you!"

Before long, Liezi returned to find the space outside the door filled with shoes. Bohun Maoren stood facing north, his staff planted firmly beneath his chin. After a time, he spoke not a word and departed. The attendant informed Liezi, who rushed out barefoot, shoes in hand, and caught up at the gate:

"Master, since you have come, will you not dispense your remedy?"

Bohun Maoren replied, "It is done. I had already told you: others will come to preserve you—and indeed, they have preserved you! You may make others preserve you, yet you cannot make them cease preserving you—why then do you seek to stir their admiration with such extraordinary displays? If you must provoke a response, let it arise from your root nature, not from empty gestures. Those who wander with you offer no true counsel. Their petty words are all poison to men. None awaken, none perceive—how can they discern one another? The cunning toil, the wise fret, the capable seek nothing. Sated, they roam at ease, drifting like untethered boats—empty, they wander freely!"

Zheng’s Huan sighed and groaned upon the lands of Qiu.

"Within three years, Huan of Zheng became a Confucian. As a river moistens nine *li* and its blessings extend to three clans, his younger brother became a Mohist. The Confucians and Mohists debated, and their father sided with Mo Di. After ten years, Huan took his own life. In a dream, his father addressed him: 'It was I who made your son a Mohist—why did you never examine his true nature? Had he already become the fruit of the autumn cypress?'

The Creator’s retribution upon men does not punish the man himself but the Heaven within him—thus it was for Huan. When a man deems himself possessed of something that sets him apart from others, thereby dishonoring his kin, even those who draw water from the same well will come to blows. Thus it is said: all in this age are Huan. From this, those with virtue do so without awareness—how much more so those who embody the Dao!

In ancient times, this was called 'the punishment of fleeing from Heaven.' The sage finds peace in what is inherently peaceful and does not seek peace in what is inherently restless; the multitude seeks peace in what is inherently restless and finds no peace in what is inherently peaceful."

Zhuangzi said: "To know the Dao is easy; to refrain from speaking of it is difficult. To know and not speak—this is the way to Heaven. To know and speak—this is the way of man. The ancients followed Heaven and not man."

Zhu Pingman studied dragon-slaying under Zhi Liyi, exhausting a thousand gold from his family. After three years, his skill was perfected—yet there was no use for his craft.

The sage takes what must not be as what must not be, and so has no weapons. The multitude takes what must not be as what must be, and so has many weapons. Yielding to weapons, their actions become demands. Where weapons go, ruin follows.

The petty man’s knowledge clings to bribes and trivial documents, wasting his spirit on the shallow and narrow, yet seeks to encompass the Dao and all things, to embody the Great Unity and the formless. Such a one is lost amid the cosmos, his form burdened, unaware of the Great Beginning.

The perfected person returns his spirit to the beginningless, merging with the realm of Nothing Whatever. Like water flowing without form, it disperses into the Great Purity.

Alas! You take your knowledge to be as fine as a hair’s tip, yet do not know the Great Peace!

There was a man of Song named Cao Shang, sent by the King of Song as an envoy to Qin. On his journey, he obtained several carriages. The King of Qin was pleased with him and added a hundred more carriages. Returning to Song, he saw Zhuangzi and said: “To dwell in a cramped alley, impoverished and weaving sandals, with a withered neck and sallow face—this is what I was poor at. But to awaken a ruler of ten thousand chariots and follow with a hundred carriages—this is what I excel at.”

Zhuangzi replied: “When the King of Qin was ill, he summoned physicians. Those who lanced boils and drained abscesses received one carriage; those who licked his hemorrhoids received five carriages. The baser the treatment, the more carriages they received. Did you, perhaps, treat his ailments? Why else would you have received so many carriages?”

"Be gone!" Duke Ai of Lu asked Yan He, saying: "I take Zhongni as the pillar of the state—will the nation then recover?" Yan He replied: "Kong Qiu is perilous indeed! He adorns himself with feathers and paints, engages in florid speech, takes the branch for the root, and suppresses his nature to gaze upon the people. He knows not what is untrustworthy, yet receives it in his heart as steward. How could such a one be fit to govern the people? Is he fit for you? Or fit for me? To err thus is permissible! Now, to cause the people to depart from true learning and embrace falsity—this is no way to govern them. To consider posterity thus is unwise. Cease this course, for governance will be difficult!"

What is bestowed upon others yet not forgotten is not the Heavenly distribution. Merchants are held in contempt; even when ranked among them by office, the spiritually attuned hold them in contempt. External punishments are meted out by metal and wood; internal punishments arise from movement and transgression. When petty men incur external punishments, metal and wood interrogate them; when they incur internal punishments, yin and yang consume them. Only the truly capable escape both inner and outer punishments.

Confucius said: "The human heart is more treacherous than mountains and rivers, harder to know than Heaven. Heaven at least has its seasons—spring, autumn, winter, summer—and its fixed times of dawn and dusk. But man conceals his nature beneath a thick façade. Thus, some appear sincere yet are insatiable; some seem long-suffering yet are unworthy; some are compliant yet perverse; some are resolute yet lax; some are gentle yet fierce. Those who rush to righteousness do so as if parched; those who flee it do so as if scorched. Therefore, the noble man sends them far to observe their loyalty, keeps them near to observe their reverence, burdens them to observe their ability, questions them suddenly to observe their knowledge, sets urgent deadlines to observe their trustworthiness, entrusts them with wealth to observe their benevolence, warns them of danger to observe their integrity, intoxicates them with wine to observe their composure, and mingles them with others to observe their demeanor. When these nine tests are complete, the unworthy are revealed."

Zheng Kaofu, upon his first appointment, bent low; upon his second, stooped; upon his third, bowed deeply, creeping along the wall—who would dare transgress! Yet there are those who, upon their first appointment, strut like Lü Ju; upon their second, dance atop their carriage; upon their third, address elders by name. Who could align with Tang or Xu? No calamity is greater than virtue with intent, and intent with eyelashes. Once intent has eyelashes, it turns inward; turning inward, it fails.

There are five inauspicious virtues, with "central virtue" foremost. What is central virtue? It is one who, while esteeming himself, reviles what he does not possess. There are eight extremes of poverty and three certainties of success; the body has six repositories. Beauty, whiskers, height, stature, strength, comeliness, courage, and boldness—those who surpass others in all eight rely on these. Following customs, reclining, or cowering in fear when inferior to others—these three lead to success. Wisdom and intelligence that reach outward breed resentment; bold action invites many grudges; benevolence and righteousness bring many demands. Those who penetrate to the truth of success are puppets; those who penetrate to knowledge are diminished. Those who grasp the great mandate follow; those who grasp the small mandate encounter it.

A man who had an audience with the King of Song was granted ten carriages. With these ten carriages, he arrogantly boasted to Zhuangzi. Zhuangzi said: "By the river there was a poor family who wove rushes to eat. Their son dived into the depths and obtained a pearl worth a thousand gold pieces. His father said to him: 'Bring a stone to pound it! A pearl of a thousand gold pieces must lie in the ninefold abyss beneath the chin of a black dragon. That you could obtain it means you must have encountered its sleep. Had the black dragon awakened, what trace of you would remain?' Now, the depths of the state of Song are not merely a ninefold abyss; the ferocity of the King of Song is not merely that of a black dragon. That you could obtain these carriages means you must have encountered his sleep. Had the King of Song awakened, you would be reduced to mincemeat."

Someone sought to engage Zhuangzi’s services, and Zhuangzi replied to the envoy: "Have you seen the sacrificial ox? Clothed in embroidered brocade, fed on fine hay and grain—yet when it is led into the great temple, would it still wish to be a lonely calf?"

As Zhuangzi neared death, his disciples wished to give him a lavish burial. Zhuangzi said: "I take heaven and earth as my inner and outer coffin, the sun and moon as my paired jades, the stars as my pearls and beads, and the ten thousand things as my funeral gifts. Are not my burial furnishings complete? What could be added to them?" His disciples said: "We fear the crows and kites will devour you." Zhuangzi replied: "Above, I would be eaten by crows and kites; below, by ants and mole crickets. To snatch from one to give to the other—how partial is that!"

To level with what is not level is to make leveling unlevel; to verify with what is not verifiable is to make verification unverifiable. The enlightened are but servants of it; the spiritually attuned are its witnesses. That the enlightened cannot surpass the spiritually attuned has long been so—yet fools rely on what they see to impose upon others. Their achievements are external; is this not lamentable?

**Chapter Thirty-Three: "The World"**

Those in the world who cultivate methods of governance are many, each believing their own way cannot be surpassed. What, then, was the Dao/Way of the ancients? It was said: "Nowhere is it not present." It was asked: "How does the spirit descend? How does clarity emerge?" It was answered: "The sage has a source of arising; the king has a source of accomplishment—both are one."

Not departing from the origin is called the Heavenly Person; not departing from the essence is called the Spirit Person; not departing from the real is called the Perfected Person. Taking heaven as the origin, virtue/power as the root, and the Dao/Way as the gate, foretelling change and transformation—this is called the sage. Taking benevolence as kindness, righteousness as principle, ritual as conduct, and music as harmony, exuding gentle compassion—this is called the gentleman. Taking law as distinction, names as markers, evidence as proof, and investigation as decision, counting one, two, three, four as their measure, officials rank one another by this, taking affairs as the norm, clothing and food as the priority, multiplying and storing, caring for the old, weak, orphaned, and widowed—this is the governance of the people.

The ancients were complete! They matched the spirit luminaries, harmonized with heaven and earth, nurtured the myriad things, brought harmony to the world, and extended their blessings to the hundred clans. Illuminating the root and the numbers, connecting to the branches and the measures, penetrating the six directions and opening the four gates, whether small or great, refined or coarse, their movement was nowhere absent.

What is clear and lies in numbers and measures—old laws and transmitted histories still preserve much of it. What lies in the *Book of Songs*, *Book of Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music*—scholars of Zou and Lu, and the gentry, can often clarify it. The *Songs* convey the will; the *Documents* convey affairs; the *Rites* convey conduct; the *Music* conveys harmony; the *Changes* convey yin and yang; the *Spring and Autumn Annals* convey names and duties. These methods spread throughout the world and within the central states, and the hundred schools of thought occasionally praise and expound them.

When the world was in great disorder, the worthy and sagely were obscured, and the Dao/Way and virtue/power were not unified. People in the world each grasped one insight and took pride in it. Like the ears and eyes, each has its clarity but cannot communicate with the other. Like the hundred schools and their arts, each has its strengths and occasional uses. Yet they are not comprehensive or all-encompassing—they are partial scholars.

Judging the beauty of heaven and earth, analyzing the principles of the ten thousand things, examining the wholeness of the ancients—few can fully grasp the beauty of heaven and earth or match the bearing of the spirit. Thus, the Dao/Way of inner sageliness and outer kingship has grown dim and unmanifest, pent-up and unexpressed. People in the world each follow their desires to create their own methods. How lamentable! The hundred schools have gone forth and not returned—they will surely not converge.

Later scholars, unfortunately, do not see the purity of heaven and earth or the great form of the ancients. The Dao/Way is about to be torn apart by the world. Not extravagant toward later generations, not wasteful toward the ten thousand things, not dazzled by numbers and measures, using the inked cord to correct themselves and prepare for the world’s emergencies—there are those in whom the Dao/Way resides.

Mo Di and Qin Guli heard of this wind and delighted in it. They overdid it greatly, yet considered it the greatest compliance. They composed *Against Music* and called it *Moderation in Use*. They did not sing in life and wore no mourning in death. Mozi advocated universal love and mutual benefit while opposing strife; his Dao/Way did not involve anger. He was also fond of learning and broad in knowledge, yet did not align with the former kings, instead condemning the rites and music of antiquity.

The Yellow Emperor had *Xianchi*; Yao had *Great Chapter*; Shun had *Great Shao*; Yu had *Great Xia*; Tang had *Great Hu*; King Wen had the music of the *Biyong*; King Wu and the Duke of Zhou composed *Wu*. In the mourning rites of antiquity, the noble and base had their proper forms, the high and low their ranks. The Son of Heaven had seven layers of inner and outer coffins; the feudal lords, five; the great officers, three; and the scholars, two. Yet Mozi alone advocated no singing in life and no mourning in death, using a paulownia coffin three inches thick without an outer coffin as the model. Teaching others this, he did not seem to love them; practicing it himself, he certainly did not love himself. Though Mozi’s Dao/Way has not yet failed, singing yet not singing, weeping yet not weeping, joy yet not joy—is this truly fitting? His life was toilsome, his death meager, his Dao/Way harsh. He made people anxious, made people sorrowful—his conduct was difficult to follow. I fear he will not be taken as a sage but will instead turn the world’s heart away. The world cannot endure it. Though Mozi alone could bear it, what can he do about the world? Separated from the world, he is far from the kingly way!

Mozi proclaimed: "In ancient times, Yu controlled the floodwaters, dredged the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers to connect the four barbarians and the nine provinces. The great rivers numbered three hundred, the tributaries three thousand, and the smaller streams were innumerable. Yu personally wielded the spade and basket to dredge the world’s rivers..."

His calves bore no flesh, his shins no hair—soaked by torrential rains, combed by fierce winds, he settled the myriad states. Yu was a great sage, yet his body toiled for the world to such an extent.

Thus later Mohists, many clad in coarse wool and hemp, bound their feet in sandals, laboring day and night without rest, taking self-affliction as the utmost virtue. They declared: "Without this, one does not follow Yu’s Dao/Way and is unworthy to be called a Mohist." The disciples of Xiangli Qin, the followers of Wu Hou, and the southern Mohists such as Huo, Yi Chi, and Deng Lingzi—all recited the *Mo Jing*, yet their interpretations diverged, each calling the others "deviant Mohists." They disparaged one another with debates on "hard and white" and "same and different," responded with arguments of "odd and even" and "non-correspondence," and revered the *juzi* as a sage. All wished to be his embodiment, hoping to succeed him, yet to this day the matter remains unresolved. Mo Di and Qin Guli’s intent was sound, but their conduct was misguided. They would compel later Mohists to advance only by emulating Yu’s self-mortification—calves without flesh, shins without hair. This is the height of disorder, the nadir of governance.

Nevertheless, Mozi was truly the world’s finest. One could seek his like in vain; though withered and gaunt, he never abandoned his cause. What a man of talent! Unburdened by convention, unadorned by externals, uncompromising with others, unhostile to the multitude, he longed for the world’s peace to preserve the people’s lives. His own sustenance sufficed once fulfilled, and with this he purified his heart.

Among the ancient arts of the Dao/Way, some resided in this. Song Xing and Yin Wen, hearing of his spirit, delighted in it. They crafted flower-shaped caps to mark themselves, engaging with all things by first dispelling prejudice. They spoke of the heart’s capacity, calling it "the heart’s conduct," seeking harmony through gentleness and inwardness. They wished to establish this as their guiding principle. Unashamed by insult, they rescued people from strife, forbade aggression, and halted warfare to save the world from conflict. With this, they traversed the world, persuading rulers above and instructing the people below. Though the world did not accept them, they persisted stubbornly, refusing to desist. Thus it is said: they were wearying to both high and low, yet forced themselves upon them.

Yet their conduct was excessive, their self-restraint too meager. They declared: "Five pints of rice suffice. Though the master may not be full, the disciples, though hungry, do not forget the world." Day and night they labored without rest, saying: "We must surely save lives! How proud we are among those who rescue the world!" They held: "The noble person is not overly scrupulous, nor lends himself to things. What benefits not the world is better left unclarified."

They took the prohibition of warfare as their external concern and the moderation of desires as their internal one. In matters great and small, refined and coarse, their conduct extended only so far. Impartial yet not partisan, yielding yet without bias, resolute yet without fixed views, they followed things without duality, unburdened by deliberation, unguided by cunning. Things were not chosen; they moved with them as one. Among the ancient arts of the Dao/Way, some resided in this.

Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao, hearing of this spirit, delighted in it. They took the equalization of the myriad things as their starting point, saying: "Heaven can cover but not bear them; earth can bear but not cover them; the great Dao/Way can encompass but not distinguish them." Knowing all things have what they can and cannot do, they declared: "Selection is not universal; teaching does not reach all; only the Dao/Way leaves nothing out."

Thus Shen Dao abandoned knowledge and self, following only what could not be avoided. He flowed with things, taking this as the principle of the Dao/Way. He said: "To know yet appear not to know is to verge on harming knowledge." Unconstrained and unburdened, he mocked the world’s esteem for worthies; unshackled and unprincipled, he rejected the world’s great sages. Leveling and severing, he turned with things, discarding right and wrong to merely avoid harm. Unbound by knowledge or foresight, without precedence or delay, he stood alone. Pushed, he moved; pulled, he went—like a whirling gale, a spinning feather, a grinding stone’s groove. Whole yet without fault, in motion or still without error, he never incurred blame. Why? Things without knowledge have no peril of self-assertion; their use imposes no burden, their movement and stillness never depart from principle, so they live without praise. Thus it is said: "Attain the state of unknowing things; do not employ worthies or sages. An uncarved block does not lose the Dao/Way."

The heroic mocked them, saying: "Shen Dao’s Dao/Way is not the conduct of the living but the principle of the dead—how strange!" Tian Pian was the same, studying under Peng Meng and receiving his unorthodox teaching. Peng Meng’s master said: "The ancient Daoists reached the point of neither affirming nor denying. Their spirit was vast and indistinct—how could one speak of it?" They constantly opposed others, avoiding observation yet not escaping judgment. What they called the Dao/Way was not the Dao/Way, and their so-called rightness was not free from error. Peng Meng, Tian Pian, and Shen Dao did not understand the Dao/Way. Nevertheless, they roughly grasped something of it.

Taking the root as refined, things as coarse, accumulation as insufficiency, they dwelled in serene solitude with the spiritually luminous. Among the ancient arts of the Dao/Way, some resided in this. Guan Yin and Lao Dan, hearing of this spirit, delighted in it. They built upon the constant of "non-being and being," centered it on the Great One, took gentleness, weakness, and humility as their outward expression, and emptiness that does not destroy the myriad things as their substance.

Guan Yin said: "Abide in nothing, and things will manifest themselves." His movement was like water, his stillness like a mirror, his response like an echo. Vague as if vanished, silent as if pure. Those who harmonized with him were at peace; those who grasped him lost. He never preceded others but always followed.

Lao Dan said: "Know the masculine, yet guard the feminine, and be a ravine to the world. Know the white, yet guard the disgraced, and be a valley to the world."

All men seek to be first, yet he alone takes the last place, saying: "I bear the world’s filth." All men seek fullness, yet he alone remains empty, holding nothing in store—thus he has abundance, towering and inexhaustible. In his conduct, he moves slowly without waste, does nothing yet mocks cleverness. All men seek fortune, yet he alone bends to preserve wholeness, saying: "Let me but escape blame." He takes depth as his root, restraint as his guiding principle, and declares: "What is hard will shatter; what is sharp will dull." Ever tolerant toward things, never carving away at others—this may be called perfection. Ah, Guan Yin and Lao Dan! They were the vast and true men of old!

Vague and formless, changing without constancy—are they dead or alive? Do they stand with heaven and earth, journey with the gods? Dim and obscure, where do they go? Where do they turn? All things are gathered in, yet none can claim them as their home. Such was the Dao/Way of the ancients.

Zhuang Zhou heard of this spirit and delighted in it. With absurd and wandering words, wild and boundless speech, endless and unbounded phrases, he gave free rein to his whims, never showing partiality. He took the world as too sunk in turbidity for solemn discourse, so he used careless words to ramble, weighty words as truth, and parables to broaden meaning. Alone he roamed with the spirit of heaven and earth, never condescending to things, never entangled in right and wrong, living among the common world. Though his writings were strange and wondrous, they flowed without harm. Though his words were uneven, their strangeness was worth beholding. His fullness could not be exhausted: above, he wandered with the Creator; below, he befriended those beyond death and without end. In his grasp of the root, he was vast and open, deep and unconstrained; in his reverence for the source, he was richly attuned and lofty. Yet in responding to change and dissolving things, his principles never ran dry, his coming never ceased. Dim and obscure—who can fathom him?

Hui Shi was a man of many methods. His writings filled five carts, yet his Dao/Way was contradictory and his words often missed the mark. In his "Survey of Things," he said: "What is utterly great has no outside—call it the Great One. What is utterly small has no inside—call it the Small One. What has no thickness cannot be accumulated, yet it spans a thousand miles. Heaven is as low as earth; mountains are level with marshes. The sun is both at its zenith and setting; things are born and die at once. Great sameness differs from small sameness—this is called 'small sameness and difference.' All things are entirely the same and entirely different—this is called 'great sameness and difference.' The south is both boundless and bounded. Today I go to Yue, yet I arrived yesterday. Linked rings can be unlinked. I know the world’s center—it lies north of Yan and south of Yue. Embrace all things, for heaven and earth are one."

Hui Shi took this as his great insight, surveying the world to enlighten debaters. The debaters of the world rejoiced in it. Eggs have feathers; a chicken has three feet; the world can be possessed; a dog can become a sheep; a frog has a tail; fire is not hot; a mountain has a mouth; a wheel does not touch the ground; eyes do not see; a finger does not reach, yet reaching never ceases; a tortoise is longer than a snake; a square is not made with a ruler, nor a circle with a compass; a chisel does not fit its handle; a flying bird’s shadow never moves; an arrow’s flight is both motionless and moving; a white dog is black; a lone colt never had a mother; take half of a one-foot stick each day, and it will never be exhausted after ten thousand generations.

Such were the exchanges with which Hui Shi matched wits, never at a loss. Huan Tuan and Gongsun Long, with their band of debaters, adorned men’s minds and altered their intentions. They could win in argument but not convince the heart—such is the trap of debaters. Hui Shi daily used his knowledge to contend with others, creating monstrosities in debate. This was his foundation. Yet in his verbal sparring, he deemed himself the worthiest, saying: "Is not heaven and earth grand? Yet Hui Shi stands mighty, though without method."

In the south there was an eccentric named Huang Liao who asked why heaven and earth do not collapse or sink, and the causes of wind, rain, and thunder. Hui Shi did not decline to answer, nor did he ponder before responding. He discoursed on all things without pause, yet still felt his words were few and added more strangeness. He took contrariness as truth and sought fame in victory—thus he never fit in with others. Weak in virtue yet strong in things, his path was narrow. Viewed by the Dao/Way of heaven and earth, Hui Shi’s efforts were like the labor of a single mosquito or gnat—what use were they to things? To be content with one thing would have been better; to value the Dao/Way would have been near perfection. Yet Hui Shi could not find peace in this. He scattered himself among the ten thousand things without surfeit, ending as a master of debate.

Alas! Hui Shi’s talent was vast but unfulfilled. Chasing the ten thousand things without return—he was like one who exhausts sound to make an echo, or races with his shadow. How sad!
